THE RED PAGE .
Bismarck’s Love-Betters.
XpURST Bismarck’s Briefe an seine Braut und Gattin,
A Herausgegeben vom Fuersten Herbert Bismarck,
Stuttgart, December, 1900.
FIVE hundred and ninety-eight quarto page 3
of love-letters printed in Gothio type, and
published in time for the Christmas tret;.
But 598 pages ! 1 began to finger this
book with a shy fear of its thickness, dipped into
it, was caught in its spell, and willy-nilly carried
away by the personality, charm and heartiness of
it, and lo ! here I am finishing the last page with
a sigh of distinct hero-worship. And I say to
myself, Ah ! that is a man ! em ganzer mann !
and fall to wondering whether some century hence
another Carlyle will arise to sing his greatness and
tumble and maul us with the billowy prose of a
Sreat enthusiasm, till even the Philistines and
Inglish shall admirehim.
N 6 one in Germany but now recognises B.’s
importance. His statues stand everywhere, in
bronze and marble; statues that seem not so much
in honour of a man, as of frowning Iron Resolu-
tion, and an unflinching intellectual will. One
was beginning to regard him as a semi-mythical
natural force. But these letters show him
entirely human, as the impatient lover and the
tender husband and father. They reveal the
affection and manliness, the rough deep piety, the
playfulness and humour, the sincerity and
courage, yes, and the poetry and fine sense for
Beauty and Nature, that lay hid in the soul of this
man of Blood and Iron,
The letters-range from Dec., 1846, to Sept., ’92.
There is one remarkable gap, for the letters of
’7O-71, written during the French campaign, have
been “lost.” Helas ! quo oui, monsieur ! The
first is to> the Herrn papa of Fraulein von Putt-
kamer, suing for that young lady’s hand. 8.,
who is now about .32, gives in it a summary of
his University and official career, and confesses
the wild blood of his youth; but he has
passed through the stormy sceptic period of his
life, and is going to eschew sack and live cleanly.
He refers to his former fits of dejection at the
thought “that the existence of himself and
humanity were a thing aimless and unprofitable,
and man a mere bauble, a casual and secondary
product of Creation, arising and then vanishing
like the dust beneath the roll of wheels upon the,
road,”—a tale full of sound and fury, signifying
nothing.
Those moods are now past, and “ I can pray
again to God,” he says, “as when I was a child,
and I feel, if not peace, such trustfulness and
courage to confront life as I never remember
before.” This note of piety deepens in his letters
as time goes on, and he afterwards jokes about his
friend von Arnim’s concern at this religiousness
in him. But he is never what one calls orthodox.
Once, on ge ting good news from “his beloved
Nanna,” he writes : “But I fear that my thanks
|p God for it were not quite in the form He would ,
wish, for I went down to the hotel in my joy and
drank off a lot of champagne.'’’ In another letter
he tells how he considers the apostles and other
biblical writers as men whose conception of
“God’s word” was likely to be modified and
dashed by their shortcomings as men, and he re-
gards them as not altogether trustworthy. But he
dons not wish her to alter her belief one jot for
him, and as for himself he says beliefs change and
develop with years, for Rome was not built in a
day. \ \ ■
So there he write's away at his long love-letters
at Schonhausen, and says “ there is no sound in
the house but the ticking of the clock and Odin
licking bis paws,” and he wishes she were there.
Any thought that comes he sets down—bits of
poetry, scraps of Be ranger, Shakespeare, Byron,
and fragments of French and Italian. And the
long winter wears away with higi there, galloping
up and down the banks among the snow as “ cap-
tain of theriver'dikes,” swearing'at lazy and red-
tape officials, fuming at the stupid farmers in the
land courts, throwing his big soul into his'thank-
less work, energising everybody and complaining
he can’t get away for a few days to see his sweet-
.heart,; He ends one letter saying what a magnifi-
cent sight the pack-ice on the stream is. “Farewell,
the ice is crunching and shrieking and breaking out
there, and I must be off. Hear the music of these
crystal masses hurtled together by the stream,
playing the Pappenheimer March to call me.
And the peasants are there on horseback along
the banks, all ready. Aren’t they going to sing
‘ Frisch auf, Karneraden,’ in their exultation ? Not
they ! But why don’t they, the silent clods ! it
would be fine! Good-bye, love; je t’embrasse.”
But afterwards he’s just as hard upon the mass
of electors—“these people,” he says, “who’ll
swallow the trash of a verbose demagogue, but
upon whom logical and weighty argument makes
not the least impression.” Sometimes he bubbles
into satire, “ Ah ! 23 ! a fine, fine age ! still many
illusions.” Or he tells how he has been
bored and didn’t speak a word to a visitor, but
kept a face “ like the door of a gaol,” and ends a
long letter with a page of sweet things that really
sound, as he says in another place, like “ written
kisses.”
DEATH AS FRIEND .
Photographed from, the woodcut by Alfred Rethel.
Fraulein von P.’s name is Johanna, and he
addresses her with all sorts of endearments. Some
letters begin “Angela mia,” or “dear love,” or
“sweetest heart,” or “ beloved heart,” or simply
“my heart,” “mon ange,” “only beloved Jean-
nette,” or “Juanita.” And (vthen she hadn’t
written for five whole days) “Jeanne lamechante,”
and again after a well-known song, “Ma Jean-
nette, ma Jeanneton.” Once he indulges in
Polish, “Czarna kotka, mila dusza” (black cat,
dear soul), and promises to explain the incanta-
tion when he comes. For Jeannette has black
hair and triste deep eyes. “Thou art like a
warm night of summer,” he writes, “with per-
fume of blossoms and the blaze of lightning run-
ning through ft.” Is not that a new Bismarck?
Here ih another letter where he touches the un-
solved questiori of tragedy: “Don’t be afraid,
dear heart, because thou now findest pleasure in
sad or tragic poems, I see in that nothing but a
sign of thy progress in sensitiveness and under-
standing for what constitutes real poetry, Inno-
cent spring lyrics belong to twelve years ' old.
But deep in the human heart, I would almost say
deep in that unconscious knowledge we have of
earthly misery and suffering, and in the dim but
mighty longings we cherish for better and nobler
conditions .of life, lies the reason for the fact that
the better kinds. of. men. are inclined to em-
phasise the wild conflict and confusion, the’
nullity, the pain ' >nd stress of- our life'
here, more than they do , the swiftly withering
sentiments of innocent mirth that belong to child-
hood. . . Every man who is cultured in heart
and mind is moved by the tragedies of the stage
and of actual life far more deeply than by anv
mere idyll or comedy. . . And if thy mind has
opened, sweet, for the moods And poetry of
Autumn, or of hoar frost in the nights of May,
and for all that is akin to the- things in the soul
of man, thou must regard that, as a proof that thou
art no longer 12 years old. The time comes for
men as for the trees in the forest to breast the
storm, and unless they strike deeper, firmer roots
they are overthrown!” And then he speaks of
the comfort of Love,and of religion, and suddenly
comes to earth again and says the “ idea of her
worrying about him spending too much money for
postage on his letters to her.” But he promises
to be very economical in all other matters.
Then a letter to his father in-law, announcing
the birth of his first-born. “ With God’s merciful
help thou hast just become the grandfather of a
healthy, well-shaped little maid. . . . Johanna is
lying quiet and limp, but in good spirits, behind
the bed-curtains, and the little creature is lying
wrapped up on the sofa, and wawls and cries now
and again. I am very pleased it is a girl, but if
it had been a wild cat I would have thanked God
when Johanna was relieved of her agony. . . .”
Then, three days after: “The little dot just
bellows like a calf led to slaughter, and never has
the slightest scruple about letting out a good yell
when everything isn’t exactly to her liking.”
Afterwards, when he is “batching” in Berlin, he
writes to his wife: “Our next-door neighbours
have five children, all young, and the youngest
bawls at night just like our little Niedchen, close
to my bedroom, so that often when I wake, still
sleepy, I fancy I hear my daughter and that I am
at home with you. It is a very good thing for
me not to get out of the habit, isn’t it, sweet-
heart?”
A.nd so on through all these letters. Love,
Religion, Duty, Patriotism, Humour, and a Good
Appetite breathe from them all to the very end of
his long life. At the age of 66 he writes from
Varzin that without his horses and his wife there
he always feels like a fish out of water. His
religion seems to me to be based rather on a
healthy instinct of compromise with reason than
,on pure intellectual process, just as Goethe’s
optimism was. His love of nature, too, was no
less deep than Goethe’s. Let me conclude with
two quotations from Berlin letters, 1851. “My
only amusement here is going for long walks. The
Thiergarten is charming, and as it is always rain-
ing now in April, I go splashing ahead through
the mud with thick - soled boots, and never
meet a soul. The leaves of the elderberries are as
big as a shilling-piece, and gooseberry leaves,
spiraea and hawthorn even, shimmer green in
the, woods, the last still very pale however.” And
a few days later: —“ All the trees are in leaf, only
the oaks have quite tiny leaflets, the beeches,
lindens, chestnut trees already afford shelter
against the rain, you can no longer see through
the green of the underwood, the fruit trees are in
full bloom and the red hawthorn is a mass of
colour. There were too many people out to-
day, but tlnijt didn’t disturb the nightingales,
which sang out as though they were in the solitudes
of our dear 8c Ik in hausen. ”
F.S.D.
From Berlin, to The Bulletin, Dec. 6.
“Death as Friend."
THE germ of Du Maurier’s verses sought for
by “Albyn” (15/12/’OQ), and here printed
with (?) Sully Prudhomme’s original
19/1/’01), was said by some contributors to
be a German poem “Der Tod als Freund.” En-
quiry has brought no such poem; but Mr. T.
Bladen kindly forwards a photograph of the “in-
expressibly noble and pathetic woodcut grotesque ”
(Ruskin), “Der Tod als Freund,” by the German
artist Alfred Rethel (1816/’59),
Mr. Bladen sends also the following piece of
verse, which appeared forty years rtgo as “a
translation” of the woodcut, and is not without
charm and value.
HER TOD ALS FREUND.
A Translation of tiir Picture by Alfred Rethbl.
Pater Noster qui es in Coelis,
Is the sun shining ? I thought he set
An hour ago ! but I forget,
And I seem to feel from over the hill
The red glow bathing my forehead still.
Sanctificetur nomen tmim, - . j
Very quiet it seems to-night,
Very quiet from left to right; !
And I know full well, though I cannot see,
How the calmness falls over meadow and tree,
; And the carven pinnacles clear and high,
How still they stand in the quiet sky.
Adveniat regmim tuum,
I feel like a sick nian praising God
When his fever is spent and he walks abroad,
And the peace that flows from all peaceful things
Wells fresh in his worn heart’s shrunken springs,
Till his eyes o’erflow, and the world grows dim
Arid he hushes the chords of his own weak hymn
To join in the silent psalmody
Floating up from the brooks, and fields, and sky
To the great God, pouring down from above
Such wealth of glory, and peace, and love.
Fiat voluntas in a,
Yea, very quiet it seems to-night,
Very quiet to left and right,
Very quiet and very sweet'
I would die to-night if God thought-meet!
Sicut in coelo. ita in terra,
Did I read it to-night, or long ago,
Of the blessed Eulalia’s shroud of snow,
And the dove from her martyr lips that flew
With the girlish spirit so white and true?
Ay me, O God, Thou hast called to thee
Full many a soul as white as she !
Ay, I tolled the bell but yesterday
For such an one in the twilight.'grey.
Was it yesterday ? lam very old,
And my eyes wax dim, and .my blood grows cold.
Is it .forty years ? There was snow on the ground
That lay on my heart as the years rolled round.
But green is the churchyard how below,
And my heart is cold, but not with the snow.
Panem quotidianum da nobis hodie,
Is it a bird ? Ay, chirp for thy crumbs,
Thou shalt have them, my child, when Tinh comes :
For there’s something strange on my heart and brow,
And I cannot get up to serve thee novv.
Et remitte nobis debita nostra,
I do not think I am tolling the bell,
Yet surely I hear it knolling a knell.
Is it Armdf ? No, he never tolled so well.
Ha ! old friend, is it truly thou ?
’Tis kind to come thus to help me now,
For I feel almost too feeble to rise,
And it seems a labour to open my eyes.
Atyl well! you can toll even better than I ;
But, who for ? I forget, for so many die,
• Yet I.thought-when they told me,.’twas one-I - knew,
One near and dear, but. I wot not vvho.
Et libera nos a malo. Amen.
Music is it?. ’Tis many a year t /
Since t heard the requiem sound so clear.
Ilona eis, dona eis aetemum !
And hark to the organ, how calm and deep !
Seven years I have heard it not, save in sleep.
Oh, hearken ! It never seemed so near ;
Is it She who singeth so sweet and clear 7
Solvet sasclum in faeilla,
Teste David cum Sibylla.
Oh, to rise and join ! If I. could but see 1
Dear God. . .
Nunc dimittis, Domine !
_ Bethel, born at Aix-la-Chapelle, is the German
Blake as mad and mystical, and perhaps greater
m his art. He early , won fame as historical
painter; but his most famous work dates from his
imaginative years after 1846, when his health and
reason became disturbed.
“ While he hovered between madness and sanity ”
(says a writer in the Encyclopedia Britannica), “ with a
mind as Mr. Ruskin has said in reference to the very
parallel case of William Blake—“ disturbed, but not de-
ceived by its sickness, nay, partly exalted by it, Bethel
produced some of the most striking, individual, and
impressive of his works. Strange legends are told
of the effect produced by some of his weird sub-
jects. He painted Nemesis pursuing a Murderer a
flat stretch of landscape, with a slaughtered body
relieved against the flushing evening sky, while
in front is the assassin speeding away itno the
darkness, clutching his blood-stained knife, thorns
and thistles springing up around his path, and above
hovering over his head, with unhasting but ceaseless
flight, an angel of vengeance, holding an hour-glass from
which the last sands are escaping, and a sword which is
slowly descending upon the fated head. The picture, so
the story goes, was won in a lottery at Frankfort by a
personage of high rank, who had been guilty of an undis
covered crime, and the contemplation of his prise drove’
him to distraction, and he became a lunatic.
Another design which Bethel executed was Death the
Avenger, a skeleton appearing at a masked ball, scraping
daintily, like a violinist, upon two human bones. The
drawing haunted the memory of his artist friends and
disturbed their dreams ; and, in expiation, he produced
his pathetic design of Death the Friend, a skeleton draped
in long monk’s robes, tolling solemnly the passing bell in
a church-tower, while beside the open window, lit by the
last sunset radiance, sits an old sexton, with the peaceful
face of a quiet departure.
So close is the correspondence between Rethel’s
some stanzas of Matthew Arnold’s
Wish, that it seems almost certain that the
poem was inspired by sympathy with the draw-
ing ; and it is here reprinted for comparison,
in the Trans] at ion 77 (author unknown) for-
warded by Mr. Bladen, readers of Austin Dobson
will see a distinct co-relation with the tone and
form of Dobson’s most charming piece of verse
‘•Goodnight, Babette ! ”
' A WISH. , i, "
I ask not that my bed of death
From bands of greedy heirs be free ;
For these besiege the latest breath
Of fortune’s favour’d sons, not me.
I ask not each kind soul to keep
Tearless, when of my death he hears.
Let those who will, if any, weep !
There are worse plagues on earth than teas.
I ask but that my death may find
The Freedom to my life denied;
Ask hutJhe folly of mankind /
Then, then at last to quit in V side.- .
Spare me the whispering,crowded room,
The friends who come, and gape,.and 'jso ;
•, The ceremonious’air of gloom—
All, which makes death a hideous showl
Nor bring, to see me cease to Jive,
Some doctor full of phrase and fame
To shake.his sapient head, and give
The ill he cannot cure a name.
Nor fetch, to take the accustom’d toll
Of the poor sinner bound for death,
His brother-doctor of the soul,
To canvass with official breath
The future and.its viewless things—
That undiscovered mystery
Which one who feels death’s winnowing wings
Must needs read clearer, sure, than he !
Bring none of these ; but let me be,
While all around in silence lies,
Moved to the window near, arid see
Once more, before my dying eyes,
Bathed in the sacred dews of morn
The wide aerial landscape spread—
The world which was ere I was born,
The world which lasts when I am dead ;
Which never was the friend of one,
Nor promised love it could not give,
But lit for all its generous sun,
And lived itself, and made us live.
There let me gaze, till I become
In soul, with what I gaze on, wed !
To feel the universe my home ;.
To have before mv mind— instead
Of tire sick room, the mortal strife,.
The turmoil for a little breath—
The pure, Ujfernal course of life,
Not human conibatings with death !
Thus feeling, gazing, might I grow
Composed, refresh’d, ennobled, clear
Then willing let my spirit go
To work or wait elsewhere or here.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Hobart Mercury wants all Boers in arms
to be executed whenever captured, pleading that
the “recognised usages of war do not tolerate
useless resistance or unauthorised attacks by com-
batants out of uniform.” The Boer uniform con-
sists only of a slop suit and a shocking bad hat,
and the Mercury thinks this shouldn’t be recog-
nised. The plea that soldiers should be executed
for holding out even when hope is lost, as the
British Gordon did at Khartoum, and gallant
Richard Grenville when he fought the Spanish
fleet in the Revenge, and brave Williams when
he defended Kars, and as the Briton prides him-
self on doing all the world over, is really worthy
of a paper of the Stone Age edited with an axe.
And the theory that soldiers should be shot when
captured because their Government is too poor to
buy them a glaring uniform and a big hat with a
feather in it is very Tasmanian. If ever Tas-
mania is invaded, and nobody except the 1800
men in uniform are allowed to defend it, the other
97,000 males being justly and indignantly executed
for defending their country without having their
beautiful official clothes on, it will be a poor look
out for Raspberry Land. How would the Mer-
cury have yelled if the Boers had executed their
British prisoners at Nicholson’s Nek because for
hours they kept up a “ useless resistance ” —a thing
which, it seems, the usages of war don’t tolerate ?
Ave Csosar!
They thronged through the streets and the high-
ways, >
They mounted the turret and steeple,
As a god was the man whom they honored
With trumpet and' chorus—the people !
The bugle re-echoed the bugle,
The cannon far bellowed the story,
And drunk with the wine of enchantment
The multitude chanted of glory.
I looked on the sea of lit faces
That swayed with a strange undulation.
I said, “ Whom they honor must surely
Have brought a great good to the nation.”
I said, “It is sure the Redeemer,
The Bringer of Heart’s-ease they cherish,
The Redresser, the anciently sought one,
The Saviour of those who would perish.”
The lights died, the noise passed, the music
And clamour in slumber were swallowed ;
None moved save the urchin and harlot,
Alone walked the lost and unhallowed.
I said, “ As of old, for the Victor
Wreath-hung are the ways of the city ;
But what of the lost and the Ishmael—
The outlaws, —the outcasts of Pity ? ”
N.s.w.
RODERIC QUINN.
Credo.
I want no Heaven for recompense,
I love no names divine ;
The World I know, and in each sens#
I feel the World is mine.
You own no sovereignty but pain.
No sceptre but the sad ;
Oh, sorrow has my full disdain,
Because my heart is glad.
The only gods whereto I kneel
Are Sunlight and the Vine,
And the great blue Night with stars of steel
And fragrances divine;
And the glories of the Absolute,
Beauties I touch and see—
Heaven may have fairer flower and fruit,
But the Earth will do for me.
The angels of your Paradise
Are breastless and above;
I own my girl’s bold laughing eyes
And bright firm breasts of love.
V.
L.L.
Diana—Venus?
Between the day and the startled night
The great white Star of pride
Joys in the flash of her beating light
By the frail Moon’s virgin side.
Slow, slow they sink beyond the pale,
In the grip of the dark held tighter,
The Star in the toils of a lurid veil,
The lean Moon shining brighter.
v.
L.L.
The Discobolus.
Flecked with cool shadows from the quiet trees,
The white Discobolus, all night forlorn,
Welcomes through misty avenues of morn
The magian sun’s gilt plausibilities.
Measuring his cast he Stands in perfect ease,
His strength and splendid beauty lightly borne—
How would he smile our wilted life to scorn
Could he descend the broken centuries !
Far through the billowing leafage of the park
The vile, old, growling, garrulous City lifts
The burden of his toiling, trampled rhyme—
Inevitable life, toil, trouble—then the Dark.
Would He accept such Fate’s invidious gifts,
This Quoiter from the idle youth of Time ?
V.
L. L.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“ Correspondence” column all contributions submitted,
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in length,
or, say, 8000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters, or Newspaper clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that we
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 9, 1901.
Free Trade and a Free Nigger Supply.
Premier Barton’s announcement that the
Federal Government intends to have a
White Australia has brought out the Bris-
bane Courier and the Sydney Telegraph as
advocates of Freetrade and Black Labor.
Freetrade in calico goes naturally hand
in hand with Freetrade in niggers. It
is illogical to let the nigger’s produce in free
and keep out the nigger himself. It is
absurd to preach the doctrine of buying in
the cheapest market, and not buy labor in
the cheapest market. It is mere foolishness
to worship at the shrine of the great alleged
Joss of Demand and Supply, and yet object
when the demand for lots of cheap nigger
labor is met by the supply of lots of cheap
nigger labor. The announcement that the
Barton Government intends to make
Australia white and keep it white
has knocked the thin veneer of Liberal-
ism off the surface of the Courier
and the Telegraph, and the former now
publishes hysterical letters demanding that
Barton and his White Australia policy shall
be got rid of together, and that great and
good man, George Reid, whom they seem-
ingly regard up North as a vigorous cheap-
higger-labour advocate, shall be put in his
place. The Telegraph also advocates the
preservation and multiplication of Kanakas—
but not openly in its leading columns. It
bolsters up the nigger semi-surreptitiously,
by means of signed articles on back pages,
while the Courier shrieks in its editorial
columns and in its editorial voice. In the
short period that has elapsed since Premier
Barton’s manifesto theCoußiEß has shrieked
twice—once in each direction. It started by
declaring that the Queensland sugar industry
did not depend on Kanaka labour ; and then,
being apparently kicked hard by the large
boots of some unseen Power behind the
Throne, it proceeded to declare that it did.
It kept to its White Labour attitude for
about 10 days. Its Black Labour attitude
has apparently come to stay.
* * * *
Though Premier Barton declared em-
phatically . for a white Australia, he was
moderation itself as to the means by which
Australia was to be made white. He distinctly
intimated that the Queensland planter was
to be let down gently and allowed time in
which to accustom himself to new conditions.
In his own words :
Legislation against any influx of Asiatic labor we shall
regard simply as a matter of course. As to Polynesian or
Kanaka labor, if we were at the beginning of it now we
should have an equally strong objection to that, but we
shall not be guilty of any oppression of those Kanakas
who are already in Australia, while we shall take care to
endeavor to restrict the importation of any more of them.
We shall try to prevent any such importation as will
increase the number of Kanakas now in Australia. We
shall ensure the gradual abolition of the importation of
these people, but our legislation will be just and firm,
and there will be no unnecessary delay.
And on this subject the Brisbane Courier
declared in its leading article of 15tli J anuary
—just before Barton’s pronouncement:—
There appears to be some misconception in the Southern
States of Australia as to the condition and needs of the
sugar industry in Queensland, and it may be well to direct
attention to the salient facts. Mr. Coghlan, in the last
edition of his “Seven Colonies of Australasia,” has the
following sentence :—“ The growth of the cane and the
manufacture of sugar are important industries in Queens-
land and New South Wales ; but whilst the climate of
the former colony renders the employment of white labor
in the field almost impossible, the plantations of the
latter are worked, as a rule, without the assistance of
colored labor.” It would appear from this that sugar-
growing in Queensland by white labor is practically
impossible. Consequently, if the Commonwealth Parlia-
ment should decide to deal peremptorily with black
labor, and legislate at once to exclude the Kanaka,
legislators would shelter themselves behind the assump-
tion that sooner or later the sugar industry would have
to go, and that no good purpose could be served by delay.
That is to say, those who pin their faith to a White Aus-
tralia would consider themselves justified in resorting to
immediate surgery, on the ground that the chance of
substituting white labor for black on the plantations was
too small to be worth considering. So much would be
cheerfully decided with Mr. Coghlan as guide. Now, it
may be remarked here that Mr. Coghlan’s own figures
prove him to be in error. He notes that the census of
1891 gave Queensland 942 S Polynesians, while a census
taken in October, 1898, reduced the number to 8617. In
seven years the Kanaka population of this colony had
been reduced nearly 9 per cent. But the point of im-
portance in such connection is the increased area put
under cane in the time. In 1891 there were 50,948 acres
devoted to sugar cultivation, while in 1899 there were
110,657 acres. While the number of Kanakas had de-
creased considerably, the number of white planters had
immensely increased. This is the only conclusion to be
drawn from Mr. Cogiilan’s figures. Why, then, assume
that sugar-planting in Queensland, without black labor,
is an impossibility ?
■* * * *
Up to the 17th January, therefore, the
Courier and Premier Barton were saying
the same thing. The Courier, which, as a
Queensland paper, ought to know something
of the matter, declared that the Queensland
sugar industry could do without black labor,
and that it was doing without it more and
more each year ; all it wanted was not to
have its comparatively small remaining
supply of infuriated Islanders cut off too
suddenly. And Premier Barton said that
for the present he was merely going to so
far restrict the importation of Kanaka labor
as to make sure that the number of
Islanders in Australia would not increase.
Importation was to be abolished certainly,
but the abolition was to be gradual. The
period of grace in which the planter is
to accustom himself by degrees to the
loss of his brown savage—that period of
grace which, the Courier asserted, is the
only thing needful—Premier Barton pro-
mises liberally., The only thing he is going
to abolish at once is the influx of Asiatic
hawkers, Chinese fossickers, Japanese
houris, and such like. Barton does not
even say that he is going to immediately
decrease the number of Polynesians ; only to
prevent any enlargement of their numbers.
And the Courier asserts that the Poly-
nesians are already decreasing under the
existing Queensland laws. Therefore the
worst thing that Barton threatens in the
immediate present is that he will keep on
doing what is doing already.
* • * *
Yet after declaring that Queensland does
not need black labor, and only requires time
to taper off, as it were, and after being
assured by Premier Barton that the Kanaka
would be tapered off and not violently re-
moved, the Courier, on 26th January, broke
out in this fresh place :
It is perfectly clear that the black labor question is
vital to us.. . . . As between Mr. Thynne and Mr. Drake,
the latter is out of court altogether as a representative of
our primary industries and most vital interests. Had
Mr. Philp realised the true position of affairs he would
have given Mr. Barton his choice of men who could be
trusted from their past careers to see that the case for
the sugar industry was fairly presented and vigorously
advocated. Instead of offering him an enemy, he would
have fought for a friend, of the industry, and Mr. Barton
would have been obliged to take the responsibility
of rejecting our manifest claims. Now he may
contend that the Premier of Queensland has
practically endorsed his manifesto on black labor!
It is quite evident that the so-called nomination was
little better than pure farce, and that Mr. Philp had
neither the sense nor the courage to make a fight of it.
Henceforth Queenslanders must think and act for them-
selves. Mr. Reid has shown himself in the past ready,
though at the last pinch, to stand by the sugar industry
in New South Wales, and if he will do as much for this
State there can be little question but that he will com-
mand a substantial following in Queensland.
Here, again, Georoe Reid, the leader of
the party which calls itself Freetrade, be-
cause it has always been found protecting as
many industries as it dared, is quoted as the
expected and hoped-for advocate of the
Black Labour Party, and the man to whom
those who desire Freetrade in niggei’s look
for their salvation.
* * *
And this is George Reid’s own special
organ, the Sydney Telegraph, on the same
subject in its issue of 30th January. It
appears as a signed article by one F. Wegg
Horne, but as the Freetrade organ gives
publicity to the article, and says not one
word in repudiation of its sentiments, it
can’t wholly disclaim responsibility :
In the actual work of cultivating the sugarcane, the
labor chiefly employed is undoubtedly black or alien.
This, it is contended' by the labor advocates, is not at all
necessary. They hold that white or European labor can
do all work required both in the field and mill, which
the planters declare is impossible, and experience cer-
tainly supports the latter. Not only did the Government
during the latter part of the eighties endeavor to prevent
the employment of Kanaka labor, by prohibiting further
importation, but the planters themselves tried to work
entirely with white labor. The attempt proved a failure,
and not only ruined many planters, but temporarily
wrecked the industry. The present Chief Justice,
Samuel W. Griffith, when in Parliament, was at first
strongly opposed to Kanaka labor, but subsequently
realised the necessities of the industry, and himself
introduced the legislation under which this and Asiatic
labor is at present sanctioned in that State At
the conference of protectionists held last year in
Sydney, this question was discussed, and, unfortunately,
many wild and extravagant statements were made which
only tend to mislead the public as regards the treatment
of the white laborer and liis willingness and ability to do
any work connected with the industry. In fact, to judge
from what was both written and said on the subject at
that time, it is evident that little or nothing is known in
the southern communities with reference to the conduct
of the industry and the conditions under which it is
carried on in the more northern parts of Australia. Be-
fore, however, attempting to clear away any of these mis-
conceptions, it must be pointed out that it is not for one
moment suggested that a white man cannot work in the
tropical heat. In North Queensland, as elsewhere, he
well and ably fills the ordinary avocations of life to which
he is accustomed in more genial climates, and this he
does with but little detriment to his health. But the cir-
cumstances under which he so toils in the open
country, or on the higher lands, or even in a mine
—no matter how high the temperature may be—are
vastly different to those under which the sugarcane
is cultivated. The direct, blazing, dry heat of the sun
can be endured, but he is naturally physically incapable
of continuous work in the miasmatic moist heat of the
lowlands and swamps on the seaboard, with an air laden
with fever and ague. Such are some of the characteris-
tics of the country found most adapted to the sugar-
growing industry. This will be the better appreciated
when it is explained that these sugar-lands consist of low-
lying alluvial flats, on the margin of rivers or inlets of the
sea, only a few feet above high-water mark, surrounded
by mangrove swamps, and generally situated at the base
of hilly or even mountainous country protected from the
winds. Heavy tropical rains fall during the first two or
three months of the year, showing a register for the
season of from 60 to 200 inches. This bountiful supply is
evidently conserved by nature just below the surface, to
be drawn up subsequently in the form of excessive mois-
ture by the great heat of a tropical sun. By reason of
this, the vegetation is rank and thick, warding off all
breezes ; so that the atmosphere becomes peculiarly dead
and oppressive. Under such conditions the field work of
preparing the ground, and the subsequent planting,
tending, and cutting of the cane, all done by hand labor,
it will be recognised, is most exhausting and enervating.
. . . With these facts and statistics before us the diffi-
culties and importance of this question are apparent.
The suggestions of the extreme Protectionists—doubtless
put forward to catch the labor vote —to impose a suffi-
ciently heavy duty to permit of high wages being paid to
white or European labor would prove'a hardship on the
general public without any compensating good results.
Not even the most prohibitive of duties would permit of
white men doing a work for which they are naturally
unfit.
* * * •
Thus we have it distinctly laid down, that
the Protectionist party is the White Labor
party, and that fact is put to its discredit by
the enraged nigger advocate whose views are
published by the Freetrade organ. And, as
a natural inference, the Freetrade party, led
by George Reid, is to be the Black Labor
and Cheap Imported Nigger party. As a
matter of fact, the Freetrade party must be
the Black Labor party and the Kanaka’s
friend and supporter, or else it must sink
its Freetrade principles and protect
the sugar industry again, as it did before.
It dare not advocate the destruction of the
Queensland sugar industry as it once did the
sugar industry of N.S.W. —before the rotund
Reid lost faith in his nostrum and turned
semi-Protectionist. The Queensland industry
is too large and powerful to be thus treated.
And the sugar business must either be pro-
tected by a tariff or else aided by a liberal
supply of the cheapest nigger labor available.
A white sugar industry, paying white men’s
wages, and not protected by a tariff, could
not possibly stand against the product of
other countries where the cheap nigger is
available, though it is quite possible that a
nigger-run sugar industry could’. The
abandonment of the sugar business being out
of the question, it is a choice between Pro-
tection and a White Australia, or Freetrade
and a piebald land, infested by the off-
scourings of Asia and the scum of Polynesia.
And apparently the Courier and the Tele-
graph hold that Freetrade is more precious
than the purity of the race, and the preser-
vation of the European type.
* * • *
Queensland is itself a living proof, in a
small way, that the sugar-industry can be
run by white labor under a Protectionist
policy. In Queensland, sugar is heavily pro-
tected, and the industry increased enormously
with no increase of Kanaka labor. In 1890,
according to figures published by the
Courier, there were about 50,000 acres under
cane, and 9362 Kanakas to cultivate this
area. In 1899, the area under cane was
110,657 more than double and the
Kanakas had decreased to 8795. So far as
concerns the difference, it appears that the
white man has, to a great extent, filled the
gap—we have the Courier’s authority to that
effect. Thereby, it appears that the white
man, under a Protective tariff, can grow
sugar in Queensland. And the Courier
itself declared, in its attitude of January 15,
that it could ; that a white sugar-indus-
try was possible ; that Queensland was not
in any sense dependent on its drunken and
murderous islander ; and that all it re-
quired was that the change of conditions
should not be too sudden—a concession
which Premier Barton freely promised.
Apparently, it was by an afterthought that
both tha Courier and the Telegraph
realised that a White Australia involves a
continuance of Protection, and that, so far
as the sugar industry is concerned, Free-
trade and the cheap nigger go together.
And having to throw one overboard, they
threw over White Australia.
» ♦ * ¥
The matter is one of cheapness only ; not
one of fitness. The Kanaka has no special
fitness for plantation work ; in faot, he is
less fitted for it than the white Queens-
lander. The short and simple proof is that
he dies off at the work twice, or more than
twice, as fast as the white man does. The
mortality among adult Kanakas in Queens-
land, as compared with white man mortality, is
enormous, andalmost incredible, andthesugar
plantations are merely a Brown Man’s Grave.
The only special qualification the Islander
possesses is that he can die without attract-
ing so much attention as a white man would ;
otherwise, judging by the mortality statis-
tics, he is about the least fit person that
can be imagined for plantation work. As
regards the “temporary wrecking” of the
Queensland sugar industry when a half-
hearted attempt was made, between 1885
and 1892, to dispense with the Islander, it is
a pretence that won’t stand very close in-
vestigation. The supply of Kanakas is less
now than it was in 1890, and yet the acreage
under cane has more than doubled. There-
fore, whatever it. was that saved the indus-
try, it certainly was not any extra supply of
Tommy Tanna. Further, white labor is
both cheaper and more abundant now than
it was in the late eighties, and the alleged
wreck which happened then wouldn’t neces-
sarily happen now. And, in addition to this,
the industry was not being wrecked at the
time when Griffith did his famous apos-
tacy, and revenged himself for the alleged
ingratitude of the democracy by turning
nigger-labor advocate. Queensland did not
go back to its Kanaka-importing disease be-
cause it was driven to that course in order
that its great sugar business might be saved,
for the business wasn’t lost. When Grif-
fith gave notice to the planters that the
supply of Kanaka labour would cease about
1892, a slump certainly did set in among the
panic-stricken growers, though not nearly so
great a slump as is commonly alleged. The
area under cane decreased from 59,186 acres
in 1885 to 54,010 acres in 1886 ; 51,815 acres
in 1887 ; and 47,340 acres in 1888. But the
fall stopped there, though the final loss of
the Kanaka was every day coming closer. In
1889 the cane area had increased to
49,741 acres ; and thereafter it ex-
panded steadily till in 1892—the year
when the ruin was to be consummated, and
the year when Griffith re-established the
Kanaka trade on the ground that the sugar
industry was Lost—the area under cane had
risen to 55,520 acres. In fact, Queensland
was just proving very successfully that it
didn’t require its Kanaka when Griffith
apostatised. The trouble was, that cane-
growing was fast becoming an industry for
small farmers instead of for big capitalists,
each working a huge plantation with a drove
of slaves. The Griffith apostacy of 1892
was not required to save the sugar industry,
but to save the big land-grabber and slave-
driver.
* • * «
The question at issue now is not merely
one of maintaining the existing Kanaka. It
goes much further than that. The Queens-
land sugar industry prospers exceedingly
with a limited amount of Kanaka labor, and
Protection. W e have the solemn assurance
of the Courier that it can dispense with black
labor altogether, and it is impossible to sup-
pose that so respectable a paper as the Courier
would tell a lie about a small thing
like that. As a matter of fact, Queens-
land is becoming less and less dependent
on its Kanaka every year, and yet the
price of sugar isn’t rising—in fact, it
is moderate enough to satisfy any reasonable
person. Concerning the progress that white
labor is making in the sugar industry it
seems impossible to doubt, both on the evi-
dence and on the Courier’s assurance, that,
with the Protection now afforded, the
Kanaka could be dispensed with by the in-
stalment process which Premier Barton
proposes, without doing the slightest in-
jury to the State. But if there is to
be Freetrade in sugar then Queensland
will want, not only all the
cheap black labour it has, but a lot more.
It will then have to compete with the
cheap black labor of all the world—
and that is a large order. So two of the
most prominent Freetrade papers in Aus-
tralia declare for a Black Labor policy, and
call on George Beid, as the Freetrade
leader, to conduct a pro-nigger campaign.
This seems a perfectly natural thing for them
to do. In this case Freetrade and a free
nigger jsupply go together as a matter of
course.
Some Freetrade Inaccuracies, Mis-
representations and Sundries.
The Bulletin has collected some of the Calico
Jimmy party’s literature of the past week, and
publishes a few extracts from the interesting col-
lection herewith. The first item is from Launces-
ton (Tas.) Examiner ,
THE “SAVINGS BANKS” MISREPRESENTATION.
Mr. Edward Pdlsford, M.L.C., the well-known au-
thority upon questions relating to Freetrade, has recently
replied to the assertion so often put forth by advocates of
Protection that the figures published in connection with
savings banks are proof conclusive that there is more
money in countries boasting a restrictive tariff than, in
those whose duties are levied for revenue purposes only.
Mr. Pulsford points out that probably no facts can be
brought together that are more capable of misleading
people “ than those with regard to money and banks—
savings banks especially.” He explains that the circum-
stances connected with such financial institutions vary to
such an extent in different parts of the World that com-
parisons and lessons deductible therefrom, are, to say the
least, riskyu Another thing, the advocate for restric-
tion has an unhappy knack of quoting only such figures
as will suit his purpose. He will take one set of statis-
tical figures which, quoted singly, may convey an
entirely erroneous impression, and argue his case there-
from ; this is the course those who are holding up the
returns from savings banks as an example of what Pro-
tection can accomplish are adopting. British prosperity,
we are told, is on the decline, because some statistics
compiled by Muliiall —a fair authority, we admit, when
fairly' quoted—show that protectionist Germany, France,


or some other foreign country has more money in sayings
banks than freetrade England. . . . “ NOT ONLYMr.
Pulsford explains, “ IS THERE NO SHADOW OF CON-
NECTION BETWEEN THE AVERAGE OF DEPOSI-
TORS AND GENERAL PROSPERITY, but there is no
connection between the amount of deposits and number
of depositors.”
* * * *
The first discrepancy here lies in the fact that
the Protectionist party didn’t start the Sayings
Bank controversy, and isn’t responsible for it in
any way. The man who started it was George
Reid, who a few weeks ago blew this remarkable
blast on his political trumpet, and kept on blow-
ing it till it became really necessary to tell him
what an exhibition he was making of himself: —
The great millions of England are rising under it
(Freetrade). The Savings Banks of England have
£150,000,000 deposited in them— an increase of £36,000,000
in four years. THERE you see the vitality of the great
principle of human freedom breaking through the clouds
that encompassed it, bringing down the high and raising
the poor and miserable.
That was the beginning of the Savings Bank con-
troversy. It commenced by the emphatic state-
ment of the Dry Dog man that Savings Bank de-
posits were a test of prosperity. And The Bulle-
tin, which took up the question, simply took
the Freetrade boss at his word. So it quoted
page 86 in Mulhall’s “Dictionary of Statistics,”
whereon it was set forth that in 1840, about the end
of the Protectionist period in Britain, that country
had £23,400,000 in its Savings Banks, while
France, Germany and Austria had only £14,600,000
among them. Therefore Protectionist Britain
could beat those three countries put together. And
in 1896, when Britain had enjoyed 50 years of
Freetrade, it had £154,800,000 in its Savings
Banks, while France had £165,800,000;
Austria, £178,400,000; and Germany, £212,500,000
(Mulhall, page 643). Where Protectionist
Britain was ahead of all these three countries
put together, Freetrade Britain couldn’t keep
abreast of even the poorest of them. It also
pointed out that Victoria had more Savings Bank
deposits and all kinds of deposits per inhabitant
than N.S.W. And then the Freetrade party,
which had declared (while it could keep up the
pretence that Britain was ahead in this respect)
that Savings Bank deposits were a proof of a
country’s prosperity, promptly declared that they
didn’t prove anything at all. Also it professed
to believe that it was the Protectionist
party, instead of its own lamentable Crowd of
frauds and figure-fakers, that had started the Sav-
ings Bank discussion, and was quite surprised,
from its superior mental pedestal, that they
should resort to such an absurd argument, and
grew quite excited about the “Savings Banks
Misrepresentation.” Yet even now, while Free-
trader Pulsford goes round declaring, since the
Freetrade yarn has burst, that Savings Bank
deposits don’t mean anything, Freetrader Reid,
who apparently doesn’t know it has burst, still
goes heavily to and fro on the face of an over-
loaded globe saying that the £150,000,000 in the
Freetrade country’s Savings Banks prove that
Freetrade is the best policy, and judiciously sup-
presses all mention of the much larger sum in the
Savings Banks of Protectionist countries. Will
Freetrader Pulsford kindly tell Freetrader Reid
to drop the Savings Bank fiction ?
» * • •
The S.M. Herald also had an outbreak, the
other day (30th January, 1901); —
In a letter appearing elsewhere Mr. Mark Hammond
alleges that we have not denied that New South Wales
raised from borrowed money and land sales £40,000,000
more than Victoria. Regarding £15,000,000 of the sum,
representing this colony’s excess of public borrowings, we
distinctly stated that the amount was more than offset
by the larger borrowings of the private capitalists of Vic-
toria, Mr. Pulsford giving figures in proof other than
those we had at hand in support of the statement.
Here, as is usual with the Foreign Shoddy party,
the S.M. Herald offers no evidence in support of
its statement that Victoria has borrowed more
than New South Wales, and is consequently more
dependent than the partially-Freetrade State on
artificial assistance from loans to keep its credit
and industries afloat. It doesn’t mention where
the figures are to be found. It merely says that
things are so, and, by way of further evidence, it
says that Pulsford says they are so ; also, that
Pulsford has produced figures, not quoted, from
some source, not mentioned, which, it says, sup-
ports #this theory. Wherefore The Bulletin,
whicli is always able to quote the source of its
statistics, as they always have a source, refers to
N.S.W. Statistician Coghlan’s latest pronounce-
ment on the question (“ Seven Colonies for 1898,
page 429 —the subject is apparently not dealt with
in the 1900 edition): —
Total net Public and Private Borrowing.
New South Wales .. ~ .. £106,655,000
Victoria.. .. .. .. .. 90,544,000
The Herald may “distinctly state,” in the
Foreign Shoddy interest, that Victoria has
borrowed more than N.S.W. until it grows grey
and old and senile, but the logic of official statis-
tics is against it.
* * * *
The next is the latest sample of George Reid’s
irrelevance, Dry Dogism and mental confusion :
Comparing Great Britain with America and Germany,
the trade of Great Britain is greater than the total of the
United States and Germany.
Mulhall, who, as a statistician of eminence,
probably knows something about these matters,
figures out, in his “Industries and Wealth of
Nations ” (page 388) that in 1896 the total trade of
the United States was £3,311,000,000; that of
Great Britain £1,884,000,000, or not very much
more than half that of the United States; and
that of Germany £1,501,000,000, or close up to
Great Britain. Reid, as it happens, was not
giving the total trade at all, though he said he
was; but only the external trade. The Foreign-
trader holds that when London sells goods to
Amsterdam (250 miles) it is trade, and when New
York sells goods to San Francisco (2500 miles)
it isn’t trade. British trade is to a very consider-
able extent a paper fraud. It is a common cus-
tom to include only external trade as commerce,
and the British Empire is the only great State
which counts the trade of one part of the Empire
with another part as external trade. If the
United States statistics reckoned the trade of
New York with Illinois as foreign commerce, in
the same way as the British Empire statistics
reckon the trade of England with India, Malta,
and other parts of the Empire as foreign com-
merce, it would leave Britain absolutely nowhere.
When the commerce of the two States is calculated
on the same basis (internal and external trade
both reckoned), the United States shows £49 per
head against Britain’s £4B per head. The differ-
ence in favor of the Protectionist country isn’t
large, but the real thing as shown by reliable
statisticians, and the familiar bumptious lie of the
Calico James party, are a long way apart.
• "* * *

These are a few casual items of the past few
days only, but the list will doubtless extend
indefinitely, for the party of the l)ry Dog man is
evidently resolved to keep on in a state of the
wildest inaccuracy right up to the date of the
Federal election, or even later. In fact, through
sheer force of habit it will probably remain in that
condition all its days.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
An "Extreme Freetrader,” or an
Extreme Importer.
Last week a N.S.W. politician of some note in a
small way, who has long been suspected of tot-
tering obscurely on a fence, got down definitely
on to his feet, and being a rather brief man, the
roof of his belltopper was barely visible over the
top rail when he reached the ground. William
M‘Millan, the politician in question, delivered
his confession of faith thus :
Was it necessary to say that in that respect he was still
a freetrader ? If he thought it was his duty to follow
Mr. Barton he would do so at the risk of the Wentworth
electorate. He might say, however, that he had not the
slightest intention of following Mr. Barton, because he
had always been an extreme freetrader. (Applause.) If
he had the honor of representing them he would take his
seat on the Opposition benches. This matter of policy
having been brought so prominently forward, he had
come to the conclusion that at the first address he
would deliver he would deal almost entirely with the
fiscal issue, leaving all other matters for subsequent
addresses. There was much talk about the Constitution
being in danger. It was all “ bunkum.” If it had ever
been in danger it was not so now. As Freetraders they
were determined to fight this battle to the bitter end, and
it was necessary they should have a leader. Certainly he
had been for some time out of public life, but as far as he
could judge the man who was looked upon as leader was
Mr. G. H. Reid.
This is a sorrowful example of how a small man,
whose belltopper is barely visible over the top-
rail when he gets down off the fence, can delude
himself with fine phrases and noble but bogus
sentiments. M‘Millan was never an extreme
Freetrader; he was only an extreme importer,
which is quite a different thing. In the days
when he was Freetrade Treasurer of New South
Wales he was certainly an extreme Freetrader so
far as softgoods and the like were concerned. He
lived by importing them, and it was his interest
to blight and destroy any local industries in these
lines, and prevent the N.S. W. people getting em-
ployment and good wages at making their own
clothes and hats, for if they did his trade would
naturally be damaged. It didn’t necessarily fel-
low that he believed that locally-manufactured
softgoods, made under a protective tariff, would
be dearer or worse than his own imported stuff ;
there is no evidence on that point. Whether
they would be dearer or cheaper, better or worse,
it was his interest, as an extreme importer, to
prevent any N.S.W. softgoods industries arising,
even if the people lost thereby, and to make
them buy imported softgoods even if the price
was higher than local factories, when they got
fairly established, could supply at. In his own
softgoods line M‘Millan was naturally asFreetrade
as anyone could ask for. But so far as concerned
wine, beer, bacon, tobacco, cigars, cigarettes, sugar,
molasses, hams, tongues, fruit of many kinds,
biscuits, butter, timber, wooden goods, galvanised
iron goods, candles, cheese, cement, cider, con-
fectionery, soft drinks of all kinds, corn-flour, fish,
jams, jellies, and very many other things.-
M‘Millan was as good a Protectionist as anybody
could ask for. All these represented industries
which it wasn’t necessary to blight and destroy
for the good of M'Millan’s vested interest, for.
he didn’t import hams and the likes. And h;e en-
couraged these industries with all the outward
semblance of a man who firmly believed that
Protection was a good thing—always provided, as
aforementioned, that the industries which were
thus created to supply the people with work and
wages didn’t interfere with M'Millan’s importing
business. All M‘Millan’s Protective duties
(which didn’t interfere with his own importing
business) could easily have been abolished, and the
revenue which they yielded could have been made
good by direct taxation, which was practically non-
existent then (and which would have fallen on the
profits of M'Millan’s business). And
if M‘Millan had believed in Freetrade half as
much as he thought he did, hes hor.ld ha\e gladly
paid his share of this direct taxation, knowing
that the increased prosperity of the country
(arising through Freetrade) would so much im-
prove his business as to make good the loss. But,
though M‘Millan regarded himself as an extreme
Freetrader, he was only an extreme importer, and
wasn’t built that way. The Bulletin is still
waiting to meet that Freetrader who believes
sufficiently in his own alleged doctrine to live
up to it. Certainly the little man who has
just slid off the fence he was suspected of
roosting on, and disappeared on the other side,
isn’t that kind of Freetrader. If he believed, as
he says, that Protection is wholly injurious, then
he was a bad, mean, treacherous, or incompetent
little person, who, by his semi-Protectionist tariff,
set out to do grievous injury to the country rather
than impose a little direct taxation which, would
have fallen partly on himself. And if he didn’t
set out to do N.S.W. an injury by his semi-
Protectionist tariff, then he doesn’t believe that
Protection is injurious after all, and in that case
it may be fairly suspected that he is only a Free-
trader so far as his own calico-importing industry
is concerned —is a sort of “ rice-Christian,” in fact.
Either way he is in danger of inspiring a good-
deal of public disrespect.
For Jurymen Only.
In a strangely humorous way the Vic. Solicitor-
General has made reference to two notable cases
of husband-shooting wherein Melbourne jurymen
distinguished themselves last year by acquitting
the wives who did the deed. It seems that Mr.
Agar Wynne was lately asked to consider whether
the time hadn’t arrived for releasing Mrs. Cecile
Anderson from prison. Twelve years ago, or
more, the much-pitied woman—a widow—was
convicted of lodging a fatal bullet in her former
sweetheart, one Fraser. She had undoubtedly
suffered scurvy treatment from the man. He had
lived free at her boarding-house and otherwise
made use of her under promise of marriage, and
when at last he went away and took another
woman to wife Mrs. Anderson expressed her
feelings s aforesaid. In the days before the land-
boom crime was crime at the Melb. courts. A
matter-of-fact jury found Mrs. Andeeson guilty
of wilful murder, recommending her to mercy on
the ground that she had received much provocation
from her contemptible masher. The hanging
penalty was transmuted into a sentence of
imprisonment for life. Several attempts
to get Mrs. Andeeson out of gaol have
been made by her sympathisers, but the
changing heads of the Crown Law Dept, have
held only one opinion re the woman who shot
a man with malice aforethought. To order her
early release as an act of mercy would be to estab-
lish a dangerous precedent, said the Law. So
Mrs. Andeeson went on wearing away her life in
a dungeon cell. Lately it occurred to some people
that the non-conviction of Mrs. Dr. Fraser and
Mrs. Weight were precedents which would
entitle the less fortunate shooter to favorable
notice. A mistaken idea, apparently. Solicitor-
General Wynne says “he does not see his way
clear to recommend Mrs. Andeeson’s release,
especially in view of recent cases of a somewhat
similar nature, wherein soft-hearted juries gave
unexpected and incomprehensible verdicts in
favor of accused women.” Are these soft-hearted
juries listening ? A woman who has passed many
long years in prison for venting her rage in the
same way that Mrs. Fraseb and Mrs. Weight
chose to go mad, is now doomed to a further term
of gaol simply because jurymen gave incomprehen-
sible verdicts in their cases. With the kindest of
intentions, they reduced trial by jury to a scandal-
ous farce by acquitting two female prisoners, and
a female convict has to lose her chance of liberty
in the interests of the outraged law. An unjust
verdict in a criminal case is as fertile of ill conse-
quences as the thistle-seed in a corn-field,
Reid’s “Trade” Tarradiddle.
“F.C.” writes on the endless list of Freetrader
Reid’s inaccuracies:—
Consider once more the capers, at Richmond (Vic.), of
G. H. Reid, the Great Dry Dog Statistician. Said he
{vide Argus, 22/l/’01): “The trade of Great Britain is
greater than the total trade of the United States and
Germany. (Not external trade only, note, but total
trade.) The more material the industries of a country
were the better it was for everyone in that country.”
Which last is a fine, large, sonorous statement, and means
absolutely nothing, for nobody ever supposed an industry
so insignificant as to be immaterial would be any advantage
to anybody. With regard to the other statement, let me
draw George Reid’s attention to these figures from the
“Statesmen’s Year Book ” for 1900 :
United States returns for year ending June 30, 'OO
(page 1181).
Imports .. 697,148,489 dols.
Exports .. 1,203,931,222 „
1,901,079,711 „ =£380,215,942.
Germany’s returns for ’9B, latest available (page 609).
Imports .. 5,439,676,000 marks
Exports .. 4,010,565,000 ~
9,450,241,000 „ =£473,061,809.
Great Britain’s returns for ’99 (page 81).
Imports .. £485,075,514
Exports .. £329,680,196
£814,755,710
Thus, taking the British returns six months later than
those of the U.S.A. and twelye months later than those of
Germany, and giving Britain the advantage of the
increase in the.interval, that country’s external trade was
less than that of the States and Germany by some
£38,522,041. So that, had the Man with the Dry Dog
confined his assertions, to external trade only he would
still be a hopeless muddler, whereas he referred to total
trade, and on that basis (external and internal trade both
reckoned) the United States has nearly twice the com-
merce of Great Britain and Ireland. So George Reid’s
great Trade fiction is hereby added to the long and
increasing list of his inventions, which includes the
Starch inaccuracy, and the Wheat inaccuracy, and many
others. In fact, it seems as if Yes-No is simply a windy
man rampaging round the country making large, vague
assertions which mean nothing, or else telling circum-
spect tarradiddles which his hearers aren’t in a position to
disprove at the moment, in both cases the intention is the
same—to deceive ; and in both cases the result is the
same—to heap confusion on the deceiver.
The Cornstalk Income-Tax Muddle.
Although N.S.W. lias had an income-tax law for
a number of years yet that law is so curiously
fixed that even the richest inhabitant needn’t pay
anything, provided his income is only sufficiently
spasmodic. The man who has £5OO a year
regularly is liable for income-tax without any
means of escape whatever, but the man who
receives £lO,OOO one year and nothing the next
isn’t liable to pay anything. His revenue averages
£5OOO annually, but the statute exempts him
all the same. Also the man who earns
£9OOO one year and only £2OOO the next is
only liable to pay on £2OOO in each year,
and the man who makes £25,000 one year
and only £lOOO the next can only be
legally taxed on £lOOO in each year. In N.S.W.
a taxpayer may make £lO,OOO in 1896, but when
he pays income-tax on £lO,OOO about March,
1897, he is not paying his 1896 income-tax on the
actual income for 1896. He is paying his 1897
income-tax in advance, on the assumption that his
1897 income will bethe same as that for 1896. And if
it is a spasmodic and speculative income, and
drops in 1897 to £lOO, which is below the taxable
limit, then he gets a refund of all the income-ta.x
paid in 1897, and he also pays nothing in 1898, it
being assumed that the income for 1898 will be
only £lOO, the same as 1897. Therefore, if a man
has an average income of, say, £lBOO a year over
and above the exemption limit, but it is so erratic
that there are fat and lean years turn-about, the
N.S.W. income-tax law specifies that he may
legally go on in this fashion :
1895 : Income, £3200. Pays no income-tax, the impost
not then being in force.
1896 : Pays £7s—not on account of the income actually
received in 1895, but for 1896, on the assumption that the
1896 income will be the same as that for 1895. The income
for 1896, owing to drought or bad speculations or other
causes, proves to be only £2OO.
1897 : The taxpayer gets a refund from the Treasury of
the £75 paid in 1896, he having paid on the assumption
that his income for 1896 would be £3200, and it was only
£2OO, which is below the taxable limit. He pays nothing
in 1897, it being assumed that his income will be £2OO,
the same as in 1896, The income for 1897 proves to be
£4200.
1898 : He pays £lOO, not on the £4200 received in 1597,
but on the assumption that he will earn £4200 in 1898
also. The 1893 income drops to £4OO.
1899 : Tile taxpayer gets back from the Treasury £95,
on the ground that he paid in 1898 on £3SOO more than
he actually received. He pays £5 in 1899 on the assump-
tion that his income will again be £4OO. Income proves
to be £l2OO.
1900: He pays £25 on the supposition that the income
for 1900 will also be £l2OO. Strikes heavy losses and has
only £l5O clear at the end of the year.
1901: Gets back the £25, which proves to be an over-
payment. Pays nothing for 1901, the wise and intelligent
law assuming that as he made only £l5O last year he will
make the same amount this year.
By this curiously spasmodic process a compai’a-
tively rich man with a fluctuating income may
receive £9350 in five years, and though the
income-tax is £205, he may quite legally, and
strictly according to the terms of the law,
pay only £2O in all. A simple way out is
to enact that the income-tax paid in 1901
is the income-tax for 1900 charged upon the actual
income for 1900, instead of the tax for 1901 paid
in advance, on the assumption that the 1901
income will be the same as that for the pre-
vious year, and subject to refund if it proves to be
less. In fact, it is difficult to imagine bow any
Legislature contrived to avoid striking this easy
and obvious idea, but the N.S.W. Legislature
did avoid it most successfully. It is only a year
or two since it was discovered that in N.S.W.
even a very rich man need not pay income-tax
under certain circumstances, and a drop of £16,000
in the income-tax receipts for 1900, as compared
with £1899 is the natural result of the discovery.
And, unless the law is amended, some further
drops may be expeoted, for the agile fat man is
only beginning to realise the immense possibili-
ties of the situation.

Gloria Madden-O in Excelsis!
And in the daily papers The Bulletin read these
things—
MELBOURNE, Thursday. Amid surroundings of
purple and black, with the background a blaze of brilliant
uniform and gold lace, King Edward VII. was duly and
officially proclaimed from the steps of Parliament House
at noon to-day. The Lieutenant-Governor (Madden) him ■
self read the proclamation from a specially erected stand,
which was heavily hung with mourning. . . . The Lieu-
tenant-Governor arrived just before VI o’clock, and was
received by the President and Speaker, with a gilt-headed
mace as a side-show, and escorted to the stand, by this
time full of persons, whose gorgeousness, even a bird of
paradise might envy. . . . The proclamation having been
read .... Madden called for three cheers for the King,
and these were given without undue lung power. The
Lieutenant-Governor bowed and smiled, then, accom-
panied hy his staff, and followed by a many-colored
crowd, he trotted up the steps to the Qijeen’s-hall, there
to be “received" in the name of the King. . . . and
the Lieutenant-Governor went back to Macedon. . . .
The following telegraphic despatch, with regard to the
proclamation of the King here, was forwarded this
evening to the Secretary of State by the Lieutenant-
Governor :—“ Ministers considered that the proclamation
of his Majesty the King should be impressively made, and
to-day a splendid and most imposing ceremony was very
successfully achieved. ... 1 read the proclamation in
the names of myself, the Premier, President of the
Legislative Council, the Speaker of the Legislative
Assembly, and the Mayor, in the presence of thousands
of the people, who received it with tumultuous cheering,
and the most loyal approval. . . .
Then The Bulletin was exalted beyond itself;
and being strong in the flesh of Madden, and
magnificent in his spirit, and clothed with his
gorgeous uniform, and waxed with his heroic
moustachios, The Bulletin arose and spake,
saying:—
“I am Alpha and Omega, the beginning and
the last—l am C. J., I am Madden. By me do
kings rule and princes declare justice ; and there
is no Lieutenant-Governor like unto me. The
sun rises at my right hand ; the moon sets at
my left; and the earth revolves round me every
twenty-four hours, for I am the hub of the uni-
verse, the kingbolt of creation. Solomon in
all his glory was not arrayed like me. Madame
Tussaud’s waxworks were never waxed like me.
There is none like unto me, none; for I am
Madden. Therefore let King Edward Watch
This Space, for lam here. I have proclaimed
him King in the names of Myself and others. I
was the centre of a Splendid and Most Imposing
Ceremony. Now King Edward reigns indeed,
for I have announced it, and I am Madden.
The glorious company of politicians praise
Me. The noble army of lawyers praise Me.
The eyes of Australia are glued to My
gorgeous uniform. The whole world acknow-
ledges mine infinite majesty. Me Maddenum
Laudamus. Observe me spread my Tail —now !
Is it not magnificent ? Is it not sublime ? Was
there ever such a tail as My Tail in
the world before ? Never; and you know
it. From everlasting to everlasting I am
Madden ; and when I die Beauty will
perish. O all ye Green Things upon the Earth,
bless ye Madden : praise him and magnify him
for ever. Oye Whales, bless ye Madden—as he
was in the beginning, is now, and ever shall be;
Madden without end. Amen.”
Then The Bulletin gasped and gave up the
drink.
A tear ago Republican Rae desired to submit a
resolution at N.S.W. Labor League Conference
condemning the Transvaal War, but so enthusi-
astically Jingo were the members of the Confer-
ence that they refused by a decisive majority to
even discuss the matter. But what a change one
year hath wrought! Last Friday a shearers’ re-
presentative submitted to the Conference, com-
posed practically of the same men, and re-
presenting the same bodies, a regplution “That
the Labor Party opposes all wars, except wars
of defence.” References to “ military snobs and
parasites who would despise and trample on the
very people on whom they feed ” were Heartily
received. As in his own effective style the shearer
made clear the views of the bush worker on the
military spirit of the day—or rather the mili-
tary spirit of a year ago—the Conference
raised an amount of enthusiasm that
no other discussion evoked. The meeting
was unanimous, the only trouble being to
word a resolution strongly enough. One wanted
to add “ for the defence of the Australian Com-
monwealth only.” Ex-M.P. Darnley proposed
“ that we oppose a standing army, but support a
volunteer system.” This seemed like passing,
until delegate Faber pointed out that if you
teach a man to shoot he wants to shoot somebody,
and to encourage the volunteer system was
only to make, it more difficult to repress
the military spirit. The conference accepted
Faber’s rash assurance that men unskilled in
the use of weapons, when, called to fight
in defence of liberty, will prove themselves equal
to any occasion that may arise ; and the resolution
eventually adopted was for the defence of the
Australian Commonwealth only, and in opposi-
tion to a standing army, the conference not being
prepared to advocate any system of militarism,
volunteer or otherwise. Later, a resolution by
Arthur Rae, calling on the Government to
refuse any further expenditure in the S’African
trouble, was carried without one dissentient voice.
Surely such events as these justify the hope that
the awakening of the people is at hand !
Sydney City Council apparently lays to its soul
the flattering porous plaster of congratulation to
the effect that if it had a good town clerk every-
thing would be well, and bankruptcy, muddle,
debt, dirt, cuss-language, and Alderman Norton’s
dog would disappear out of its midst. Where a
second-rate city is divided into 41 municipalities,
loaded with debt, and hampered by old traditions,
limited powers, a narrow property franchise, and
the well-earned contempt of the public, even a
good Council couldn’t do much.' And Sydney’s

one that, with a big deficit every year, and holes

in the main street, it hadn’t even the courage to
levy sufficient rates to pay its way. Neverthe-
less, when the late able Town Clerk dropped his
swag of responsibilities in disgust, on the ground
