(Last Nights.)
Hall Snazelle and “ Our Navy ”
Last Monday being the 20th anniversary
of Mr. W. F. Hawtrey’s first appearance on the
stage, he did a little extra celebration at Sydney
Palace in the shape of Brandon Thomas’s pleasant
one-act play, “ A Highland Legacy.” It is a brief
proceeding, honorably located in an attic at some
address not clearly defined, and its hero is a
fatherless law-student who has just recovered from
a long illness to find himself with nothing left in
the world save 2s. 6d., a summons for £7 odd, the
honored name of MacDonnel, the love of a girl
whose apoplectic parent threatens to put her in a
convent, and the undying devotion of a broken-
down friend in a terrible hat. The friend with
the blighted hat is an actor who once played
Macbeth for one consecutive minute when nobody
was looking, and he hopes to get the young
man (who is of noble family) a billet at
255. a week as an egg-dodger in a small
provincial company that never meets with
any luck. In the midst of these disasters
there enters softly a large, ancient, humble person
in a kilt, with two inflamed knees and a letter.
The letter states that the hero’s rich uncle is
dead ; that not being on speaking terms with the
rest of the family, he has bequeathed his pile and
estate to the Kirk ; but in token of a Christian
and cantankerous forgiveness he has left his old
servant, the large, humble, aged person with the
red knees, to spend his declining years on his
nephew’s hands. The ancient servitor, in addition
to delivering this message, offers to bring ap the
bagpipes and blow them in the attic there and
then. Any other young man in such straits would
have cast the kilted encumbrance to che four
winds, but this young man treats him kindly
and introduces him to the friend with the dread-
ful hat. And he gets his reward, for the appari-
tion proves to be the allegedly dead rich uncle,
who has seemingly disguised himself by putting
red ink on his knees, and all ends well. Mr.
Hawtrey represents the uncle with an imposing
presence and a fine Scotch accent, but the most
interesting personage in the cast is Percy Walshe
as the actor friend with the good intentions and
the hat which looks as if it had been rushed over
by all the flooks and herds of the late
Tyson, M.L.C. Also an honorable mention should
be made of H. V. Preston, as a legal minion dis-
guised in beer, who tries to collect a bill but sees
three bills at once and doesn’t know whether to
insist on immediate payment of the middle debt
by the end student or otherwise.
* * • *
The story of the serious-minded brown
apparition from Mars still constitutes the regular
bill at the Palace, and no definite news of any
change has come to hand.
** * *
William Anderson’s co. has returned to
Sydney Lyceum after a long absence and the
“Ladder of Life” is the first result of its re-
appearance. This drama tears probability to
?ieces till a late hour. It is the reddest drama,
ar one, that the present writer has seen for a
very long time, and is full of red-kilted soldiers
rushing up red mountains under a glaring red
Bun, while the Russian spy, who is responsible for
most of the trouble, lies low in red clothes Dehind
an adjacent rock. It takes its name from three
acrobatic Highlanders from London, the M‘Jones,
the M‘Smith, and the M‘Brown, whose speciality
is the human rope business. Almost everything
that goes wrong is put right by these three form-
ing themselves into a rope. The M‘Brown hangs
by his heels from a sky-light whenever things
get desperate, and dangles the M‘Jones by
the ankles, and the M‘Jones dangles the
M‘Smith, and the M‘Smith defeats the ma-
chinations of the villains with both hands.
This long cataract of human being stream-
ing down on them from above is a shock
that the villains never reckon upon. They
usually remember to fasten the doors and windows
when they are going to be nefarious, but when the
three pour down on them in this fashion it never
occurs to them to hold a lighted newspaper under
the M‘Smith, whereby he might be made to
swarm rapidly with curses up the M‘Jones and
the M‘Brown and quit the scene. In melodrama
the hero never has any confidence in his best girl
till after the third act, and so Robert Inman,
when a strange child wandering past says “ muv-
ver” to his sweetheart, Miss Ada Guildford,
heaps reproaches on her that would be hardly
justifiable if she took in boarders, and rushes
away to die if possible. Then his property goes,
per forged will, to H. O. Willard, and he
is condemned to death on the statement of
a strange _ white man in a skirt, who says
he is a nigger chief; and if it wasn’t that
the M‘Jones, the M‘Brown, the M ‘Smith, and
the M'Dougall desert and carry him away with
them, he would be inevitably talked to death.
* * * »
Among a multitude of people Bert Bailey,
who is the M'Dougall, stands out conspicuously as
a cheerful red Highlander, who doesn’t take him-
self too seriously. One Bill, played by Mr.
Frank Reis, only speaks twice, but he lights his
clay pipe eloquently all the evening, and always
intends to do a deed of crime, only fears that the
other party will hit back. In one of his rare
utterances, Bill says frankly that if his wife will
hold Miss Guildford down, he doesn’t mind kick-
ing her ’ed, but he will take no chances while the
lady is up. As Miss Guildford continues up, Bill’s
orime is still reluctantly postponed. Miss Eugenie
Duggan, supposed to be the heroine, is a lady with
a painful accent such as nobody else on earth
possesses. She is pursued through four acts by
two tall murderers, who capture her occasionally,
P^ an such slow and elaborate deaths for her that
she always has time to get away. Their greatest
effort is when they propose to crush her gradually
but thoroughly with an iron foundry about the
size of Mort’s Dock, and a very realistic steam-
hammer. It is characteristic of the slowness of the
very slow, tall murderer that the only time he
really rushes at her suddenly with a knife is after
she has died from a lingering illness, evidently
consumption. Even then it takes him a little
time to realise that he is rushing at a corpse.
Miss Ada Guildford, also Robert Inman, J. H.
Nunn and the M‘Brown, M‘Jones and M‘Smith
deserve a kindly word in passing ; and the moun-
tains, the big steamer, the horses, the hosts of
men in kilts, the steam-hammer, and the battle,
murder and sudden death are all praiseworthy.
** * *
“ Djin-Djin,” which left so big a footmark
on the shores of the public mind that even the
long wash of the Commonwealth celebrations and
the like has never quite obliterated the same, will
be revived at Her Majesty’s (the Sydney one) on
Saturday. The dogs and monkeys who greatly
cheered up the late panto are included in the
cast, and The Bulletin especially trusts that the
small pocket monkey, with the long upper lip and
the clay pipe and the moleskin Ipants, who shoves
along the little barrow of bricks with an injured
expression of countenance, will not be forgotten.
That small monkey is such an accurate copy of a
labourer trying to make a living under the nefari-
ous influences of Freetrade that he amounts to a
work of genius.

The opera season afc Sydney Royai is to
positively end this week. It was to positively
end two weeks ago. but owing to loud public
encouragement it was extended for a fort-
night. However, this time it is to end for a dead
certainty. To-night (Thursday) and Saturday the
mystic German opera of “Tannhauser ” is on the
bill, and “Maritana” fills in the intervening
night.
** » *
oyaney Criterion audience has been sen-
tenced to another week of the “Bush King,” and
it is probable that the brown Australian scenery
and the interest aroused by the dogs behind the
scenes (only one canine ever becomes visible) will
carry this drama still further into futurity. After
the first act, which is ordinary indoor melodrama,
this production gives itself a violent wrench and
oecomes a diluted “ Robbery Under Arms.” It is
put together more loosely, however, than the other
gum-tree production, for the girl who loves the
hero so well that she alternately betrays him to the
police and drags him back to safety is just dropped
when the play ends without anything being done
with her at all. As a rule the girl with the strong
pure love and the cronk way of showing it dies
sitting up. often allowing herself to be shot
instead of the hero. Sometimes she marries the
quiet good man of the piece, but generally she
contrives to get shot.

The unco guid don’t have much say in
things at Kalgoorlie. Donald Macdonald was
billed to lecture there four nights, missing an
intermediate Sunday, but the Kalgoorlie Miner
of Monday duly reported that “in compliance
with strongly-urged requests Mr. Macdonald
repeated his initial lecture last evening, beginning
at 8.30 p.m. after church exits.” Mac. appears to
have squared his Presbyterian conscience by
delaying his sermon until worshippers from the
opposition establishments could arrive in time for
the overture.
** * *
At Melbourne Royal the balloon and other
scenic effects are now going for all they are worth
—especially the balloon. The cricket match is a
fairly genuine-looking event, though the catch in
the long-field, by which a bogus Maclaren dis-
missed the last Australian batsman, seems to have
been carefully provided by the management from
an easy distance. Also Brown’s jewellery shop
presents a solid, business-like appearance;
the four-in-hand, which conveys the busted vis-
count’s party to the place where their “dead-
bird ” gets beaten by a nose, is a striking success ;
likewise the Lounge at Oatlands Park Hotel, with
staircases and corridor in the rear, is a fine oppor-
tunity for Mrs. Bland Holt’s sleep-walk. But
none of these, things are as impressive as the
balloon. It is surrounded by an atmosphere
of mystery. It appears to ascend on its own with
no chance of ever coming down again, seeing that
Ranji Baker took no instructions on the subject
and has never previously travelled by means of a
bladder. In an ordinary way of aerial navigation
Baker would pull Albert Norman into the car and
ask his advice. The ties of common danger would
draw them together. But instead of seeking a
friend in need Baker simply steals the Great
Ruby from the man who is hanging on to the
basket, and then cuts him adrift. Norman when
last seen is making a swift, lonesome drop through
the clouds whilst Baker presumably continues his
strange career behind the plush curtain. Any-
way, Norman is a considerable bit of ballast to get
rid of, and the balloon ought to go up with a rush.
* * • •
“ The Great Ruby ” has a number of good
acting parts for people built to fit them. The people
at the Royal don’t fit them as a rule, and the
improvement in the performance since the first
night is not very startling. Miss Ross, as the
lady president of the Gang, can be congratulated
on making a hit, but she must beware of a
tendency to lapse into her old, slow method.
Baker’s brown Prince is a commendable change in
all respects from the heroic white Baker of other
dramas, Styan gives a bright character
sketch. Miss Florence Seymour is satis-
factory, _ Corlesse plays a robust burglar of
the ancient Briton type with Corlesseian vigor,
and Albert Norman is making an earnest
endeavour to conquer his troublesome grin. When
that is subjugated he will be able to take severe
measures with his right hand, which has a trick
of waving like the paw of a candidate at an elec-
tion meeting. Meanwhile Norman is getting
along nicely. None of the other people are quite
at home in this drama. Miss Harrie Ireland and
little Miss Athena Claudius should certainly ex-
change places. _ Each of them would be better
than the other in the other one’s shoes. The inci-
dental lords and captains and society girls
want a little more mannah all round. Chas.
Brown is not a bit like a quiet, wealthy
jeweller who thinks the aristocracy was made to
receive love, honor, and long credit from shop-
keepers, neither does Mrs. Holt fill the bill as the
fussy, dressy, expansive spouse of the man whom
Brown isn’t. Mrs. Holt gets many laughs, but
she never identifies herself with the character,
which complaint applies to Bland Holt’s detec-
tive in a less degree. Bland merely manages to
repress himself with difficulty in a part that
would be a soft thing, and effective withal, for a
comedian like Brough, for instance.

George Darrell’s benefit “ send-off,” timed
for Friday afternoon, at Her Majesty’s, Melb., pro-
mises to beabrightand lucrative transaction. Nellie
Stewart, Brownlow, Tapley, Lauri, and co. will
revive the second act of “ Ma Mie Rosette,” which
means a full house, anyhow—thermometer per-
mitting. The Bland Holts supply the droll cot-
tage scene from “Flying Colours ; ” some tit-bits
from “Florodora” are promised; Snazelle,
Rickards and Lottie Collins will take the
floor in turn , and George Darrell, in his original
“Sunny South” capacity, will sport his re-
nowned red flannel shirt and face death unflinch-
ingly, as of yore. Present scribe never looks
at the cable news from S’Africa without thinking
of George. Up to now there has been no re-
corded instance of a man straightening himself
up against a kopje and defying the Boers to blaze
away and be d dto ’em. The ambushed war-
rior appears to surrender every time when called
upon. But perhaps it is because he lacks the in-
spiring influence of a red shirt. Put Darrell into
kharki, and maybe he would prefer life to dis-
nonor, even at the cannon’s mouth.
** * *
Melbourne last week felt some doubt about
the deoth of its grief for the late Queen when the
news of W. G. Barker’s death was published.
The passing-out of the popular basso gave the
city a real sense of personal loss. At one time or
other everybody who goes anywhere had heard
Barker sing, and had remarked favourably on the
difference between his manly, unaffected style
and that of the average vainglorious pro. They
would have liked him to put a little more “ devil ”
into his work, but none the less they admired him
for his fine voice and his grave self-respecting
appearance. He looked a good fellow, and those
who knew the old-young man (he died in his 30th
year) off the platform testify to his amiable quali-
ties. A meeting of sympathisers, fixed for the
day on which he died, was adjourned to Tuesday
of this week. It now only remains for the Mel-
bourne public to express its sentiment of regret
in solid cash for the benefit of Barker’s widow
and child.
** * *
xs ot so manyyears ago a certain young song-
stress was lauded to t he skies by th e Australian press
as a coming prima donna. Perhaps she was never
quite so promising as her admirers thought her,
but anyhow her future looked bright enougn. A
few nights back that same vocalist had a painful
experience in one of the cities where she was for-
merly a favorite. After singing one verse of a
ballad in a weak, tuneless voice whilst the gallery
boys “guyed” her, she got a mysterious mana-
gerial order to come off, and the poor woman
slowly retired from the stage in a dazed condition.
It is an awful thing for one who has known the
rapture of glowing newspaper notices, and floral
tributes from johnnies, to get ordered, and very
properly ordered, to come off.
** * *
That rather humorous little man, Parson
Haweis, died last week. Australia, or some of it,
remembers Haweis as a very passable lecturer
(with a game leg) who talked intelligently about
the strong moral influence of music for good or
evil, its patriotic uses and abuses, and its history
ancient and modern. He illustrated his discourse
with fiddle-scrapes of a peculiarly harsh character,
for which he used to apologise, saying that his
one-time skilful hand had lost its cunning.
Haweis was very emphatic on the point that the
art of music reached its zenith ten years ago,
and certainly nothing has occurred since then to
shatter the little parson’s theory. “We are living
in the golden age of music—and we don’t know
it,” he declared.
** * *
“ Florodora ” is running its eighth lap at
Her Majesty’s (Melb.), and still goes strong. One
or two of the original songs are sometimes dropped
out. One or two others, notably “ I’ve an Ink-
ling ” might as well be replaced by new goods;
but, take it for all in all, the story of Cyrus Gil-
fain, the millionaire, remains an unimpeachable
success. No other musical comedy has turned
the power of journalism to so good an end.
Cyrus is a triumphant and defiant old fraud for
three solid hours, but at the last moment Grace
Palotta comes forward in the winding-sheet of a
Welsh ghost, named Ethelwynda, and tells him
The Bulletin will interview him when he is
dead. That breaks him up. The thought of
getting interviewed by this scurrilous newspaper
m the World Below is more than Cyrus can bear.
He begs for mercy on his evil knees, and leaves
Carrie Moore and the perfumery business to their
fate

Another show that altereth not is Our
Navy under the command of Admiral Snazelle
and Captain Edwards, R.N. The handy men in
blue continue to fall off the greasy pole, heave the
lead, and fire torpedoes at the bounding wave
whilst Snazelle talks and sings. Probably he
warbles better than at first; anyway he now
sounds equal to the fruity basso of ten years ago,
who was always received with gratitude in a
rousing ballad.

The Wild Cat Column.
/ictoria is the only State which has a big
English loan (£3,000,000 at 4 per cent.) falling due this
year. Considering the very bad state of the English
money market the prospect looks woeful enough. It is at
a time like this that Australia begins to realise (1) the
folly of borrowing ; (2) the special folly of borrowing in
England ; (3) the exceeding folly of making loans payable
at any fixed date, so that it may be necessary to borrow
for renewal purposes at exorbitant interest in some time
of panic, instead of fixing the liability for so many years,
and making it redeemable thereafter at the Government’s
option only. In 1899 Victoria had a £1,500,000 loan to
renew. The London market was better then than it is
now, but it wasn’t good, and Victoria got the money at
£3 4s. 4d. per cent, by adding £lOO,OOO to the principal
of the debt. That is to say, its credit was good
enough to enable it to borrow at about £3 7s. 6d. per cent.,
but it borrowed at £3 4s. 4d. and added the rest of
the interest to the permanent debt, and called it dis-
count, and thus paid a good part of the interest out of
borrowed money. An honest Government would have
paid the £lOO,OOO discount by instalments out of revenue,
but Victoria hadn’t an hcmest Government—no Austra-
lian State has.
The £3,000,000 loan now falling due is at 4 per
cent., and if the new loan was floated at par at 3£ per
cent. Victoria would add nothing to its debt, and yet save
£15,000 a year interest. It is about ten chances to one,
however, that it will be a 3 per cent, loan floated at a
heavy loss, and a little more interest will be saved by a
large addition to the total debt, just as usual. And it is
commonly reported that the new loan is to be floated
in the disastrous London market, also as usual.
The money could almost certainly be obtained
a good deal more cheaply in Australia, and
the annual outflow of about £lOO,OOO a year in
interest be thus saved. But though a good many
new loans are floated in Australia, the idea of paying off
an old English loan by borrowing locally, and thus con-
verting a foreign into an Australian liability, is so wildly
novel that none of our stereotyped legislators have yet
attempted it. It would be worth while to stop the out-
flow of £lOO,OOO a year, but it would burst up a foolish
old precedent, and Australian politicians are slaves to
precedent in money matters—to bad precedents, that is.
No good financial precedent ever attracted the slightest
notice in this country.

* * * *
Since the start of the Broken Hill Proprietary’s
new 5000-ton mill, most of the Barrier mines have been
engaged in remodelling their concentrating plants on up-
to-date lines, and wisely show a preference for machines
which have proved most successful on the difficult fines
and slimes. The machines that can claim this distinction
are the “ Luhrig ” Yanners, as supplied by Messrs. W. &
J. Lempriere, of Melbourne. The South’s new mill will
include a dozen or so, and the Junction Co. has also de-
cided to erect a number of Luhrig Vanners as an impor-
tant adjunct to its large plant. Other mines would do
well to follow this good example.*

&c., of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, reports that the

works, and he is now in a position to supply orders at

inches in diameter, with either patent cast-iron bolted
joints, flanged joints or the ordinary spigot and faucet
lead joints. Mr. Ferguson also manufactures boilers,
pumps, fluming, patent Nelyambo earth-scoops or tank-
cleaners, rabbit exterminators, refrigerators, ice-making
machinery, bolts, nuts, forgings, and all sorts of iron-work. *

Editor Bulletin.— ln your issue 5/l/’Ol a writer
observes that if Mt. Morgan g.m. co., instead of a 7d.
div. had paid a 6d. div., the saved Id. per share would
have amounted to £4166 13s. 4d., which would have kept
1200 of its workers for 10 days at full pay. Now the co.
has done all in its power to keep the men on as long as
possible. Men have been sent to all places where water
was lodged, where they have blasted and trenched. The
water has been used over and over again until it has be-
come so highly mineralised that it is impossible to use it
without stopping the engines. Every effort has been
made to keep the works going. The writer quoted im-
plies that all the hands had lost their billets ; whereas
out of a total number of 1600 (average for the year) onlj
873 have been suspended from work. Again, he alludei
to the co. having contributed towards the equipment o'
men for the Transvaal. It is not money, however, buj
water that the mine is in need of, and if water could be
bought the necessary coin would be forthcoming.— Gamma.

Mother o’ Gold (Vic.) : Owned in London,
where the shareholders have great expectations. The
gold-yields have so far been very small. Described by
the chairman as a parent exploration co. holding some-
thing like 1600 acres, all gold-bearing, in the heart of a
district which was accounted as one of the richest in Vic-
toria. But that was many years ago, and like most other
things in this world gold-mines are not inexhaustible.
There have been many mother o’ gold mines without any
offspring but mullock, though the writer never heard of
any mine being frankly called Mother o’ Mullock.

There is also an item described as “ Commission Reserve
account, £7830,” and if it is included the total reserves are
£19,167. The last half-year’s profits vvould have been
much larger, but for the extra expenditure of £575 for
extension of premises. The Perpetual Trustee Co. has
now charge of estates amounting to £3,928,521. At the
end of 1891 the properties in its care amounted to
£891,426, so the business has multiplied fivefold in nine
years. And as security for its clients the co. has total
reserves of £19,167 ; paid-up capital, £25,000 ; and un-
called capital, £975,000. No private trustee offers one-
tenth as good security as this for the honest administra-
tion of the estates in his charge, and it is unaccountable
that anybody should be so mad as to leave the assets of
his widow and orphans in charge of the “ gent one,” who
so often leaves unaccountably with the money, when
companies like this are available. A very_ large part of
the Perpetual Trustee Co.’s business consists of estates
transferred from private trustees. And it is a notable
fact that a very large proportion of the estates of this
class offered have to be declined because the private
trustee has, by neglect or fraud, brought them into such
a state of chaos that it isn’t good enough to take up the
business. Another notable fact is that it finds the bulk
of its new business comes to it through the recommenda-
tion of old clients whose good opinion is based on their
own experience.

* * • *
The Mutual Life Association celebrates the new
century by introducing a most useful form of new policy
in which ordinary life assurance and accident assurance
are combined. Under this system a man aged 30 can take
out a policy for £lOOO, which not only guarantees his heirs
£lOOO and bonuses at death, but also ensures the payment
of £lOOO to himself if he meets with permanent total dis-
ablement by accident; £5OO for permanent partial dis-
ablement by accident; and £5 per week during tem-
porary total disablement. And the price of this lot, for
a healthy man aged 30, is only £26 10s. per annum, with
, sound office and about £1,500,000 of assets as a guarantee
of prompt payment.

The co. has written down goodwill to £lOOO, and proposes

opinion that it has £490 undivided profit. The present
writer holds that goodwill, as an item that yields no
revenue, and which is never to be found when it is
wanted (i.e. when the co. has burst and the assets are
being realised) is not an asset in any sense at all, and
should be written off to the last copper. Whichever way
this matter is looked at, however, the New York and
Brooklyn Tobacco 00. appears to be making good pro-
gress under N.S.W.’s Freetrade system of protecting the
tobacco industry.

Some recent figures relating to the North Coast
Steam Navigation 00. (Sydney) : ,
.. . Total
Profits. Dividends. Reserves.
June, 1899 .. .. ■ •
Dec., 1899 ..£7201 .. 8 per cent. = £5250 .. 25,769
June, 1900 .. 6244 ..8 „ = 5250 .. 26,763
Dec., 1900 .. 6254 ..8 „ = 5250 .. 27,767
The reserves abovementioned consist of insurance fund,
£17,498, and reserve for equalisation of dividends,
£10,269. As regards the latter it would be much better
to drop the painful and invidious subject of equalising
dividends, and call the thing plain reserve. If ever a co.
gets into such a bad way that it can only keep up its
dividends by drawing on dividend-equalisation fund it
has generally also got to such a pass that the
dividend equalisation fund is all wanted to
cover depreciation of assets. The profits shown
above are considerably larger than those openly
announced in the co.’s balance-sheet. In addition to the
profits shown by the co., the insurance reserve, and occa-
sionally the dividend-equalisation fund as well, have a
habit of growing by their own volition, presumably by
the operation of the law of compound interest; and these
increments are included above, in order to show the pro-
gress of the total reserves each year. And taking all
these things into account the progress is very satisfactory
indeed.
* * * *
Once the B.H. Prop directors gave shareholders
a pretty fair amount of information about their property,
but latterly they have developed a large capacity for say-
ing a few things that mean little and leaving altogether un-
said the rest. There never was so much kicking about it,
though, as during the last few weeks ; with the result
that at the half-yearly meeting Chairman Harvey
Patterson ’umbly dealt out a supplementary lot of in-
formation. Among other things he told how much ore
had been opened up- said there was 4,568,000 tons above
the 650 ft level, with exploration still in progress. After
all, though, this doesn’t mean so much as shareholders
seemed to think. For what is the value of this ore ?
There probably isn’t a single mine at Broken Hill whose
ore-value has slumped so shockingly as B.H. Prop’s ; and
the question is how far the slump is going.
Take the year 1898 (Nov. ’97 to Nov. ’9B) and
compare it with 1900—two periods with only a year in
between. In ’9B the total quantity of ore treated was
408,798 ; in ’OO it was 525,938 tons. In ’9B the (approxi-
mate) average price of silver was 2s. 3d. and of lead
£l3 7s. 6d. ; in ’OO it was 2s. 4d. for one and
£l6 7s. 6d. for the other—a vast difference, when one
remembers that the annual production of silver is
4,500,0000 z., and of lead 40,000 tons. Yet in ’BB the net
profits amounted to £262,016 ; in ’OO they were £223,034.
In the latter year the co. used up 30 per.cent, more ore ;
and got over £125,000 more for its product; and yet
net profits have fallen £40,000. And this has been in the
teeth, too, of reports of repeated improvements in the
methods of mining and ore-treatment. Writer knows of
nothing that could account for this but an awful slump
in the value of the stuff handled —not altogether a slump
in the metallic contents, but partly in the ease with
which the stuff may be treated. As to the average
metallic value of the ore, the directors have knocked off
giving figures ; what are given are figures purporting to
show’“ the average extraction per ton of ore,” but really
showing the extraction per ton of material smelted, most
of which is concentrates ; and as it takes six or seven tons
of ore to make one of concentrates, it is clear that, so far
as showing the real value of the ore goes, these figures
are several degrees worse than useless. It is certain that
either the co. is now working abnormally poor stuff or
that formerly it worked abnormally good stuff ; and the
question that ought to be settled is whether it wouldn’t
be a great deal better to do as the Sulphide Corporation
is doing—so mix the ores, that uniform value can be main-
tained over a long term of years. If that is not done any
estimates of the quantity of ore in the mine, unless
accompanied by statements of value, are of very little
account.

For some time past it has been darkly hinted
that the B.H. Prop, directors had an astonishing cat in a
bag somewhere ; and the chairman showed an ear of the
animal at the half-yearly meeting. Having got the Iron

animal’s ear.

The present North 00. was formed five years
ago with a capital of £lOO,OOO, of which only £lO,OOO was
called-up. The mine (which had been idle for years) lost
over £7OOO. The co. had to clean out the workings, pro-
vide plant, and put up a mill out of earnings ; and those
earnings had to be made out of sulphide ore carrying
about 9oz. silver and 20 per cent. lead. It has paid over
£20,000 for plant, it has done a good deal of development
work, and its divs. have totalled £17,500 so there are
not many silver-mines can show a better record. Wisely
it kept its profits in its stocking for three years, and used
them to buy plant; but now it has got to buy more
plant, and it hasn’t anything to speak of in the stock-
ing. It must not violently surprise shareholders there-
fore if divs have to be reduced for a while. The r: "e
will probably suffer if this isn’t done ; for more deveio >-
ment work, and mullock passes, &c., are needed. The
Junction is an awful example of the madness of attempt-
ing to pay divs. too fast. The co. has got, so far as
exposed, a very fair body of some of the most easily T-milled
ore at Broken Hill; and it will have to be careful not to
spoil the whole show.
r , • * *
Except where a silver-lead co. has a large
variety of ores at command it is doubtful whether it ever
ought to do its own smelting. It paid even the Broken
Hill Prop. 00. last half-year to sell concentrates rather
than smelt them. In the light of that, what must be the
position of Block 14 ? It has long been suspected that
the bigger profits of the British, Block 14 s poor neighbor,
are due to it selling its product and not attempting to
either roast or smelt. Block 14’s published figures are
not ample enough to allow one to form a definite opinion,
but if the directors don’t feel like going into the matter,
a committee of shareholders very well might.

Sulphide Corporation: “Shares sold down to

• * * *
Turon River dredge suffering now from want of
water. Before long, however, we are told, the dredges
will work better ground, and then there is going to be a
huge “ scramble for the shares at high prices. The
knowing* ones are picking them up now, and they are
going to make big profits.” Buyer, 10s. ; sellers, 17s. 6d.
No business —margin too wide. A div. whispered two
months ago, but none yet declared, and no excuse given
for advising the purchase of scrip when shares were four
times their present price. To-day the Turon has a higher
valuation than any other N.S.W. dredging co., in face of
the fact that it has been dredging more than a year vvith-
out a div., and its returns are as low as any, and less than
one-third those of the Macquarie No. 1, which has about
half its market valuation. Inconsistencies of this kind
are by no means confined to the Turon dredges ; they
arise from want of discrimination and general public
ignorance, which do more to keep gold-mines going than
the gold got out of them.
Lucknow Consols (N.S.W.) : This came out in
London just before Xmas ; promoted by the Associated
Financial Corporation, the head, shoulders, legs and
everything else of which are controlled by Horatio Bot-
tomiey. The prospectus contained reports from nine
different experts, and the Mayor of Orange was quoted as
an authority on the mineral wealth of the property. The
co. proposed to acquire the “ Lucknow Canoblas ” mines
and “ Lucknow Consols.” A lot more seems to be known
about these mines in London than in N.S.W. They were
being sold for a modest £600,000. and no doubt the pro-
moters will be able to make good use of the money—when
they get it.
* * * •
The “Lloyd” Copper Co. (N.S.W.): Annual
feneral meeting of shareholders in London in Dec.
ales of copper, 650 tons, realising nearly £50,000. Over
70 tons of copper unsold. Ore, matte, and copper on
hand, upwards of £lO,OOO. Considerable delay and diffi-
culty had been experienced in obtaining the services of
a capable metallurgist—who had condemned the plant as
unsuitable, and his opinion had been confirmed by
Schlapp. All the reports agreed that the mine was de-
veloping splendidly. Schlapp’s estimate of ore in sight
and on surface : 150,000 tons, of the gross value of
£643,500. Deducting his estimate of cost of production,
£35 per ton, left £328,500 profit in sight. The lode, Bft.
wide, showed splendid ore at the greatest depth, and had
been described as a true fissure lode, undoubtedly per-
manent. Negotiations pending to borrow £50,000 to put up
the new pi ant recommended by the metallurgist. The chair-
man said there seemed to be some extraordinary fatality
about mines, but especially about copper mines, that they
started by erecting unsuitable plants. There are some
questions certainly unanswered in this report. Has the
proposed system of hot-blast smelting been proved use-
less by working, or is it condemned without ? Did not
the previous manager satisfy himself that the ore could
be smelted on the mine without expensive fluxing, and
were not 150 tons of it put through the Blayney furnace
without difficulty? It was never contended that the
Burraga ore was a pyritic - smelting proposition, but
experience elsewhere has shown that it does not need a
high percentage of sulphide to make hot-blast furnacing
economical and effective ; in fact, pyritic smelting, in a
modified degree, is so young and experimental an indus-
try that to erect an enormous smelting plant such as
they have on this mine, then to condemn it without a
trial and to spend £50,000 on another, is certainly a most
extraordinary proceeding.
* » • *
“ Jimmy S.”: The greater part of the miners
and wheelers in Newcastle (N.S.W.) district belong to the
Colliery Employes Federation, and yet at nearly every
pit after the holidays (when the miners refused to start
work until aware of the terms they were working under),
while the miners had deputations appointed by the
various lodges to arrange their grievances, the wheelers
had to resort to laying the pit idle on the following day
before they got their rise. Owing to there being no
shiftmen’s union in this district the prices paid for shift-
work vary from 6s. to 6s. 6d., 7s. and 7s. 6d. There are
some shiftmen in the C.E.F., but until the others join in
a body they may as well throw away their subscriptions.
The above prices are the same as paid for shift-work be-
fore the miners, wheelers, drivers and trappers got their
rise. Hope this will be an eye-opener to them.
* * * *
Blayney Copper : Smelting stopped and not
likely to be resumed for some months; meanwhile the
300 ft. level will be well opened up and main shaft sunk
another 100 ft. when the lode should be into it or near it,
and the surrounding squatters from Blayney to Carcoar
will have a show of proving whether their sheep have
been killed by copper and sulphur fumes or have died
from natural causes. No less than five actions are
threatened and heavy damages are claimed by the
squatters, unmindful of the fact that smelting went on for
a year without complaint, that no action was taken when
the furnaces worked on the mine years ago, and that
nothing is said against copper-smelters in other places.
However, if they mean business, the question will be
thoroughly fought, for if the squatters succeed they must
kill an industry which employs more men than some
Blayney squatters have sheep.

A color-sergeant of Tasin uiian Volunteers
has written to Hobart Mercury complaining
that, though he had passed the necessary exam.,
the commandant had refused to recommend him
for a commission, “because his social standing
was not good enough,” as he, the commandant”
wanted for officers only men who were in a’
position to visit at Government House.” Aus-
tralasian State Governments should promptly
strangle the growing tendency to restrict the
officering of their troops—British fashion—to
young men of the “classes.” The conditions of
modern warfare make it more and more
evident that' what is wanted for an officer is
srniply and absolutely the best man in the field
—whether he eats with his knife and drops his
‘ h’s,” or says “ Haw ! ” and ignores g-final. One
phuu lesson of the Boer war is that the dude-
officer is a hideously expensive mistake ; and every
man entering the military service of this allegedly
democratic country should do so with the know-
ledge that nothing but personal unfitness can
stand between him and a commission.
• * * *
A London paper states that the granting
of British army commissions to Australians and
Canadians is not always so blessed a privilege as.
might be supposed—for the Australians and
Canadians, that is. Very often his British fellow-
officer flatly declines to speak to the objectionable
“colonial” if he happens to have been a manual-
worker or business man and sprung from the
ranks ; the whole British crowd resents the advent
of the inferior animal, and he becomes a sort of
Invisible Man whom everybody professes not to
see. Years ago, when the purchase of commis-
sions was abolished and promotion by merit was.
supposed to be established in the British army, a.
haughty crowd of British officers was shocked by
the advent among them of a certain Lieutenant
Tribe, the first inferior being who had made his
way up by his own personal ability. He
was persecuted so brutally and so re-
morselessly by the high-toned gentlemen and
fellow-officers of that period that he either
committed suicide or deserted—anyhow he disap-
peared after a few months of misery. Since then
the British officer has grown partly reconciled to
the man who has made his way up from the ranks.
A NEW TERROR.
What we may expect as a result of the visit of
the Imperial troops.
but apparently the advent of the beastly colonial
is a fresh shock to his dignity, and he has got to
get reconciled over again. Also, the wife of the
superiah British officer has never become recon-
ciled to the “ranker/’ or anybody of that sort,
and even if she condescends to nod haughtily to the
ranker, she is never able to see the ranker’s wife.
The life, therefore, of the ranker, whether he be
British or “ colonial,” is calculated to be a little
liell-upon-earth.
* * * •
Forty sermons upon the death of Queen
Victoria were preached in Sydney churches on
Sunday, January 27, but none of the preachers
could find a text from any of the Gospels upon
which to base his discourse. Old Testament
prophets and New Testament commentators were
drawn upon abundantly, Prov. xxviii., 31, doing
duty four times, and Rev. ii., 10, thrice. Two
inferences are allowable. The first is that the
Modern Churchmen pay little attention to what
the Founder of Christianity had to say for himself,
and the second is that he took verv little interest
in Monarchy. Sacerdotalism is "killing Chris-
tianity, which would get on very well if the
priest would let it alone.

From Edward VII. to Edward VI. is afar
cry, but the new King’s title is a reminder of the
schools founded by the previous Edward, and as
an unintentional illustration of the jobbery asso-

we find Australian newspapers mentioning that
the late Archbishop of Canterbury got a free
education at one of them. The billet of Primate
is only possible to men who have had influential
friends from the start. The “Bluecoat” and
other schools which Edward VI. designed for the
poor are now run by the English middle-class
batman for the benefit of his nominees. They
ought to be incorporated with the modern State
School system, and thrown open to the children
of the masses, but class influence bars the way to
reform.

INNOCENCE.
HIS MOTHER (to Willie, aye 4, who has just re-
turned from a visit to the Jones's): “ Where
have you been all the afternoon, my boy?"
WILLIE: “Down to the Jones's baths watching a
lot of children bathing.”
HIS MOTHER : “ Were they boys or girls?”
Willie; “I can't say, ma. They didn't have
any clothes on.”

Hereby the wily draper got the public in such a
tight place that the only way it could absolutely
prove its loyalty was by buying a great quantity
of clothes at his shop on Monday whether it
wanted them or not. If it didn’t buy a lot of
clothes at his shop on Monday there always re-
mained the suspicion that it had bought clothes
from a pro-Boer on Saturday. So the drapery
man’s big advt. trots respectfully behind the hearse
of his Queen, _ and intrudes at the tail of her
funeral; and his seven-and-sixpenny shirt becomes
somehow identified with the expansion of Greater
Britain ; and his eighteenpenny double-breasted
linen collar, warranted not to bag at the knees, is
in some sense a bulwark of the Empire ; likewise
his special, high-heeled bell-topper, at a guinea,
which buttons down the back. The soul of the
respectful and loyal draper followed his sovereign’s
funeral cortege on Saturday, but his body opened
on Monday as usual with a keen eye to business.

churches. Also, while the traders were supposed
to shut shop on Saturday many public-houses were
m full blast.

every distance—from Ift. to 20.

During Queen Victoria's official mourning-
week S.M. Herald mourned only once with
black column-rules—on the day of the funeral;
the Telegraph mourned all the time. The
Herald mourned and was resigned ; the Tele-
graph mourned in hope. The Herald, by the
way, has got its title; the Telegraph’s title is
still to come.
* * * *
One reform following the abolition of the
old useless Sydney Transit Commission has come
in quietly, but effectively. No through heavy
traffic is now allowed in Pitt and George-streets,
nor in the cross streets south of Bridge-street.
For years this reform has been advocated, but its
fulfilment was declared impossible by the old
governing body. But it has been done simply
enough, to the evident relief of the congested
streets of Sydney. Big wool-drays, lorries with
hides and tallow, furniture-vans and the like no
longer block Pitt-street, and, with the dwindling
’bus traffic, that and other thoroughfares are now
comparatively safe.
The shadow of a great woe fell upon a re-
„ ' cent wedding at Ballarat, and what
should have been an occasion of delirious
happiness was converted into a bitter sea-
son indeed. The sun shone on the beauti-
ful bride, and her dress was all that mor-
tal, woman could unreasonably desire,
consequently she was clothed in joy right
up to the moment when she declared she
would take that man to be her wedded hus-
band. Then suddenly a great sob sounded
from a side pew near the middle of the
church. It was followed by further sobs
and gasps, and the familiar snip's and
snuffles of a weeping female. All eyes turned
upon the pew, and discovered a young
woman in black, watering the matting
with her tears, and throwing out symp-
toms of heartrending grief. Instantly those
eyes travelled from the distracted young
thing in the pew to the quailing bride-
groom, and they were obviously—everybody
could see it—filled with
accusations and in-
sinuations of con-
duct unbecoming
a gentleman. The
bride turned
Sale, and finished
le ceremony in
tears, which de-
veloped into hys-
terics as they bore
her along the
aisle. Arrived
at the carriage,
s h e heaped
reproaches
and insults
upon the
hot, red
bridegroom,
and then flop-
ped into hys-
terics again, and
it was a distracted
and dismal man
who bore away a
damp, jigging,
dishevelled bride
from amongst her
icy relatives,
and away from
a silent and re-
proachful crowd
of respectable
residents. Mean-
while, a gentle
soul had interviewed the weeping female in-
side, and found that she was howling because
she always made a point of howling at wed-
dings, which somehow reminded her of various
dead husbands of hers. This explanation was
subsequently conveyed to the bride and bride-
groom, but too late to save the honeymoon from
being a failure —all wailings, accusations, a. d
gnashings of teeth.
A woman unexpectedly, died in an Aus-
tralian hospital, the other day. A post-mortem
was ordered and it was found that all organs (in-
cluding the heart), which are ordinarily on the
left, were on the right side, and all the right
organs were on the left. The case is extraordinary,
but by no means unique.
* * » *
The other day a scared woman who had
been treated as a casualty patient attended a
Sydney Hospital and anxiously asked the doctor
what was the nature of the liniment prescribed
for her. She breathlessly went on to say “Me
and my sister have had an awful row and out of
spite she’s gone and swallowed all the medicine
you gave me, and now she’s awful ill.” The sister
who “ got the needle ” is now lying on her back in
one of the wards, and the linimentless sister daily
brings her flowers.

On his return to Maoriland, a local
contingenter was brought up for failing to
maintain his wife, married to him just before he
left for S’Africa. He pleaded that he was subject
to epileptic fits, and, if he had married the lady,
he certainly had no recollection of doing so.
Also that he was only 19, although his age was
stated in the register as 21. His spouse owned up
to being 32. She had only known hubby for a
fortnight before marriage, and had a baby three
months afterwards. According to her own story
she had explained her position to him, but as he
remembered nothing about it, he was probably in
an epileptic fit during the explanation. As soon as
the Court rose, the returned warrior went off to
the brigade office and volunteered, in his capacity
as an acknowledged epileptic patient, for the
sixth contingent.
* * * *
There is trouble in the Maoriland district
of Rotorua. Hitherto visitors who called there
to “take the waters” were not allowed to wear
bathing dresses. They trotted down to their par-
ticular dip, slipped off their garments, and went
into the water as they came into the world. Now
the verra gude have risen to complain that this is
Wrong, and to bathe in the Lake District baths
without proper bathing costume is to commit a
misdemeanour, and get fined by the blushful
law. The people who run the baths have
threatened insurrection and the creation of a
Lakes District Republic. The advent of the
bathing dress means a great financial disaster, as
a large proportion of the people who seek to be
healed at Rotorua and the other Hot Lakes are
sufferers from skin-diseases. There are special
baths reserved for them, and they are not allowed
to frequent those in which the clean-skinned
sport. But the bathing-dress will cover a multi-
tude of things, and it will be difficult to compel
all people to strip for examination before allowing
them to take the morning or evening dip; and
once the idea spreads that tourists with skin-
diseases are using the ordinary baths there will be
a large slump. Rotorua is a place where every-
body wonders’ what is the matter with his or her
neighbour, and the habit of bathing in the “nood ”
is a guarantee of good faith.
* * • *
Melbourne papers turned on the gush-tap
when describing the religious ceremony at the
Exhibition the other Sunday. The Age recorded
that a deep sigh shook the manly frames of the
parti-colored warriors assembled there when
reference was made to the Queen’s death. Which
was a mere reportorial invention'. All the
Imperial Tommies seemed to be concerned about
was the admiration with which they were gazed
upon by the giggling girls. There wasn’t a sigh
or a weep, but plenty of wink and leer. The Age
reporter, bathed in his professional tears, took a
humid view of what was, after all, a very un-
impressive spectacle. Except for the aureole
struck by the sun out of Waxellency Madden’s
bald pate, there was no dignity in the business.
* * * *
The Melbourne press conspired to disguise
the truth re public opinion of the Imperial
Tommies in comparison with .local troops —Per-
manent Artillery and others. The “ splendid
physique” and “machine-like swing” of the
visitors didn’t “win universal admiration” as
alleged by newspaper mis-reporters. Expressions
of disappointment mingled with friendly excuses
were heard on all sides. The marching of the
dismounted cavalrymen was uneven and straddle-
some, especially on the journey up to the
Exhibition when they hadn’t even a drum-beat
to assist them, and as for the line-regimenters,
with their almost limping action of the right leg,
they were less impressive than the sturdier-built
and freer-actioned lot of Yictoiians. Returning
from the Exhibition to a band accompaniment the
Britishers marched better, though not, perhaps,
so smartly as the Indians. The immediate result
of the display was to increase Melbourne’s respect
for “colonial” soldiery at the expense of its old
belief in Tommy Atkins, which was presumably
not the result intended by the local authorities.
* * * *
At one of the Melbourne Courts the other
day a wife declared that she was ready to go back
to her husband if he would turn away the lodger.
She had been married four months, her liege lord
had “ill-treated” her (she said), but she thought
she could live happily with him if they had the
house to themselves, “her objection to the lodger
being that he had struck her.” This must be
about the first instance on record of a lady calling
her husband a brute whilst still further objecting
to the lodger. But she was a Jewess. Perhaps
that makes a difference. In Christian circles it is
ever the paying guest who offers consolation.
Most women are more or less “struck” by the
lodger.
Concerning a parsonical row :
The Sydney ecclesiastical-precedence controversy is
small potatoes in a certain little far-northern town com-
pared with the local religious rumpus. The local church
is built beside the only public hall, which latter is used
by the other clergymen on Sunday. Both are weather-
board buildings and, as voices in either are quite audible
in the other, the clergymen amicably arranged the
time of their respective services so that they should not
clash. But there came to the church a new parson, who
fixed his time of service regardless of those next door.
So now the congregations in both buildings enjoy at one
sitting the saving grace of a double service, and the
rival clerics display great ingenuity in fixing on hymns
which will make the most noise at the most impressive
parts of the other fellow’s sermon. One of the
ministers has backed out of the contest, but the other
has taken up the gauntlet, and he and his opponent are
vigorously reinforcing their respective choirs with lusty-
lunged recruits. The ungodly have taken to attending
the services in the hope of still more interesting develop-
ments.
Salomons, Q.C. (or K.C., or whatever it
is), sets at rest, as far as any lawyer’s opinion can,
the fear that the Truck Act lately passed in
N.S.W. prohibits employers making authorised
deductions from employes’ wages for the upkeep
of semi-public hospitals, &c. Salomons says that
an employe can as well authorise a hospital secre-
tary to collect a portion of his pay as he can his
wife.
* * * *
The celebration at Ebenezer, on the
Hawkesbury, the other day of the 99th anniver-
sary of the establishment of Presbyterianism in
Australia recalls that the late Sydney Burdekin
came to possess all that part of the Hawkesbury
on which the church is situated. Mrs. Burde-
kin is as generous as her late husband in
all that concerns the historic structure. The
clever Sydney police of the early days arrested
the whole congregation one Sunday, under the
impression that they were Irish rebels planning
an insurrection. In 1802 and thereabouts the
dominating fear of the little settlement was that
Irish rebels would grab Australia.
* * * •
A Williamstown (Vic.) Bench displayed
unusual common sense the other day, when,
instead of at once gaoling a man who had stolen
£4 10s., it adjourned the case for three days to
give him a chance to make restitution. But there
should be some guarantee that the offender makes
restitution himself, instead of bullying others into
doing so.

• • * *
Dear Bulletin. — Re the approaching
census, let me point out that the returns, as
usually sent in, entirely misrepresent—under the
head of “Religion”—the real state of the case,
and that the various churches are thus enabled to
claim as adherents thousands of persons who,
either through lack of courage or through pure
indifference, allow themselves to be enrolled
under one or other of the denominations. Perhaps
a note from you might stiffen up some of those who
(like myself) are creedless, honestly so to record
themselves, and thus help to show that the various
sects have not upon this new country anything
like the hold they lay claim to. —F.W.M. [The
utter futility of the returns in this respect is
further shown by the fact that in every case of
omission to specify religious belief the gentle
N.S.W. compilers have been in the habit of calmly
filling-in the hiatus with “ Church of England.”
Also, in Maoriland all persons who describe them-
selves as “Freethinker,” “Agnostic,” and the
like, are tabulated as having “ refused to specify
religion.” —Ed. B.]

A Melbourne aboriginal, lately
in trouble for treating his white wife
much as his fathers used to treat their
gins, is reckoned about the smartest wheat-
humper in the city, and can, if he likes, earn his
£3 a week.
The Coninghaui case is to be tried again
on 11th March, but what Judge will preside, or
who petitioner’s counsel will be, is so far un-
known. In legal circles it appears to be thought
that either Mr. Justice Owen or Mr. Justice
Cohen will have the hearing of the case. Last'
Friday this circular was published in Sydney :
Chambers, 93 Eiizabeth-street,
Sydney, Ist February, 1901.
CONINGHAM v. CONINGHAM.
Dear Sir,- I respectfully beg to draw your attention to
the fact that the above-mentioned divorce suit, which re-
sulted so unsatisfactorily after a protracted hearing in
December last, has been set down, on the petitioner’s
own application in person, for re-trial on Monday, the
11th of March proximo.
I venture to point out that this matter has, unfor-
tunately, given rise to sectarian feeling of the bitterest
character, and that the petitioner is wholly unahle to
provide the necessary funds for the due prosecution of
his suit.
He is placed in a still more awkward position by having
to fight, single-handed, against the combined forces of
the entire Roman Catholic Church, and the array of legal
ability which its unlimited resources are able to provide.
It. behoves every Protestant who wishes to see THE
ROMAN CHURCH HUMILIATED to assist in seeing
justice done between the parties upon equal terms, by
subscribing liberally towards the Petitioner’s Relief Fund,
so as to enable him to secure the services of legal
gentlemen and eminent counsel in his forthcoming trial.
Any amount, even pence from the poor Protestants,
however small, will be gratefully received, and, in
appealing to you for your generous assistance, I beg to
point out that no donor’s name will be divulged (in or out
of court), and every subscription will be duly ac-
knowledged by Mr. Arthur Coningham himself.
Thanking you in anticipation of your early response, I
am, yours faithfully, Percival R. Waddy, Barrister-at-
Law, Hon. Sec. Coningham Relief Fund.
Mr. Waddy has since denied having had any
hand in the foregoing production, the two italicised
portions of which (the italics are The Bulletin’s)
would hardly have been put into one and
the same document by any sane legal practi-
tioner. Mr. Waddy states that as the committee
of the Coningham fund would not allow their
names to be published, he took the bull by the
horns and signed a circular which was drawn up
appealing for subscriptions in aid of the peti-
tioner ; but the circular in question, he declares,
in no way corresponds with that issued to the pub-
lic. In a word, Mr. Waddy appears to suggest that
the published circular was a ’‘trick of war.” He
goes on to say that, having been worried out of
his life since his acceptance of the secretaryship
of the Coningham fund, and having been the
recipient of several threats of personal violence,
he has resigned the position, the exciting atmo-
sphere of which (and three glasses of stout)
brought about a certain little police-court affair,
in which he figured on Saturday morning. It is
to be hoped that the alleged appending of Mr.
Waddy’s name to a document he never signed will
be investigated with the severity demanded by
the occasion.
• * * *
A Collingwood (Vic.) small boy, anxious
to get his name into an Evenin’ Herral heading,
gathered up a second suit of clothes, deposited it
on one side of the Yarra, crossed to the other, and,
having undressed, swam the river, put on his
second suit, and “ mysteriously disappeared.”
When the left clothing was found there was weep-
ing and wailing, and the police settled down to a
long dragging job. But just when the business
was getting wearisome the youngster turned up at
his own home, sick of fame, and ravening for
bread and jam.

THE LODGER
FOR THE BULLETIN.
T am not wot they calls a miz-
zenthrope,
I do not ’ate me sober fellow-
man,
I pities ’im, an’ ’elps ’im all I
can,
For I’ve been sober, too—w’en
I’ve been broke.
An’ yet, just now, I do feel most
inclined
To view my fellow-man with
deep disgust;
An I might go upon a record
bust—
If Jane ’adn’t taken all my cash
to mind.
W’en she first snared me I was
very young,
An’ thought as ’ow I’d ’old the
purse an’ rule ;
But now I know I was a san-
guine fool—
I ’ave to ’old the kid and ’old me
tongue.
An’ as I sucks me empty pipe
alone,
There is a weight sits ’eavy on
me chest ;
For wot the missus calls a
“ payin’ guest ”
’As cast a shadow o’er me ’appy
’ome.
An’ ever since the fatal night ’»
came
Jane ’asn’t ’ad no time to wait on me,
W’en I come ’ome from work, an’ want me tea,
She’s waitin’ on the toff who’s jumped me claim.
Jane sez ’e’s invalided ’ome from war,
Through fightin’ w’en ’is kharki socks was damp.
(I know Hyde Park is not an ’ealthy camp ;
But w’en I mention it, Jane shrieks “ pro-Boer ! ”)
’E’s commandeered me fav’rite easy-chair.
An’ puts ’is feet up on me mantel-piece.
Me parlor cushions fairly shine with grease—
From wot Jane calls ’is “ millingtary air.”
I never dared to smoke “ no stinkin’ pipes”
Among the parlor curtains Jane adores ;
But this ’ere bounder—wot me soul abhorrs—
May smoke about the ’ouse just w’ere ’e likes.
Meanwhile I goes about me daily jobs,
An’ takes the kid on Sundays for a blow.
*E don’t talk like a book, an’ yet I know
’E don’t despise me ’cos I ain’t Lord “ Bobs.”
I know the missus loves me—in ’er ’eart,
Altho’ she’s got the kharki fever now ;
But, all the same, there’s goin’ to be a row,
An’ I’m in train in! to perform my part.
I’m patriotic—in an ’umble way—
An’ sings Gord save the Queen—an’ all ’er loot;
But, tho’ it seems disloyal, I’m bound to boot
That kharki “ payin’ guest” who doesn’t PAY.
TAS.
CRIPES CLARK.
THE SHIP.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Her way is through the trackless deeps
Of God’s mysterious sea;
The force that moves her never sleeps,
But works all silently.
She keeps her course in calm serene ;
No storm shall do her shame ;
Yet no man hath her Captain seen,
Or knows her Pilot’s name.
Her passengers, for weal or woe,
Her decks by myriads throng;
But many more sleep down below,
And will not wake for long.
The poorest passenger on board,
The man without a friend,
He keeps his place with prince or lord,
Until the voyage end.
She sails unto a Port unknown,
If any Port there be,
Yet goes not on her way alone
On that mysterious sea.
We see them in the purple dark,
And in the moonlight pale,
The thousand thousand lights that mark
Where other vessels sail.
And so, come sorrow or come mirth,
On deck, or down below,
We sail in our good ship, the Earth,
As far as she shall go.
N.S.W.
V. J. DALEY.

The value of the estate in N.-S.W. of the
late 0. C. Skarratt (a big shareholder in Mt.
Morgan) has been sworn at £378,500. Mr.
Skarratt, who lately died in England, was well
known in Sydney years ago as a sportsman and as
owner of the Royal Hotel. The estate goes to
trustees for the benefit of deceased’s wife and
their eight children—F. C. Skarratt, Charles
Sydney Skarratt, Mary Eller Bridge (wife of
C. E. Bridge), Emily Charlotte M'Quade (wife
of Fred. M Quade), Lucy Ann Theobald (wife of
C. H. G-. E. Theobald), A. C. Keigwin (wife of
1. H. Keigwin), Violet Skarratt, and Daisy
OKarratt.
