A WOMAN’S LETTER.
„ „ Sydney, February 5, 1901.
Mr Dear Myee.—
A week of Dead Marches—Chopin, Beethoven
Handel. Music by day and night. The heavenly
melody of Chopin’s Marche Futimbre singing itself
in our churches, in our halls, and under the open
sky, made the week of mourning blossom like a
rose.
By the way, I beg leave to disagree with
Shelley. He calls the tuberose the sweetest
flower for scent that blows, but nothing could be
sicklier than tuberose mixed with the odours of
the crowd —as on Saturday,, when the wind stirred
the heavy white wreaths on the Statue.
I thought the Funeral Service at St. Andrew’s
lacking in dignity and poetry. At ten o’clock the
vergers closed the doors against the crowd, and there
was one time when a loud-voiced official shouted
through the keyhole to “ Keep back. You haven’t
a ticket, so you can’t get in ! ” The decorations
of the Cathedral were the stiffest in the city,
drapes held up at regular intervals with little hard
bunches of immortelles.
It was meet that the organ should lend the only
note of beauty. Mr. Massey sent Chopin’s
Nocturne of Death, Chopin s March Funebre,
through the aisles. At the last great death
Chopin’s music was unknown to the people, but
Victoria’s passing has made its golden melody
familiar to everyone.
This service was Lady Hopetoun’s first appear-
ance in public. She was looking remarkably well,
not a trace of the invalid about her. She wore deep
mourning—but though deep, becoming—a black
crepe-de-chine gown, with much fine black laci-
ness about the bodice, and a big black hat and
veil. His Ex. looked white and casual in black
clothes with a black hat, and a green band across
his chest. Mrs. Corbett wore a finely-tucked
black glace silk, and had her skirts trodden on all
the way down the aisle by the young Hopes in big
white, turned-down collars.
It is understood that Government House takes
the Royal Death badly to heart. His Ex. has
not given his feelings away to the reporters, for
the Queen was a friend to her Lord Chamberlain,
and it is as a personal friend that the Hopetouns
lament her death. There will be no entertain-
ments at. Govt. House, under six months, and the
V.R. visit to Tasmania is abandoned.
Anybody trying to see “her Excellency” in
the flesh gets badly snubbed. Her Ex. is going
to be as invisible as Isis. No interviews, no little
chats, no nice friendly committee discussions.
She is only to be got at through the medium of
Mrs. Corbett, who is stated to have come out to
act as “Lady-in-Waiting” to Lady Hopetoun,
and stand between her and the crowd.
An official at Govt. House lately informed an
inquiring society woman that Mrs. Corbett didn’t
take that outrider round the town for “pomp.”
On Saturday afternoon extraordinary scenes
went on at Queen’s Statue. I was in a room over-
looking the square, and in the course of an hour I
saw one, two, three, four, five, six, seven women
borne out of the crowd on policemen’s shoulders.
There was an epidemic of fits and fainting. Evi-
dently hysteria was rampant. From all patts of
the crowd policemen would appear suddenly,
hoisting white-faced women aloft. The women
were carried—after terrible struggles to get
through the packed masses—to the Oxford Hotel
near by, where the hall quickly assumed the ap-
pearance of a hospital.
I have come to the conclusion that Sydney
doesn’t understand the Art of Grief. It travesties
even its tears What think you of the following’
You’ll hardly believe it. But I saw it. With my
own eyes I saw it. I saw a baby-girl dressed up
as a little Queen Victoria. She had a little gold
helmet on her head, was decked with black rib-
bons, and draped about with a motto that had the
words “ Mother and Wife ”in it. That was car-
ried proudly through the crowd on its father’s
shoulders, and moved women to tears—they knew
not why.
The Liedertafel’s singing in the open air was
tender and impressive. Those hundreds of men’s
voices, unaccompanied, made the'best requiem of
all. Alfred Hill, looking dark and sombre—while
I admired his intense expression as a conductor I
couldn’t think of him as one of the coming gar-
deners up the North Shore line where "he lives—
led the male voices through “O Sanctissima.”
Four thousand school-children sang ; bands played
Chopin and Beethoven; and above the music,
above the rumbling distant thunder, over the
clashing bells of St. Mary’s, and the rumble and
clang of trams, came the military tolling of the
big Post-office bell, clear, majestic, incessant.
Do-o-m, do-o-m, do-o-m, till the nerves tingled
and the mind was filled with the thought of a
Juggernaut of Fate rolling remorselessly over
peoples, Queens and Empires. Do-o-m, do-o-m,
do-o-m.
I cannot help expressing my astonishment at
the way in which Sydney has manoeuvred its
black. How did it manage it all ? People known
to be without a penny are going about in the
most elegant black toilettes, new hats, new gloves,
new silk petticoats, frills, and frou-frous and
feathers galore. Perhaps there will be the piper
to pay later. Who knows ?
We do know, though, that a purple silk petti-
coat and crcpe-de-chine costume can’t be had for
the asking, but evidently the woman who can’t
get a new dress when she wants it is fast fading
into oblivion.
Crepe-de-chine is the most swagger mourning
material. I saw a simple but ineffably Frenchy
toilette on Saturday—on an Englishwoman. It
had a black crepe-de-chine skirt gathered and
gathered and gathered, round and round and
round, right away down from the waist-line to
below the knees. That was all the trimming on
the skirt, which flowed * out from the gathers in
soft, graceful flows. _ The bodice was what
Americans call “a waist,” of finest glace, but-
toned down the back, and as simple as you please
in the front, with just a yoke of infinitesimal,
sloping tucks to break up its simplicity. But the
cut— ! With this was worn a black silk hat, three
large bows in front, and a roll of silk round the
crown—the hat that Paris never tires of.
What a time the florists have been having!
They could do with a Commonwealth celebration
and Royal funeral every month. One hundred
and sixty orders for Dr. Leo Neill’s funeral are
said to have been unfulfilled in consequence of the
stress of business. “Leo” Neill was truly a
coming man, and one of the most popular in the
medical profession, but too much work and too
little play soon ended a promising career. Drs.
Mills and Scott-Skirving were in attendance, and
many leading doctors were in consultation, while
Nurse Munro, matron of the Trained Nurses’
Home, and two other nurses also exercised their
skilHn vain. Dr. Neill intended leaving for Tas-
mania on the following Saturday, but pneumonia,
getting hold of a run-down constitution, proved
too much for poor old “ Fog,” as he was endear-
ingly called by his medical brethren.
The sudden and utter blankness that followed
Commonwealth week has proved rather detrimen-
tal to the two Art Exhibitions. They were just
too late in opening. By the time their shows
were going everything was off, and the shows were
left. The Art Society closed up last week, on
account of the Royal death, and broke up its show.
The Society of Artists closed too, but it artfully
kept its pictures together; now it re-opens. Some
“At Homes ” will be given among the S.A. pic-
tures shortly. Mrs. Edmund Barton will act as
hostess one evening, and the Art Students have
fixed their At Home for Wednesday, 13th Feb.,
at 8 o’clock.
You will be sorry to hear that Marion Drewe,
that charming girl and energetic artist, is about
to take her departure from Sydney for San
Francisco. Last Friday wvoniug the pupils
of artist J. S. Watkins ve a dance and
a f ? u .PP er at his studio in Hunter-street, at
which a large number of friends attended
to wish Miss Drewe and the Junoesque Miss
Cavill, who goes with her, a hearty farewell and
all sorts of prosperity in the city of the Golden
Gate. Miss Drewe and Miss Cavill leave per
Sonoma on the 26th.
The Palace Theatre flared up with a bright little
revival house on Monday. All the boxes full, and
lots of bare shoulders in the d.c. Mrs. Ted
Simpson and Mrs. W. Street were in a box
opposite the three Austin sisters. Mr. B. R. Wise
showed up for a short while in the d.c.
The cause of this extra glitter coming in the
middle of the long run of “A Message from
Mars, was a new curtain-raiser, and the twentieth
anniversary of W. Hawtrey’s stage career. “A
Highland Legacy,” by Brandon Th mas, showed
Hawtrey in a Scotch brogue, kilts, and vermilion
knees. , Winnie Austin looked her prettiest in the
heroine’s part. She wore a snappy little cream
satin coat with wide sailor collar edged with
turquoise, a well-cut, cream voile skirt, and a
crearn Tam o’ Shanter toque with turquoise choux.
The thrillful Tommy Holding came well out of a
lot of collar, and circulated more thrill than ever
about the houseful of females.
That wire from Mr. George Musgrove, “Don’t
feel justified in singing same time funeral pro-
ceeding. Greatest respect due from profession to
Gueen and-King, who are great supporters of
music, was ndt a brilliant idea. The abandon-
ment should have been made gracefully or not at
all, without giving an unhappy reason like that.
At the Ogilvie dinner last week a pretty meet-
ing was that of the Swedish contralto, and the
young Scotch poet. Madame Janson—-M rs
Fischer is the wife of the Swedish Consul in a
Scotch town near Ogilvie’s home, and she and the
poet talked “ home ’ enthusiastically. Then the
lady wrote her address on his menu card, and
their next meeting will probably be among the
braes and burns of Scotland.
The Coningham case comes on again in March
and is the only exciting thing society has to look
forward to now. By the way, a young Sydney
barrister has, it is averred, been made the victim of
a practical joke. Someone— members of the legal
perfesh, it is whispered— sent out printed circulars
re the Conmgham case, asking people to assist in
the humiliation of the Roman Church,” and
signing them with the name of the barrister
aforesaid—Percival Waddy. On dit, that more
will be heard of this affair, later on. It isn’t the
first time Mr. Waddy has been treated to a prac-
tical joke, either. He has been singled out again
and again, but this may be the last time—if all we
hear is true.
From Elvy and Co. comes the best waltz of the
season— The Commonwealth Waltz,” by Marv
M Carron Maguire. This is tuneful, full of verve
and charm, and the gifted young composer de-
serves much sympathy if a period of mourning
puts waltzing out of the question this season.
The Brisbane folk are actively investigating the
causes which led to the general gigantic orgy
indulged in by the Imperial Tommies at a local
racecourse, borne say that the section of the
public so generally blamed are “not in it” with the
committee, which supplied free so much Bulimba
beer m the enclosure where the thirsty Britishers
were herded. Perhaps one of the most pathetic
and comical sights was the despair of the officers
of the naval brigade when the men formed into
fine at the call wearing the giant busbies of the
Grenadiers, which, no doubt, were more comfort-
able than their own head.gear after so
much beer. One lot of incapable Tommies were
taken to the wharf piled neatly in rows on a lorry.
No doubt T. Atkins’s idea of Paradise will be
Brisbane and its beer, and not Sydney and its
champagne.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“Melbourne suddenly realised, at the death of
the Queen, that the city did not possess a statue
of H.M. The Age immediately started a shilling
subscription towards the erection of one, beating
the Argus by one day. Next morning, the
Argus announced that about £2OOO would be
needed for the work. To collect this, the paper
remarked, small collections were a farce, and
called through its columns for large, fat cheques.
But, as Melbourne is nothing if not up to date,
she had better hustle round for a statue to King
Edward VII., who is now nearly 60 years of age.
“ The expensive funereal drapery of Melb.
Parliament House was made to look cheap and
foolish by the placarded announcement—‘Our
Beloved Queen Has Gone To Her Rest! ’ Appar-
ently the Vic. Govt, got its idea of official mourn-
ing from the In Memoriain advts. in the dailies.
Men who could think it necessary to explain the
meaning of all that blackness by sticking up such
a notice must have been very nearly capable
of selecting ‘ Darling Mother in Heaven ’ for their
text. One thing needed of the Federal Govt, is a
calm, gentlemanly Minister for Griefs and
Gratulations, with full authority to draw up
rules for the guidance of hysterical officialdom
in all matters of ‘ loyal ’ display. Any who in-
cline towards ‘ln Memoriam ’ gags must be put
under restraint. Why, there were 1000 State-
school children singing the Anthem outside Melb.
Parliament House last Thursday, and 1000 young
minds came away impressed by the maudlin sen-
timent of the legend that stared from the top of
the building—‘ Our Beloved Queen Has Gone To
Her Rest.’ In years to come they will quote it
to their children as a classic in the literature of
Australian parliamentary sorrow. Ye gods !it
is a precedent.
“ The memorial service at the Exhibition was
rather a fiasco. The general public was kept in
the dark about the arrangements till the daily
papers broke to it the news that all tickets were
already disposed of. Then, when the day came,
there were lots of empty seats in the building.
However, absentees didn’t miss much. It was a
listless, flabby function. The Imperial Tommies
chatted among themselves, and a much-parodied
chestnut vulgarised the hymn-list.
“The Ways of South Australia have gone to
the extent of mourning-cards for the late Queen.
So have Premier Turner’s family, and a few other
earnest good souls who are sorrowing as for one
of their own flesh and blood.
“The mourning habiliments of Premier Tur-
ner’s family and Mayoress Gillott approach the
gloom of intimate grief. Not a particle of jewel-
lery is in evidence among them, and the common
or garden steel pins are replaced by small spikes
of black color. Even their smiles are dark.
“In the purple pavilion crowning Parliament
House steps at the Proclamation the cream of the
cream collected. Mrs. (Chief-Justice) Way, of
South Australia, commanded attention by her tall
splendid figure and telling style. Mrs. Justice
Williams admitted a tucked bib of white into her
black gown and introduced the only suggestion of
jauntiness into the solemn gathering. The
Madden girls looked snug and dimply m black
frocks, with new bebe-bodices buttoned up the
back. Miss May Seddon looked dashing in black
and white, and sported a black tasselled stick 4 la
Palotta in lieu of a gamp. All the Wellington
girls, like Premier Seddon’s daughter, sport sticks
°/ fashion. Who says Maoriland isn’t
Advanced ?
“ At the Proclamation, the light of the Russian
Consul’s uniformed figure went out in the grand
picturesque presence of the Rev. Athanasius
Kantopoulos (the Greek priest), who, dressed in
towering black turban and black-and-purple robes
with long gold chain and large cross dangling
from his neck, turned large, mournful eyes on the
assemblage, and in an uninterested way returned
the looks of the curious.
In proposing the toast of the King, in the
Town Hall, after the Proclamation, his Worship
got through the ordeal with lawyer-like despatch.
Mayor Gillott’s speech halts or drawls somewhat,
m the pulpit manner, but he marshalsh is alleged
facts carefully, fixes his eye on a given point, and
gets there without figure of speech, play of wit, or
let or hindrance of any kind.
“Hurried messages were despatched by letter
telephone, and wire on Wednesday morning to
the Wives of all the Parliamentarians to rally to
the House in the afternoon for a view of the
English officers out of uniform — i,c, 9 in plain
dress. The fair ones collected, and were pre-
sented (or vice-versa), and some escorted the dear
feUows over the grounds, and the gardens, and
the library, and some took the chappies in tow to
sit the worn-out and venerable ‘ Queen’s
Chair, and some to write their signatures and
sentiments in autograph albums, and some to ask
for more buttons. And what did the brave fellows
say to all this ? Scarcely one fair flutterer can
report anything sensible out of a long, drawling
don t-you-know ’ string of anaemic ejaculations,
ihe heroes seemed (out of uniform) to be de-
pressed by their own terrible weight of tiredness.
“Mrs. George Turner was one of the few pri-
vate entertainers of the Imperial officers. They
took afternoon tea in her little St. Kilda bower
on the eve of their departure for England. It
was a bit of a squeeze for some of the supercilious
heroes, but the old adage of the ‘heart being
bigger than the room ’ came in for general expres-
sion, and saved the situation. By the way Mrs
George vaunts the quill with which the Proclama-
tion was signed. The good lady’s jolly face when
the suave Excellency turned aiid presented it to
her was a study in wholesome delight.
Now that the English officers are gone, minus
a gross of buttons and ‘ don’tcherknows, ’ general
opinion among women gives palm for popularity
to that dashing Captain, who really didn’t say
much, but looked a lot of unutterable things, and
who confided to so many that he had £6OO a year,
and was going to throw up war for the stage.
Moreover, that he was coming out to Melbourne
to make his first trial on the boards —‘ where you
jolly people are so good to beginners don’tcher-
know.’ The girls he made this remark to are still
wondering whether it was a compliment to the
Nation or otherwise.
“The officers attached to the Indian regiments
were given the run of the theatres during their
stay in Melbourne, and one night the circle in a
fashionable house was packed with the gorgeously-
tailored treacle-colored heroes As a result
the manager’s office was presently invaded
by some indignant whites in evening dress, who
clamored for their money back, refusing to sit out
a hot evening in a theatre swarming with ‘ colored
persons.’ The black brother is good enough as a
defender of the Empire, but when it comes to
associating with us and our wives and daughters,
bai jove, that’s too much dontcherknow.
“Lieut. Osborne, who has been enrolled in the
sixth Contingent, belongs to an athletic, rich, and
vigorous family; otherwise, as he is married and
is 40 years old, no reason could possibly be given
for his acceptance.
“The Calvert family being in mourning, the
birth of Mrs. Gordon Lyon’s daughter last week
was not attended by the bonfires the Western
squattocracy generally set ablaze on such occa-
sions.
“Premier Seddon, his better half, daughter
and son ‘ did ’ Melbourne in serious family-party
style. Mrs. Seddon’s relatives are old Williams-
town folk and in her youth she gathered shells in
company with bairns now grown to be solid,
Scotchy local notabilities. During his recent
visit, George Reid was very chummy with
Seddon, while our own Mason, M.L.A. showed
himself sweet as honey to the M.L. magnate.
The latter, in return, conferred various official
compliments upon Victoria’s ‘ picturesque ’
Speaker.
“ The late Wesley Hall, the gilded mining man,
who (unlike North Lyell Crotty) has, in his will,
been so good to his wife, was a kind, gentle man,
of sallow, serious appearance. His first wife, a
very pretty girl, with no fortune but her face,
went to Brisbane from Williamstown (Vic.) to
visit relatives, and captivated the rich, retiring
bachelor, who always said he could never under-
stand what the pretty young thing saw in a dull
old fellow like himself. They were very
happy in their married life, but the bride
was ambitious, and soon transferred herself,
husband, babies, and sister, from Mount Morgan
to London, where the household was launched
with great magnificence. When Mrs. Wesley-
Hall died, a few days after the birth of her third
daughter, the bereaved husbancl fled with his
family from England and its sad associations and
settled in Melbourne. After some years he mar-
ried the sister-in-law, who had been with his wife
throughout her married life, and who had devoted
herself to his children from the time of their
mother’s death. The present Mrs. Wesley-Hall
is not a beauty like her predecessor, but is a bright,
comely, little woman, leal and generous to her
own people and devoted to her niece-step-daugh-
ters. The latter havebeen reared hitherto like little
princesses, never wearing a speck of anything but
white from top to toe, and surrounded by a bevy
of foreign nurses with a view to future lingual
proficiency.
“A correspondent of the daily papers makes a
suggestion re a statue to the dead Queen that, in
the interest of the criminal classes, ought to be
adopted with enthusiasm. This genius proposes
that the statue shall be surmounted by a coronet
of solid Victorian gold, and bear a golden sceptre
in its hand. In a city where Parliament has to
put up with a wooden mace because the more
valuable one was stolen, and where gas-meters,
bronze pillars, iron fencing and brass door-
knockers have a habit of disappearing nightly,
this golden proposal should be received with joy.
It would pay Little Lonsdale-st. to contribute to
a shilling subscription towards such a statue.
Yours affectionately,
GOULI-GOULL.


The Value of Indications !
In 1898 twenty-five of RAFFAN’S Bronchial Catarrh
Remedies were bought. In 1899 the numbers were
260. In 1900 the sales were 2000—convincing testi-

One of the dangers of Sydney during the holi-
day season will be Che burglar and sneak-thief. The
Federation festivities will attract all the rascaldom of
Australasia, and plate and purses will be constantly
menaced. Those who are leaving Sydney, and visitors
to Sydney, therefore should deposit their plate, cash
and valuables ih the Sydney Safe Deposit for security
Access can be had to them at any time during business
hours. The vaults are situated behind Paling’s, off
George-street, and are always open for inspection. They
are in themselves a sight worth seeing.*

PERSONAL ITEMS.
It is recorded that Queen Victoria would
have been named Georgiana, but for the inter-
ference of the Prince Regent.
Of the immediate predecessors, in name, of
King Edward VII., Edward VI. reigned six years
and Edward V. three months.
The Governor-General, it is plain, has no
wasting trouble. He has gained 281 b. in weight
since his arrival at Sydney.
Madden apparently wants to be Lord Mel-
bourne. But lie never will be. Australia greases
lords —but it wouldn’t stand locally-manufactured
ones.
The Duchess of Fife, eldest daughter of
King Edward VII., will be 33 on Feb. 20. Only
her brother York and three young children stand
between her and the throne.
The coins which will be minted for Edward
VII. will be the first issued at London in 400
years with a full-bearded head. Henry VIII.
was the last with a full beard.
Yes, York and May will come. Albert
Edward is a King and a man of some sense, and
he knows that a visit to Australia from York and
May will be the right thing for the welfare of the
King business.
The anti-French feeling in Britain may
have been a factor in the Queen’s death. Had
H.M. spent the cold months in the south of
France as usual, she might have weathered the
severe English winter.
George Turner states that the first official
to draw a salary from the Federal Govt. Treasury
department is an office-boy whom he appointed a
few days ago. Asked for the name of the historic
boy, Turner couldn’t remember it.
When it was cabled that Kruger had wired
condolence on the Queen’s illness, several Maori-
land papers had headlines, “ Kruger’s Impu-
dence.” But when the Queen kindly wired her
sympathies to Mrs. Joubert, noM.L. paper called
it “impudence.”
The new King of England had a habit of
saying that. he would certainly not outlive his
mother. He was thinking of the short lives of
those who drink much champagne, which he is
understood to have practically abandoned in favor
of whisky-and-soda.
Rumor indicates that Primate Cowie, of
Maoriland, proposes shortly to retire. He has
been over 30 years Bishop. He successfully
pacified the Maoris on many touchy occasions.
Originally he hailed from London and is now on
the brink of seventy.
A son of Bryan O’Loghlen, a white-headed
young man of small stature, goes with the sixth
Vic. contingent as Lieutenant at 17s. 6d. per day.
Young O’Loghlen went from a wine-and-spirit
business into the Bushmen’s Contingent, and re-
turned after a few months’ service with Tom
Price.
Michael Heaver, retired member of Vic.
Solice force, who died at Warrnambool the other
ay, was escort in Dublin for Chas. Gavan Duffy
when the latter was on trial for treason. Apropos
of that event, Melb. Age once said in a leader
that Duffy ought never to tie his cravat of a
morning without thanking the gods that his
cervical vertebrae were still intact.
A SKETCH ON MELBOURNE BLOCK.
A Queenslander who just missed by a
point promotion to the Federal Cabinet was
Charles Hardie Buzacott. Buzacott has done
much pioneering in journalism and politics, and
despite the Courier policy, always detested black
labor. As it is, lie hopes to carry a seat in the
Commonwealth elections.
Sixty years ago the map of N.S. W., from
Bathurst to Bourke, was a blank, yet at Warren,
on the Macquarie River, 353 miles out, was one
settler who went there as a boy of 15, and re-
mained there all his life. John Readford,
who at one time held large pastoral properties,
died at Warren on Feb. 1.
Of the first Cabinet in which G. H. Reid
had a seat, only himself and F. A. Wright con-
tinue active volcanoes. Copeland is in England
buying yachts from millionaires and teachings
decorum to duchesses; Stuart, Farnell, Dailey and
Jennings are dead, and the rest are politically
dead. And that is only 17 years ago.

for the Dilke isn’t
sauce for the Cham-
berlain apparently.
Dilke had been wir-

“ Tun ” : Lord Roberts is not a teeto-
taller. I have seen an order from him for a
large consignment of Carbinet, from a Melb.
vigneron.
While G. H. Reid was fishing at the
Scamander, Tas., he asked a yokel, “ Are you a
Protectionist or a Freetrader ? ” The ancient
raspberry-picker, somewhat staggered by the
question, replied, after reverently eyeing the
portly proportions of the champion wobbler, “ I
am a Free Methodist, sir.” He had mistaken
Georgey Porgey for a “ parson.”
The death of Bishop Salvado, of Westralia,
leaves Archbishop Murphy, of Hobart, the oldest
bishop in the Australian R.C. Church. He was
ordained 63 years ago. It was Dr. Murphy who
performed the last rites over Daniel O’Connell.
There are only two prelates alive who were ap-
pointed by Pope Gregory XVI. One of these is
Archbishop Murphy and the other Pope Leo XIII.
Perhaps the most extraordinary double in
Australia is formed by George Reid and “ Teddy”
Finn, the Melbourne journalist. Reid looks much
the older man, but the resemblance between the
two is astonishing. Finn need only buy a monocle
and pertinaciously tuck in his toes to absorb all
the glory Melbourne has to spare for the
unveracious politician whose trade-mark is a dry
dog.
Almost as a matter of course crops up a
yarn to the effect that when the late Queen was
in extremis she sent for a R.C. cleric—Cardinal
Vaughan, this time. Victoria—again as a matter
of course—is also credited with having had during
life those secret R.C. proclivities which that
Church has made a point of attributing to every
noted Protestant monarch—except Billy the
Third !
The dilatoriness of Premier Lyne in ap-
pointing a N.S.W. State commercial agent in
London is caused by a still-lingering doubt as to
whether Lands Minister Hassall is really the
“ boy for Galway.” Ex-Mayor and routed aider-
man' W. P. Manning is not enthusiastic over
immediate politics either in the State or the
Federation, and it is said Lyne is strongly pressed
to give the knight a year or two in London.
There has been a mild rush for private
secretaries lately by State and Federal politicians.
In some cases the billet is seized with alacrity;
but a few who “have been there” regard the
work as only good enough for an unemployed.
Disraeli and Gladstone put their private secre-
taries into Parliament and started them on the
road to a peerage, “but,” said an ex-p.s. lately,
“you are in Australia simply the man who licks
postage-stamps, runs errands when the telephone
won’t work, and keeps tally of drinks.”
What is described as a peculiar point to be
decided in connection with Vic. Old Age Pensions
is that of a lady, born in Hanover in 1825 and
never naturalised in Australia. George Louis,
Elector of Hanover, became King of England in
1714, and that country remained under the British
Crown till in 1837, when, through the operation of
the Salic Law, it passed from the rule of the late
Queen to that of her uncle, the Duke of Cumber-
land. So the aged would-be pensioner claims to
be British-born. And as she certainly was British-
born, her claim looks pretty good.

vote !

BILL: “So that's the parson bloke wot wanted to

LIZ: “Fes, the old silly. I wouldn’t so much

Poor old Rev. Haweis is dead. How he
did pester the Australian dailies to let him spin
along on “music and morals!” “I feel,” he
would say, “that when I address the public
through your great paper, 1 address eighty or a
hundred thousand people, whereas from the pul-
pit three or four thousand are all my voice can
reach.” And then he gave the name of the bank
into which the cheque for the article could be
paid.
A half-forgotten story is that of the telling
of the late Queen’s “fortune” by a gipsy woman
whom she encountered near Balmoral, and who
foretold that Victoria’s death would be presaged
by “ the falling of the Great Stone.” It is well
known that for a long time the Queen took this to
refer to Gladstone, but now comes news that three
weeks before the Queen’s death there fell flat the
largest remaining stone of the famous Druidical
circle at Stonehenge (Eng.).
Trenwith is living up to his reputation as
Vic. Works Minister. T’other day a pick-and-
shovel gang engaged on Yarra bank struck upon
being asked to work “piece,” and carried their
complaint to the Minister. Trenwith went back
to the job with them, and, stripping to his boiled
shirt, began mud-punching with great zeal, and
demonstrated to the satisfaction of the men that
lie could knock out Bs. a shift at the price.
Whereupon the strike was declared off.
The shuffling of the Queensland cards
which gave Drake to the Commonwealth ensures,
it is calculated, the Commonwealth Chief-Justice-
ship for Griffith, and the Q. C.J.-ship for Rut-
ledge. Griffith would prefer Federal active poli-
tics, and is bitter at the popular rejection of his
attempt to grab both a pension and a political
position, but his bitterness will be nothing to
that which Rutledge will experience when he
presses his mediocre claims for the first judicial
seat up North.
A well-advertised Melb. pastor, who is al-
ways topical, preached an expensive sermon on the
new King, Edward VII., the other day, and
pointed out that Albert Edward had always been
an influence for good, a perfect gentleman, and a
man of most exquisite morals. According to the
complacent parson, the King, throughout his
religious early manhood and his saintly middle-
age, has been the righteous victim of shocking
calumnies —nothing more. Nothing succeeds like
succession.
Melbourne papers advertise sale of furni-
ture, pictures, horses, carriages, and other ad-
juncts of wealth and position, in the estate of the
late Otto Hug. Hug, a big handsome Swiss,
was an alleged healer who diagnosed when in
“trance.” He could “go off ”at any time, but
preferred to have his fee “ planked ” as a facilita-
tive. He treated persons at a distance by exami-
nation of their hair “cut close to their heads.”
Did a big business through the post-office, and
must have had enough’ hair on hand to stuff a
mattress.
Mr. Nicholas Lockyer, for years in
N.S.W. Treasury, afterwards Collector of Customs
and till the other day Chief Commissioner of
Taxation, goes into the service of the Federal
Govt, as Collector of Customs at Sydney. Mr,
Lockyer has been a highly successful public officer.
His father, Major- Lockyer, formed the first
British settlement in Weitralia, and was the first
to recommend Brisbane as a white settlement.
Mr. Lockyer, the new Federal officer, is com-
paratively a young man, being midway between
40 and 50.
Cabled that a sketch of the body of the
Queen, lying in state, lias been made by Prof.
Hubert Herkomer, R.A. Let us hope it is not
on a par. with Herkomer’s alleged portrait of the
Queen for which ' he or somebody else got some-
where between 700 and 1000 guineas from Mel-
bourne National Gallery. That so-called portrait
is about the most villainous thing of the sort ever
seen in any civilised country. Tt is an outrage on
Art, a blasphemy against the British Royal House,
and an insult to the human race. It should at
once be tomahawked and burnt, and now is the
time to burn it. Also, it might properly be used
to light the' funeral-pyre of the man: who
engineered its purchase for so outrageous a hunk
of the taxpayers’ money. It is not worth 2s. 6d.,
and would not fetch 7d. at public auction without
the frame.
Apropos a recent Bulletin paragraph to
the effect that Premier Salisbury hadn’t taken the
trouble to speak to Australian Federal leader
Barton :
An item has been eroing the rounds of the U.S. press to
the effect that Lord Salisbury has never so much as
spoken to Mr. John Morley, who was one of Mr. Glad-
stone’s ministers, and is almost the best-known literary
man in England ; also that Lord Salisbury never saw Mr.
Parnell. This was supposed to be a joke, but the follow-
ing, from the London Daily Express, seems to bear out
the statement as serious : “It is reported in Downing-
street that Lord Salisbury has in these reconstruction
arrangements made the acquaintance of several of his
Ministers. ‘ Who is that pleasant-looking man who
bowed to you ? ’ he is said to have asked his nephew one
day when they were out walking. ‘ That,’ replied his
nephew, suavely, ‘is a member of you Cabinet, Mr.
Walter Long, the Minister of Agriculture.’ The Marquis
of Salisbury, adds the Sussex Daily News, as such has
never seen the House of Commons. And he has been
Marquis now for 32 years.”
When that loud self-advertiser and gro-
tesque striker of attitudes, Clement Wragge, dis-
covered that the Styrian peasants fired cannon in
the hope of thereby dissipating hailstorms, and
persuaded the guileless cable-man to send the sur-
prising discovery all this way, he really struck, in
a very ignorant and backward corner of Europe,
the lingering remnants of a century-old supersti-
tion that has gone broke elsewhere. And it
would seem as if Clement W. had accepted this
belated fragment of old tradition as a new dis-
covery. The Wragge myth is as hard to under-
stand as the Reid myth. Clement has no better
luck in weather-prophecy than other Australian
authorities —in fact of late he seems to have had
very little luck. He has done no original work in
meteorology that anybody seems to have heard of.
His fad of a high-level station on Kosciusko was
only the revival of European and American
idea which had fallen into comparative disrepute
in other countries —a bit of old conservatism which
was passed off on the guileless Australian as some-
thing new—and it has produced no known results.
How do such things as the Wragge and Reid
myths keep afloat ?
“ Brew ” concerning the cabled release
from prison of Gabrielle Bompard :
It sets one recalling the stir made by the affair in the
Rue Tronijon-Ducoudray in ’B9. Having decoyed an
attorney named Gouff6 to her room, she and her lover
Eyraud strangled him, put the body in a trunk and drove
out to Lyons, where they had a quiet little funeral with a
ditch as grave. Some time afterwards she quarreled with
Eyraud—it shows how careful one must be when murder-
ing people in the company of a woman—and cheerfully
gave the whole affair away to the police. They locked
her up while looking for Eyraud, but gaol had no terrors
for Gabrielle. She liked it, and enthusiastically remarked
to a reporter that the table d’ hdte was excellent and the
gaolers jolly good fellows. When they took Gabrielle
to the little back room in the Rue Ducoudray and
went through the solemn farce of the reconstruction du
crime, she sat on the sofa and roared with merry laughter
as she explained the details. One gendarme got behind the
curtains and noosed another with a rope. “Magnificent!”
laughed Gabrielle, as the strangled bobby rolled his eyes
and lolled out his tongue, with due respect for detail,
“ That is the exact pose, monsieur. Please do it again, for
it makes me laugh. That is Gouff6 to the life.” The
Parisian populace adored its Gabrielle, and wore her fair
portrait over its heart. When she came up for trial there
were yet other surprises. The gentle Gabrielle accused
Eyraud of having hypnotised her. Gabrielle was a
woman, and beautiful ; Eyraud was neither. A gallant
court bowed to Gabrielle, gave her a term of imprison-
ment, regretting the sad necessity, and guillotined
Eyraud. Now Gabrielle, after 11 years of an excellent
table d’hote and the company of jolly good fellows, has
been released as Jane Small, in return for her exemplary
conduct. She will marry, and will bring into the world
lusty children to strangle more attorneys—or (stand back,
thou Horror !) to become attorneys themselves. Charm-
ing Gabrielle ! • ■

“ W.S.” writes to The Bulletin re the
horrors of the Western drought, and accompanies
his letter with some absolutely blood-curdling
photographs, which are too faint in definition to
be effectively reproduced
It is impossible for the city dweller or the denizen of
rain-blessed country to form any idea of the utter desola-
tion and misery wrought by the drought in the Far West.
Miles on miles of parched and burning desert—of empty
creeks and water-courses, the cracked beds of which
see in to gasp ; shrivelled scrub and dusty, stunted trees,
under which the famine-stricken stock have huddled up
seeking shelter from the pitiless sun, and died. A line of
thick timber in the distance marks a “ permanent ” hole
in a creek—never before known to fail, but now dry, with
the cattle rotting in heaps on the banks, in the bed, and
all around to the number of over two thousand ! And
hundreds of other dry water-holes bear similar witness to
the drought-fiend’s awful havoc. From the S.A. border
to the Cooper, across fiery sandhills on which the spinifex
is burnt black ; across the stony downs of the Cooper
country, from the Wilson to the Bulloo, from the Bulloo
to the Paroo, and from the Paroo to the Warrego, it is all
the same—one long stretch of withered wilderness, with
not a hoof to be seen for miles.
The breeding herds are all gone. The strings of
motherless calves following the cattle-pads out from the
water-holes told their own tale ; and that was many
months ago. Day to day, from bad to worse, the strong
have followed the weak, until, in the Cooper country,
total annihilation now marks stations which used to run
20,000 to 60,000 head of cattle.
Eastward, in the scrub-country, the miserable remnants
of once-numerous flocks and herds still linger, existing on
scrub felled by armies of scrub-cutters ; but without
green feed, most of them must also die. “Scrub-balls”
are formed in the paunches of the sheep, so large that
they can be felt, and it is marvellous how the animals
survive.
Could it have been known that the scourge would last
so long it would have paid better to let sheep take their
chance, because when the losses which have been cut for
are reckoned the balance now scrub-fed will have cost, in
places, somewhere about £i a head to keep them alive.
To sum-up, it is utterly impossible to write of the
country as it is ; no one who had not seen it would believe
the truth. It would be interesting to know what the
composition of the scrub-balls aforesaid is ; it would give
an idea of the “tucker” that unfortunate stock have to
live on in these parts.

Peter Waite, managing director of Momba,
which has a bigger acreage than any other N.S.
Wales sheep-squattage, to the Lands Commis-
sion : “In 1891 we paid £5OOO for shearing; in
1900, £l9OO. And yet per thousand it costs us
more than it used to.” This in illustration of
drought losses.

After a particularly hard struggle another
far north-west (N.S. W.) squatter, 22 years in the
district, has just put his possessions into a carpet-

Dear Bulletin.— Re “Mac’s"
combined strong woman and glass-
masticator (B. 15/12/’00). Writer did
the Pacific slope (U.8.A.) in ’96 with
a “ variety ” co. that billed a “ strong
woman.” At Monterey, after the
show one night, we had a “family”
supper, which the state of the treasury
justified us in lubricating pretty
freely. Conversation had turned
upon strong-jaw acts, when the S.W.,
who had imbibed rather deeply, but
had remained hitherto silent, sud-
denly jumped up, grabbed a pair of
glass salt-cellars—big, “unbreakable ”
articles weighing about 41b. each—
and snorted forth: “Talk about
chewin’ glass ! L’k ’ere, y’
1 how’s this, and this, and
this ? ” —rending first one salt-cellar
and then the other with her powerful
jaws. The co., rough as it was at
the best, gazed at her in horror
whilst glass-chips flew all over the
place. But quickly the fragments
turned a bright red and the fury gave
in two-thirds of the way through the
second salt-cellar. Bleed ? Yes, a
small bucketful. Now, will “ Mac.”
say how much his woman used to
Meed—just for technicality’s sake.
as for strong women in general, saw
one once in Hanover-street, Boston,
with a big police-officer under each
arm and busily kicking another in
front of her. This was at midnight,
and the three were objecting for all
they were worth, but with no effect
worth mentioning. Also, it may be
news to “ Mac ” that it is easier to
carry two sacks of flour the way he
mentions than one. While as for
teeth-lifting, those “in the know ”
smile hard every time that topic
comes up for discush.—A Retired
“Sampson.”

toric.
“Carl”: A Windsor-district orchardist
has a plan of his own. He is breaking-in a young
heifer to draught. Has her tethered to a log 9ft.
long, 18in. wide and 9in. thick with a plough-
chain, which is fastened round animal’s neck and
secured by wire. The poor beast is forced by
hunger to drag this encumbrance from place to
place, the ground all around being deeply scored
by the log.
THE HEATHEN IN HIS BLINDNESS.—IN ANTICIPATION.
“ Mammy,” said the clever boy of the family, “ that long-legged old geeser over there talking
the king, my father — that’s the new clergyman, isn’t it?” “ Yes, my child,” said his mother, her
maternal eye glistening with pride at the thought that his tender mind was thus early turning to
serious subjects. “ Yes, that’s the new clergyman.” “ Then, mother, won’t you save me one of his
shin-bones to make a flute?” “ How can I promise you, tiresome boy, when, for all I know, your
father may want ’em both to make a blow-gun. Now run away and play, and don’t wake your
little sister.”
“ Pyalla ” tells about the “ blue-gums ” :
Bush-pub. “brandy” is prolific enough Of snakes and
spiders, but only once did I see a case of the “ blue-
gums.” He was an out-back wanderer of the “ better
days’ ” class, and had been drinking hard, but we had no
idea that he had “ got ’em.” He was sitting propped up
against a tree near the pub. when suddenly he sprang to
his feet with a yell and made off as fast as he
could run, screeching as he went “ A wattle! a
wattle ! Oh, God ! for a wattle.” We followed up,
and presently we found him lying at the foot of
a wattle tree with his arms clasped tightly round the
trunk. “ What game’s this, Doc ? ” said one of the boys ;
“ Gome along back with us and have a drink.” “God !
no—let me be,” he said ; “ I’ve found a wattle—l’m safe
from the blue-gums ! ” We tried hard to unloose his
clasp, but it was no go. All the time he kept saying in a
hoarse whisper, his eyes staring wildly out of a hideously-
atfrighted countenance, “ Oh, look at the blue-gums !
They’re after me ! Didn't you see them as I ran -drag-
ging their roots out of the ground and following me ?
Oh, look at their twisted trunks ! that’s where their eyes
are. Look at them glarin’ at me ! Didn't you hear them
calling to each other as I ran, ‘Head him off! head him
off ! Stop him from reaching a wattle ! That’s the only
thing that can save him ! ’ Oh, look at their roots twirl-
ing and curling. Look at their eyes ! My God ! no—
don’t take me from the wattle ! ” We let him lie there
for some time ; then we got him loose and began to drag
him along. He got away from us twice, and each time
ran like mad until he reached a wattle. At last we had
to give him his way, and we watched him with his arms
round a wattle until next day. Then we managed to drag
him back to the pub., but he died two days later, still
crying that the blue-gums were after him.
TRUE SUPPORTERS OF THE POLICY OF
DRY DOG.
CHORUS OF FOREIGN IMPORTERS: “Now then,
George , remember you are to fight for us poor
men.”
“ Ita ” re the “ chaffing ” habit :
With the shearer and the navvy their open-air environ-
ment partly accounts for their excessive levity ; but the
conditions obtaining under-ground—the isolation, dark-
ness, community of danger-circumstances which ordi-
narily draw men closer together—render the deep-ground
miner of the “flash” order the most selfish and self-
centred of beings. The only bond uniting him to his fellow
workers is a spirit of aggressive ridicule towards some
unfortunate who either has not the strength or the spirit
to fight. Once knew a trucker —a mere boy—whose life
was rendered a hell by reason of an indecent nickname,
bestowed upon him by a certain hairy giant, which
“ stuck.” He used often to miss “ shift” and pay rather
than face the jeers of the crowd on tip. The miners were
lowered to their work, two at a time, in a bucket, in
which each man placed a leg ; and the bully made a
point of going down with the lad for the sake of making
some filthy jest as they disappeared from view. But one
day something happened in transitu,' and the two were
emptied down through some hundred feet of blackness.
The jury found “ accidental death,” caused by the bucket
striking a ledge and tilting, but those who knew the cir-
cumstances believed that the young fellow had cast both
himself and his tormentor to perdition.

“ Yang Anyufc ” : Re superfluous digits (B.
19/1/’9l). A certain well-known Australian official
and literary man was thus afflicted or blessed in
both feet. Of four children, only the eldest
(a daughter) inherited the peculiarity, which
she duly transmitted to her eldest child (a son),
who in turn may be expected to impart the super-
fluity to at least one of his progeny. A second
thumb, boneless, but bearing a nail, is the com-
monest deformity 1 have noticed on Chinamen,
but strangely no case of the kind in a Chinawoman
Ij&s come under my notice, though I have taken
special care to seek for such an abnormality.

“ Ababon : ” I know a, man in Dubbo
(N.S.W.), who has such an intense dread of
lightning, that \vhen a thunderstorm comes on he
creeps into a chest kept for the purpose, while
as an additional precaution his wife acts as
lightning-conductor by sitting on the chest till
the storm is over.
“ Thurla ” : Re Xerxes’
query (8., 29/12/’00) as to the
wherefore of cod-fish in the
anabranches and not in the
main river after a fresh. Per-
haps this may enlighten :—The
Warrumbungle blacks used to
enclose with stakes large beds
of sand that had been water-
holes in the Castlereagh and its
feeders. When a fresh came
they would spear and catch
the fish in these enclosures.
Their version was that the fish
got cut off in the rapidly-falling
river, and during drought were
forced to burrow and follow
the water as the holes gave out,
just as the crayfish, &c., do.
V hen these beds became water-
holes again, the blacks would
wade in, and wherever bubbles
rose, would spear a fish as it
came up from the sand. Be-
lieve that the whites have copied
tliis method, but they use wire netting in
place of stakes.
GOHANNAS I HAVE MET.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Some decent sorts of chaps, in * Ab-
Originalities,’
Tell of “ six-foot gohannas’—well,
Such 1 truthful ’ yarns as these
Just make me smile ; now listen ! but—
(You’ll doubt my words, I’ll bet!)
A song I’ll hum—or sing—of some
Gohannas ‘ I have met.’
" Bring on y’r ‘ Dipperotodons,’
Y’r Meggertheriums,’
Y’r ‘ Pleasysaurs,’ y’r ‘ Mastodons,’
Y’r whole aquariums !
And when y’ve brought th’ biggest Dipp—
Or Megg that you can get,
I tell y’ that they’re nothink to
Gohannas ‘ I have met.’
“ In days when beer had ‘ bite ’ in it;
The good old long ago ;
Th’ days of sprees, an’ cheques, an’ love—
When men were not too slow ;
The day of tramps ; before the time
Of cattle-trucks an’ trains,
I carted wool with teams of ‘ bulls ’
Across the Ole Man Plains.
“ One night I struck camp rather late,
An’ as I had no dog
To round th’ bulls up through th’ night
I chained ’em to a log ;
I chained ’em to one end, ye see,
As tight’s Y could desire,
Then went down to the other end
An’ lit me campin’ fire.
“ The fire was burnin’ brightly up,
An’ gettin’ nice an’ warm !
When, jumpin’ bunyips, boys ! that log—
Began to move, an’ squirm,
Then shook itself an’ gave a cough 1
(I knelt down then to pray.)
It looked at me ! then walked with ‘ bulls ’
An’ chains an’ all away.
“ Quite sleepy-like it towed them ‘ bulls,’
Like feathers, in a heap ;
He dragged ’em up a red-gum tree,
Then lay down fer a sleep.
I never got a bullock of
That blessed team again ;
The bones an’ chains hung there fer years,
Out on the Ole Man Plain.”
“ There ain’t no trees out on the plain I ”
“ Well, now, who said there was ?
Er—well, y’see—y’see, my boys—
They all died oil, becos—
With beasts like that a-climbin’ ’em—
They couldn’t stand th’ strain !
That’s why, y’see, there ain’t a tree
Out on the Ole Man Plain.”

A Girl in Danger.
A COFFEE PALACE AFFAIR.
Hearing that a young lady residing at the
Victoria Coffee Palace, Nolan-street Mary-
borough, Vic., had been in danger of her life,
under peculiarly distressing circumstances, a
reporter called upon her at the establishment,
which is noted for its home-like comfort and
moderate tariff. Miss Minnie Lethlean gave the
following particulars:—
“l am twenty-four years of age,” said she, “and
a native of Bendigo. It is quite true,” she con-
tinued, in answer to a specific enquiry, “that I
have been in danger of losing my life, though,
judging by my appearance, you would scarcely
think so. When I was eighteen, I was attacked
by influenza, and, subsequently, by pneumonia.
No one thought I would recover, and so severe
were my sufferings that partial paralysis set in.
By degress the paralysis left me, but I became
anaemic. I felt utterly run down, my face and
1 ips were pallid and bloodless ; I frequently
fainted, and on hot days could scarcely put one
foot before the other. I was depressed in spirits,
and the anaemia seemed to be developing into
consumption. I was placed under medical treat-
ment, but only temporary benefit was received.
Several so-called tonics were then tried, but they
proved useless. I read in the newspapers how
Dr. Williams’ pink pills had cured many cases of
anaemia, and I began taking them. After the
first box, I felt brighter and happier. As I con-
tinued with them, the color returned to my face
my eyes and skin were brighter, and I began to
put on flesh. My blood became enriched, and in
every vein and nerve I felt the benefit I was
receiving. Six boxes completely cured me. lam
now quite strong and plump. I eat and sleep
well, and take a real pleasure in life.”
By building up the blood and retoning the
nerves, Dr. Williams’ pink pills make weak and
listless men and women strong and happy. They
have cured thousands of cases of anaemia, debility,
paralysis, consumption, bronchitis, skin diseases,
dyspepsia, dysentery, chronic headaches, ladies’
ailments, loss of manly strength, etc. Sold by
chemists and storekeepers, and by the Dr
Williams’ Medicine Co., Queen’s Place, Sydney,
two and nine per box, six boxes fifteen and three,
post free. “ How to Cure Anaemia ” ; write for
special instructions.*

SCHWEPPE’S /CRATED
SARSAPARILLA
Dear Bulletin, —Isn’t the Jaypee busi-
ness a bit oppressive? In our township you can
be served with sausages by a J.P. butcher, with
cheese by a J.P. storekeeper, and with calico by
a J.P. draper. If you drive after dark without
lights you will be served with a summons by a
J.P. inspector of nuisances ; next day you will be
fined ss. and costs by a J.P. publican (who is
mayor as well). And then the local newspaper
(owner a J.P.) will report the proceedings. In
fact, the only man in the town who'is anybody,
and is not a J.P. is the schoolmaster. — Kay.

“Where’s that
fat-lamp, Jim ? ” asked
the contractor of one

away,” replied Jim ;

in the world (writes a

ling). Australia is re-

roo. Almost every-

line hailing from this

“kangaroo” at Ant-

labelled “kangaroo” is a big snake. There is

may be found. One pink streak sets forth that
the kangaroo is very plentiful in Sydney and
simply rages about Burwood and Parramatta.

“Yang Anyut ” : Be friction and fire-
making. In Borneo I have seen fire obtained in
a dozen ways; commonest method being to make
in a bit of soft-wood a 1,-in.-deep hole, in which,
when filled with tinder, a piece of split bamboo is
twirled with right hand, top end pressed by palm
of left hand —said bamboo being bent by pressure
into a semi-circle. Sparks, too, are readily obtain-
able by striking dry bamboo against a sharp edge
of broken glazed china.

“Pyalla” : Be the flying-fox
pest (B._ 15/12/’00). On the Macleay I
once witnessed a great slaughter of
these bats. Hearing that the blacks
were going to raid a stenchy “ camp,”
I attended, and when at sunset the
flying-foxes had sallied forth in thou-
sands the blacks cut about a dozen of
the “ roosting’’-trees nearly through.
Then we waited on the outskirts of
the scrub till near day-break when the
bats began to return and squabble, as
usual, for position. Hanging head-
down wards from the limbs of the trees
and from each others’ wings their
numbers -increased until the united
weight brought the weakened trees
down. Then the waiting blacks rushed
in, and great was the slaughter— and
the stink. Peaches merely brushed by
flying-foxes’ wings are tainted ; yet the
blacks eat these fulsome delicacies.

Found recently, 10ft. underground, at
Okete Falls, Rotorua, (M.L.), two pieces of quartz
rudely shaped as tools and probably belonging to
an earlier race than the Maoris of the Arawa
migration.
W. F. Hawtrey.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
W. F. Hawtrey, the man who brought the
“Message from Mars” to Sydney, is brother to
the famous London actor - manager, Charles
Hawtrey.
“I will be 45 years old on Monday, will have
been 20 years an actor by 2nd Feb., and 11 years
a teetotaller on 26th Feb. Tea? I like five or
six cups in an afternoon —this hot weather. My
first part was Mullins, the footman, in Burnand’s
play of ‘The Colonel.’ Pinero’s wife, Myra
Holme—she was a widow then, a Mrs. Hamilton
—was also in the cast.
“ I like to have as many pretty girls as I can in
the company, because the more pretty girls I have
The natural water-hole on what is now Mt. Poole station, far-west
N.S.W., alongside which explorer Sturt camped in the awful drought
he had the had luck to strike, has lately run dry—the first time on
record. Poole, Sturt’s lieutenant, who died in this depdt, was buried
under a tree which still stands; it bears an inscription. The property
is in the hands of the family of Melb.’s cx-Mayor Lang, who pre-
serves the historical landmarks.
the more the public is pleased. You see, a man
gets credit for the work done for him by others,
and pretty girls who are capable actresses contri-
bute wonderfully to success.
“ The actors in London, generally speaking, are
much cleverer than the actresses ; but out here I
find, that girls born in Australia, with only Aus-
tralian experience, have much more idea of acting—
are much more artistic—than the Australian men.
There’s a heap of stage talent in Australia; all
that’s wanted is for it to be brought forward.
Theatre conditio.is here now, as they were in
America 20 yeais ago—require the English lead
to make the company acceptable to the public.
But just as they’ve done away with the English
lead in America so they’ll do away with it here.
It has been said that both my brother Charley and
I lay ourselves out to get prominent society girls
into our companies, but that is not quite correct.
We try to get as nice a company as we can—we
want suitable actresses, and we pick the best who
come along. I always work hard at the small
parts, because they give a finish to the whole
piece that is wanting if the small parts are badly
or indifferently played.
“We call ‘Mars’ a play, but, as a matter of
fact, it’s a melodrama with a light comedy hero.
THE SPREAD OF MILITARISM.-ROYAL
BENGAL CAVALRY.
“ Heh! Tuppence yer don’t hit the spud, Billy.”
Ganthony brought my brother the play, and he
read it. It was then absolutely unlike its present
form. It was very much longer and almost in>
practicable to produce. But Charles saw in it
the makings of a big play ; recognised that the
audience would be intensely amused and delighted
at the selfishness of Parker in the first act; saw
the opportunities for striking situations and novel
effects in the second act ; and also saw that by
various little incidents in the third act, such as the
clock striking and the fire-engines passing, that
the audience could be shown the psychological
reasons for the dream in the second act. My
brother Charles therefore bought the entire rights
of the piece from Ganthony on the sole condition
that Ganthony’s name should always appear as
author.” (This sounds rather like what “Mars”
was punished for.) “ Charles, on buying the play
from Ganthony, gave it to my brother George to
rewrite and reconstruct on the lines that my
brother Charles had thought out. The piece is
now running its 15th month in London, and at
last mail to over £lOOO a week. It did well through
the hot weather of June and July ; it prospered in
the dead season of August and first half of Sept. ;
and Ch arles F rohman of N. Y. unsuccessfully offered
my brother, first, £5OOO, and then a much higher
sum for the rights of ‘Mars’ in America. And
all this has happened since the first night of
‘Mars,’ when my brother received the worst of
bad omens by putting his shoulder out on the
stage. Since I staged the piece in Sydney two
reputed actual messages from Mars have been re-
ported in the newspapers, and this fact caused a
cabbie whom I passed the other day to remark to
his mate, ‘ Bill ! look over yer right shoulder ; the
fat bloke ; that’s ’Awtrey, who’s runnin’ the ‘ Mes-
sage from Mars.’ I wonder ’ow much ’es payin’
for those yarns in the papers about real messages.
’E must think we don’t know anything out ’ere,
eh ? ’
“ Clean plays ! —that’s the maxim of my brother
Charles and myself. Look at the exceptional
runs:—‘Our Boys’—over three years; ‘Private
Secretary ’ —two years ; ‘ Charley’s Aunt ’ —over
four years ; ‘ Our Flat ’ —l played in ‘ Our Flat ’
during three distinct years. Now ‘Mars' is in
its 15th month, and Charles Frohman is offering
my brother £lOO per week salary and a guarantee
that my brother’s share of the profits shall not be
less than another £lOO per week to play ‘ Mars ’
in New York at Frohman’s theatre; and Nat
Goodwin prophesies that Charles will make
£lOO,OOO out of the two American tours. You see
it’s the absolutely clean plays that have the big
runs. I’m perfectly certain that the public would
sooner not have blueness; I believe in keeping the
theatre clear of it. The public like fun and
amusement without it, and they can have it, too.
There’s been a public outcry in London against
blueness ; and managers are appreciating the fact
that fun without nastiness or vulgarity will bring
more money and more people than questionable
performances will. Twenty years ago ladies were
never seen in London music-halls, but now they
go to them nearly as often as they go to the
theatres. The music-hall manager is getting more
and more careful as to what his people sing and
say and do, not only because he wishes to preserve
his license, but because he also recognises that
while a blue song will appeal to a certain section
of the public, a clean song will appeal to every-
one. And ladies are going now to the music-halls
where people smoke, because smoking is not con-
sidered the sin that it was 20 years ago.
“The whole tendency of the London stage is
towards the purer drama and the cleaner songs;
and I wouldn’t give a blue play, however brilliant
in dialogue and clever in construction, more than
a three months’ run in London. My brother
Charles put on a somewhat cerulean piece some
years ago, and the disappointment of his
audiences was so great that after quickly with-
drawing it lie said : ‘ Never again will I put on a
play that’s in the slightest degree off-color. First,
because I don’t-care for it myself; second, because
it appeals only to a very limited section of the
public ; third, because we cull the harvest from so
much larger a field if the play we produce is
clean.’ And my own desire here is to show the
public that we can give them any amount of
amusement without touching on any sexual pro-
blem or introducing suggestive matter. Yet, on
the first Sydney performance of ‘ Mars ’ I received
this criticism from a city paper: ‘ “Mars” is a
play where marriage and adultery are the com-
ponent ethics.’ There’s misrepresentation for you
—from a critic who didn’t go.
“ ‘ Tanqueray ? ’ Of course it is a marvel of
construction and a masterpiece in dialogue—
beautiful as a literary work. It’s intensely clever,
from the conversation of the men in the first act
to Paula’s suicide in the last —it is what I call a
play of extraordinary working. I went to see it
four times in London ; I was so charmed with
Mrs. Pat Campbell’s acting. Pinero based the
human interest of Tanqueray not on sensational-
ism, not on blueness for blueness’ sake, but on the
fact that in London Aubrey Tanqueray very often
does marry Paula. That’s where the interest
came in. It so often happens in London, that a
man falls in love with a woman ‘with a past,’ and
so many men have gone to the altar with this un-
answered question : ‘ Can a man reinstate such a
worn an by marryin g her ? ’ As the question works
out for Paula Tanqueray, the question goes
against her; her bad luck sticks to her; she can’t
get away from herself; poor woman ! she is
always on her defence ; and in the end she sees no
way out but suicide. In real life luck sometimes
comes the way of Paulas ; look at , and ——,
and , and scores of others who have lived
their reputations down, have led perfectly
placid and exemplary married lives, and have
died in the odor of sanctity. The reason !”
(so saying Hawtrey put his hand in his pocket
and pulled out a handful of money) “ That ! and
Luck ! A friendly hand came their way, lifted
them on to another plane and ever after things
went smoothly. Many a man and woman have
;one helplessly down for want of the least bit of
sustaining luck or power or friendship.
“Kipling’s own adaptation of ‘The Light that
Failed ’ with the sad ending of the man going
blind again, will be produced by my brother
Charles; Anstey’s ‘ The Man from Blankleys ’is
also in preparation.
“ Nance o‘Neil? Until I saw her in ‘ Macbeth ’
I thought she was only an apt pupil of M‘Kee-
Rankin’s. But after I saw her save ‘ Macbeth ’as
a play in Sydney I gave her all credit for initiative,
acting, originality, courage and determination.
Until she came on the play dragged awfully and I
suffered intensely. The tears were starting in my
eyes. I felt I must shout out or do something to
relieve the tension consequent on the feeling of
imminent fiasco. Then Nance O’Neil came on
with her commanding presence and forceful act-
ing, and saved the play—yes ! saved the play !
A strong word of praise, however, must be given
that most capable man, Kingston.
W. F. EAWTBEY, as the Tramp in “The
Message from Mars.”
Photo, by Talma & Co., Sydney.
“I asked Cardinal - Moran to come and see
‘ Mars,’ but he told me that it was against the
law of the Church for any of the clergy to visit
the theatre.
“Amy Roselle appeared with me in ‘The
Colonel,’ 20 years ago. lam not surprised at her
wanting to die; Dacre was a most depressing man
to know. He always had a grievance; always
imagined that every man’s hand was against his.
“Charles Wyndham is a lovely actor; anything
I’ve seen him in he has done charmingly. There’s
Beerholm Tree, Wyndham, my brother Charles —
(although I say it ‘as shouldn’t ’) Charles Groves,
and Titheradge —possessing the rare gift of con-
vincing their audience of the reality of it.
Wyndham, you know, at the outset of his career,
met with disappointment after disappointment—
fot the sack. And the marvellous thing about
im is that, although his voice is hard, rasping,
and metallic, you cannot go to hear him without
finding him intensely interesting and most con-
vincing.”
S. A. MILLS.

The Mutual [ife Jssociation of Australasia
Offers in the first year of the Twentieth Century and of the Australian Commonwealth,
Something Absolutely New.
Ordinary Life Policies do not cover Accidents unless they result in Death.
Ordinary Accident Policies do not cover Death unless caused by Accident.
The Combined Life and Accident Policies
cover both, and at specially low rates.
They are issued in the three following forms :
Table AC.—Assurance Payable at Death:
Premiums payable during Life of the
Assured.
Table CC.—Assurance Payable at Death:
Premiums payable during specified
term of years.
Table EC.—Endowment Assurance:
Payable at end of term of years, or
previous death.
Combined in each case with Compensation for—
Permanent Total Disablement:
Permanent Partial Disablement; or
Temporary Total Disablement;
if resulting from Accident during currency of the Policy,
and while Premiums are being paid thereon.
For Tables of Rates and full particulars apply at any Office of the Association, or to

SYDNEY.
The rainfall at Mount Morgan (Q.) is
attributed to blasting operations. Is “blasting”
to supersede praying for rain ?
It was a pleasing attention for Arch-
deacon Gunther, the studious son of a studious
father and the lineal head of his Church in Parra-
matta, to invite E. B. Forrest, the Queensland
M.L.A., to spend a day in the historic borough.
When King’s School, Parramatta, was started, at
the expense of the British Govt., in 1831, the
Imperial authorities sent out as first head-master
Rev. Robert Forrest, uncle of the present Q.
member. The Rev. Forrest’s rule laid the founda-
tions of a great school. He was neither pedant
nor pietist, but a manly, strong-believing church-
man, and so impressed his character on the estab-
lishment that he is in many respects there still.
King’s School was called so after the King of the
time, because of his grant of money, and not after
local Gov. King, as so many local people contend.

an account of a scrap in which 160 Tasmanians
and a number of guns were engaged :
I fired 280 rounds away. We only know of ki mg
Supposing the other 159 Tasmanians did the same
amount of shooting it seems that a l asmanian
to fire 11,200 times at a Boer before the latter
drops. And that is saying nothing of the artil-
lery.

for the carriage of materials on the Zone and Ward

POLITICAL
POINTS
Will Lyne or Seddon get there first with a
proposal to re-christen his state “Edward” or
“ Albert ” or “ Albertland ? ”
Leahy, chief barracker for private-syndi-
cate railwayism, has been made Minister for Rail-
ways in Q. This is a little stronger than usual—
even for Queensland politics.
Barton’s announcement that he favored
“ preferential treatment of British goods where
reciprocity is possible ” is interpreted by Maori-
land Anti-Federalists to mean that Australia will
be willing to enter into a treaty of reciprocity
with M.L.
AT THE CEMETERY.
MRS. MURPHY: “ Shure, Mrs. Flaherty, that
beautiful tombstone do show the amount of
respict ye had for the ould man.”
MRS. F.: “ Oh, yis, Mrs. Murphy — an' the whips
of money he had.”

N.S.W. Guerilla Minister See, presumably
by Gen. French’s advice, has gazetted the forma-
tion of a new corps, ominously named “The
N.S.W. Border Scouts.” Why “Border” isn’t
clear, unless N.S.W. has an idea of some day
going to war with one or more of the neighbour-
States.

The estimated value of N.S.W. Govt,
House, now occupied by Earl Hopetoun, is
£103.535 4s. lOd. The furniture tots up to about
£lO,OOO. The buildings alone are valued at
£60,500. This stone, mortar, and furniture should
certainly satisfy N.b.W. s desire to properly house
its potentate. To build or buy another similar
structure for , State Governor must bo stoutly
resisted-
Although fe- A. Fieetraders are spending
so much money in boosting their creed, there is a
strange silence amongst the men who might be
expected to lead. Symon, an erstwhile Freetrade
champion, is believed to have become a convert to
™, W i ’ : v, .rj.
a moderate Protectionist tariff as a national
necessity, and Glynn is in the same box. Ihe
only orator now left seems to be the deceased
Dan O’Connell whose statement, “Protection is
Robbery, ” is still displayed on the Adelaide tram
cars . *
Harold Parsons, M.L.C. (W. A.) in London
Times “advocates the recognition in the King’s
titles of the Kingdoms of Canada and Australia,
or else the raising of Canada and Australia to the
dignity of Kingdoms in the Imperial System.”
The Times backs up this idea ; wherefore the
Prince of Croajingolong, the Duke of Eunony-
hareenya and the Marquis of Woolloomooloo may
shortly become actualities.
Actually the three Brisbane dailies sup-
port the municipal candidature of Labor-member
Higgs, who, by hard-fighting and pertinacity,
gave ratepayers an insight into the hideous
muddle and mismanagement that enveloped the
Council. For the last year Higgs has made things
so lively that retiring aldermen are anxious to
escape into obscurity. And he has lately become
a shareholder of the Q.N. Bank. This looks like
opening a new sphere of public usefulness.
T. B. Cribb, the new Q. Treasurer, owes
his promotion to Prayer. He has been on his
knees for years beseeching successive Premiers
for office. T. B. is partner in the firm of Cribb
and Foote, which rules pious Ipswich and the
German farmers for 20 miles around. Armstrong,
Annear, Bell, and Stephenson, who together with
Cribb have chased every portfolio that has been
vacant within the memory of the oldest inhabit-
ant, will have to be placated anew. Only £14,000
was spent in job-Commissions last year; and
Premier Philp can easily double that.
Adelaide Corporation means to proceed
with Snow’s Electric Tram grab directly 8. A.
Parliament meets in June. Meanwhile Bing-
ham, the council’s previous proteyd, who has
been dropped, is pouring out his wrath in
the press. Lamentable that the move-
ment for a Municipal Trust has ceased
owing. to bickering amorist the
numerous municipalities interested.
So far Premier Holder is dumb
as to the Govt, policy on the
question.
One of Victoria's first
pensioners was so overjoyed that
he spent all his first fortnight’s
allowance on a glorious drunk,
and was fined ss. And now he has
been called upon to show cause why his
pension should not be cancelled for this
misconduct. If Victoria kept a simi-
larly keen eye oil pensioners who get
£lOOO a year, and stopped their allow-
ance when they got drunk, it might
be fair enough. As nothing of the
kind is done it is again a case of one
law for the rich and another for the
poor. Also some consideration
might be shown to an old man
who promptly handed the State
back almost all its pension money
in liquor duty and police court
fines. George Turner lives, in
•i political sense, on people like
that, and when he rounds on so
zealous a supporter of the
Treasury it is a case of un-
gratefully kicking his bene-
factor.
Premier Philp, of Queensland, again
promises, as he did a year ago, that his Govern-
ment is going in for adult suffrage. But last time
he said positively that it wa3 coming on last
session, and it didn’t. Now he says it will
“probably” come on next session. In 1902 he
will probably be heard saying that the adul
suffrage matter may possibly come along in
another few years if he feels rested enough.
In Australasia the only State which be-
lieves in Freetrade is one which never tried it. If
N.S.W. would leave off protecting its sugar, beer,
newspaper and other big industries, and try Free-
trade, or pure Revenue Tariffism, for even five
years, and then say that it believed in it, its word
would have a lot more weight. The wild assur-
ances of the State which protects so many of its
big industries that it believes in Freetrade is sug-
gestive of the ox preaching the advantages of a
meat diet and stating, with its month full of grass,
that it is a striking proof of the advantages of the
flesh-eating system.
J. G. Griffin is a N.S.W. candidate for the
Senate. This politically-kaleidosc-opic alderman
appears just now to be a Freetrader. His idea on
the preferential tariff scheme :
To admit free the goods of a country that admits our
goods free is not really preferential treatment; it is
merely common commercial decency.
The other way of looking at it is that to give a pre-
ference to the goods of the country that gives our
goods a preference would be common decency.
But Britain doesn’t, do that. The idea of the re-
ciprocity crowd is that Australia should give
Britain’s goods a long start in the Australian
market over the goods of all other countries ; and
that Australian goods should scramble in the
British market on exactly the same terms as those
of all other countries. Australia is to give a pre-
ference, but it isn’t to get any.
The deplorable E. M. Clark, member for
St. Leonards, a candidate for the Federal Parlia-
ment, in an address he delivered at “ the Shore,”
stated: —
I have not a solitary word to say against Mr. Barton—
in fact, he is too fair a man to be a leader in politics, and
it is a pity there are not more politicians of his stamp.
As to Mr.’ Reid, no one has a greater admiration for him
than I have. I regard him as the leader of political
thought in Australia. It is not for me to state a policy ;
people will follow leaders of political thought, and it is
only my duty as a humble representative to name the
leader I will follow. . . When Mr. Reid issues his
manifesto I hope the issues of the campaign will he more
clearly defined.
It isn’t every day that the country comes across
a refreshing person like this who frankly acknow-
ledges that he has no ideas of his own, and doesn’t
see why he should be expected to have a policy of
his own, and that he is simply going to follow
Reid without even waiting to know what Reid’s
policy is. Suppose Reid declares for polygamy
and cannibalism ?
Letter for Jas. Meury at this office.
Yankee millionaires have now taken to
bossing the U.S. Universities. Rockefeller has for
some time controlled Chicago University ; and re-
cently Dr. Ross, the leading U.S.A. sociologist,
was summarily “sacked” from the Leland-Stan-
ford University of California by Mrs. Stanford.
His offence was a scientifically-founded objection
to the free admission of Chinese to the Stales.
Concerning which ’Frisco Chronicle remarks :
“ The independent and fearless thinker in the
Middle Ages was placed upon the rack ; now he
is choked with a bag of dollars.”
Dear Bulletin. —Ry recent “ B.” par.
about beaching ships on the coast of Westralia I
can produce the photograph of a sailing-ship high
and dry and being loaded with wool. This ship
came from England for years to Condon, on the
N.W. coast, bringing station supplies and taking
back the wool from most of the stations within
100 miles of Condon. Ship came up creek as far
as possible, and was left dry at low tide, and in
unloading and loading bullock-teams used to fol-
low out tne tide from shore to ship and make as
many journeys as possible before rising tide
stopped work] This ship’s last visit was made in
’97 or ’98,, as the Freemantle-Singa;tore steamers
now call at conveniently near ports. —L.P.
Writes “Old Warrior ” to The Bulletin :
S.M. Herald recently devoted a ponderous leader to
prove that it might be worth while to mount soldiers on
cycles, and quoted a recent cable to the effect that a
body of cyclists at the Cape acts like “mounted infantry.”
Should think so ! It’s about time to knock the stuffing
out of this “ mounted infantry ” fad. No European army
has adopted the English system. The French have frankly
taken to the bike. The Germans, as far back as ’6O and
’7O, when they wanted infantry to support their cavalry,
sent them forward in light waggons. The English drill-
book of ’B9 directed the employment of cyclists, and in
Aug., ’OO, there were some elaborate cycle manoeuvres in
Sussex. Now for figures: 100 mounted infantry give, on the
English plan, 75 effective rifles at most. There must be
25 horse-holders to 100 horses, which need a ton of forage
a day. The same men could be carried in four four-horse
waggons, giving 96 effective rifles and four drivers with
only 16 horses, which eat 1601 b. of forage a day. Put the
same 109 men on bikes, and they are all effective, because
the “ wheel ” needs neither holding nor feeding. Also, a
horseman is a big target—loft. high by 9ft. long, while
the man on the bike is little bigger than on foot. No
horse, even if well fed and stabled, can carry a rider much
more than 30 miles a day for (say) a fortnight, whereas a
robust man, in training, can, with passable roads and a
good bike, do his 60 miles a day for an indefinite time.
After that, what is the mounted-rifle or mounted-infantry
fad but an excuse for allowing certain influential persons
to show-off their horsemanship and wear uniforms ?
