N.S.W. Public Servants have a fresh
grievance against the Public Service Board. , n
1898 the clerical division was classified under a
complicated set of regulations into various grades.
Promotion throughout all the grades was to be at
the rate of £lO a year provided the necessary cer-
tificates of ability and efficiency from the depart-
mental heads were forthcoming. Clerks at £loo,
£2OO, £250, £3OO had to pass a set examination
before they could lise higher. This system proved
so costly that its early break-down was predicted
when it first appeared. At the beginning of this
year the Board issued a brand-new set of regula-
tions to govern the service, among them ° ne can-
celling all annual increases on salaries over £150..
In Oct. of last year the usual recommendations
and certificates under the old regulations were
demanded by the Board and duly submitted by
the various under-secretaries. Consequently the
public servants entitled to these increases con-
cluded that they would be granted them for 1900
at least. And their opinion was confirmed by
No. 3of the new regulations just issued. This
reads as follows:
All regulations under the Public Service Act of 1895 in
force at the time of the coming into force of these regula-
tions are hereby repealed. Provided that such repeal
shall not affect the previous operations of any regulation
so repealed, or anything duly done thereunder, or any
right or obligations acquired, accrued or incurred, under
any regulation so repealed, or any remedy in respect of
such right.
Last week an intimation was sent round to the
Departments that the granting of increments
would be considered as from Ist July, 1900 on the
basis laid down by the regulations published on
31st Dec., 1900, thus cutting off any increase to a
salary of £l5O or over, and repudiating all
responsibility for increases due last year. The
Service regards this as rank repudiation. Morally
and (as they think) legally the Board is bound to
the old regulations, as per No. 3 of the new ones
right up to 31st Dec., 1900.
“8.A.” to The Bulletin :
One Of the first things that strikes a visitor to Japan is
the very evident spirit of militarism among the people,
young and old. Every school-boy is uniformed in blue
with brass buttons ; everybody in Govt, employ sports
more or less gold lace and brass buttons, and even street
pedlars wear semi-military attire. Ask a schoolboy what
he intends to be when lie is a man, and the invariable
reply is “ A Soldier.” The schoolboys turn out in heavy
marching order, knapsacks and all, and many
of the Australian cadet corps would suffer by
comparison with them. The little brown people
seem thoroughly alive to the fact that they are
going to have a big war on hand sooner or later.
A Victorian country J.P., within
earshot of whose, residence two brass,
bands perform and the Salvarmy “cor-
roborrates,” recently said in granting
an old-metal license : “ There should be
plenty of old metal about this town
shortly, judging by the noises made of
an evening. This place is simply a hell
upon earth.” The protest was reason-
able. What with bands, Salvarmy,
bells, the bottle-oh miscreant, the oyster
fiend, the ice-cream demon, and the
eternal yapping dog and crowing rooster
—life in most of the towns of Australia
is merely a perpetual sacrifice to the
God of Noise. The continuous ear-
torment of urban life is probably respon-
sible for more deaths than the world’s
wars.
In the nature of an apology for
undertakers:—
. Dear Bulletin. —As your paper has frequently
depicted the very necessary undertaker as a
red-nosed, watery-eyed hamr-dog sort of person
in a frock-coat, I should be pleased if you would,
in common fairness, allow a little space (say
6 x 2) to describe a very highly-respected mem-
ber of the “ Boxer ” society, and one who would
give you the same fair measure, should you
ever have any dealings with him. You can’t
always judge a man’s character by the black-
ness of his cloth. Our neighbor is an ideal
undertaker, whose family has followed the
game for generations. We have lived close
handy for many years (all still going strong,
but he bears us no ill-will on that account).
As he passes by, his naturally sad face wears
an apologetic smile, as if to say, “ That’s all
right, don’t mention it, friends ; any time ’ll
do ! ” Some mornings I am awakened by a
tell-tale “ tap, tap, tap, tap”—tacking-in the
lining. He buys the timber already sawn and
shaped into sizes, varying from Commonwealth
babies to that of the ponderous Freetrade
George ; and in less than half-an-hour he drives
past with his pretty little daughter riding
astride the latest order. Occasionally on Sun-
days I hear him whistling “Praise God from
whom all blessings flow”—and then the
familiar tapping. He was lately elected to
one of the most honorable of city positions
simply because—as the defeated opponent put
it—“ he hadn’t an enemy in the world.” The
undertaker who tips his child a “ bob ” every
time a customer calls is not a bad sort ; though
he does smoke a dozen or so of the best Havanas a day,
when the writer has to be satisfied with the ordinary
common briar. —Long Un.
lie the colossal statue in New York Har-
bour. There have been as many as 13,000 birds
found killed or wounded, at daybreak, in the
circular gallery under the lantern. During big
migrations they dash for the electric light.

N.S.W. Comptroller-General of Prisons
has ukased that gaol-officials shall, until further
notice, wear mourning bands on the left arm.
What a lack of imagination in the supposition that
the prison atmosphere is susceptible of any
deepening of its gloom! A gaol is itself the
House of Mourning par excellence.
Melbourne Stock Exchange brought out
its old Jubilee “transparency ” picture of Queen
Victoria last week and hung it across the front of
the building, amidst funereal draping, to show
how deeply the jobbers mourned their inability to
use it again for illumination purposes. The pic-
ture might very well have been entitled “The
Last Chance.”
For most cruelly maltreating a little infant,
a Sydney P.M., on Jan. 31st, sentenced a man to
six months’ hard labor —the heaviest sentence in his
power. The helpless mite was shockingly bruised
and in such a state mentally that the doctor who
examined her stated he never in his experience
met a human being in so terror-stricken a
condition: The injuries received by the child (18
months old) were likely to affect her for life. Yet
in summarily dealing with this brutal criminal
the presiding magistrate announced that he had
such a poor opinion of juries that, for fear the
prisoner should escape punishment altogether in
the superior court, he had decided to sentence him
to six months, the limit allowed him, although if
committed he (the child-beater) would perhaps
get two years’ gaol—a sentence more in keeping
with his deserts. The Bulletin’s opinion is that,
on the whole, the community has muoh less to
fear from juries than from magistrates.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par-
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance,
is solicited.
In a serious assault charge recently heard,
and dismissed, in Yass (N.S.W.) before Mr.
Thomas, P.M., the court sat from 10 a.m. till
midnight, making, with two hours’ intermission
for meals, 14 hours. Apart from the merits of the
case, surely the P.S. Board or the Attorney-
General ought to pull Mr. Thomas’ ear for this
imitation of the frequently-condemned practice of
certain Dracos of the Supreme Court. “ I have
never found,” said an English Chief-Justice,
“ that much justice was done in courts by candle-
light.” And, not a,part from the merits of the
case, there is very little doubt that the prosecutrix
—apparently under the influence of sexual de-
mentia —was speaking what she believed to be the
truth. So that, between the deliberate female
perjurer and the hysterical inventor of “out-
rages,” it’s slippery walking for any personable
young man in N.S.W. The Yass alleged assault
would seem to have much the same sort of basis as
the charge against the unfortunate Suffield.
A remarkable story of awful suffering
and splendid endurance is that of one Edward
Small, who recently, in the bush between Grafton
and Casino (N.S.W.), had a leg badly smashed
through his horse falling with him. According
to the account furnished to Sydney D.T.,
The unfortunate fellow, upon regaining consciousness,
took off his coat, and with his braces and tie firmly
fastened the coat around the injured leg. He lay all
Friday night without moving, and on Saturday morning
started to crawl over the rough sandstone country to
Myrtle Greek Hotel, nine miles distant. After going two
miles he manufactured an impromptu crutch, taking off
his shirt to prevent the jagged ends of the sticks cutting
his armpit. The blazing sun pouring down upon his un-
protected back soon, caused him most intense agony,
but still he persevered, and after going another
two miles he fell fainting, and there lay till
nightfall. Knowing that he was near a swamp
he made another desperate attempt to reach the
water. Up to this he had not touched a drop since
the time of the accident. After crawling a quarter of a
mile along the fringe of the swamp he found it was dry.
Recognising that it was then a matter of life and death
he started off again in the direction of the hotel, and
during Saturday night covered another two miles. On
Sunday morning, when making another effort, and while
he had hold of a barbed-wire fence to assist him, his
crutch snapped, and he fell, the barbed wire catching him
in the face and nose. There he hung until the skin broke
and _he fell on to his injured leg. Then a swarm of
soldier ants attacked him, and, urged by the agony of
their stings, he made one last desperate effort to reach
civilisation, and, after crawling another mile, was dis-
covered and saved.

Government Notices.
Public Works Department,
Sydney, Jan. 17, 1901.
CITY KAILWAY EXTENSION AND DEVONSHIRE
STREET, CEMETERY.
P having been determined to extend the City Railway
to Devonshire-street, notice is hereby given that in
all cases where application has beeh made to'the Minister
of Justice, by the representatives of deceased persons
buried in the Devonshire-street Cemetery, and permission
obtained to exhume such bodies with the sanction of the
Cemetery Trustees within two months from this date, the
Department of Public Works will bear all reasonable
expenses incurred in the re-interment of such bodies in
such Cemetery as the said representatives may desire.
The cost of removing and re-erecting all monuments,
stone-work, etc., now in a fair state of preservation, will
also be borne by the Government.
In the case of those bodies for which no applications
will have been received at the expiration of this notice,
exhumation will be carried out by the Government and
re-interment effected at Rookwood Cemetery.
Forms of application and other information may be
obtained on application to the Clerk in Charge of Bonds
and Contracts at this Department.
ROBERT HICKSON,
Under Secretary for Public Works and
Commissioner for Roads.

“Bushman ” : Can any Bulletin reader
rive me any information about a drover (or
drovers) who perished of thirst, together with a
mob of sheep, in N.S.W., about five years ago ?
Have reason to believe that a relative of mine met
such an end.
Some idea as to the unearned wealth of
the dissipating class in England (for Scotland and
Ireland are small items in this connection) can be
drawn from the fact that Sam Lewis, the
“ society ” money lender, left a fortune of
£4,000,000, derived mostly from discounting the
bills of white-handed wasters. And yet Sam’s
clients represented, probably, not more than five
per cent, of the thousands of “ swells born to
the inheritance, certain, or almost certain, of pro-
putty in land, chattels, and hard cash. Usurers
serve a godly purpose, anyhow. They are easy
mediums for the bursting-up of big estates, and
by dragging down the lordly from high places and
turning them loose among the crowd, they tend to
educate the multitude in the true meaning of a
good deal of class “distinction. 5 One often hears
it said by a rough toiler of a plucked pigeon :
** j[ ,3 was a real gentleman before he lorst his
money.”
This is a true tale of N.S.W. Public
Service :
In one of the departments under the Minister for Jus-
tice was an ambitious clerk whose salary was £245 a year.
To secure early promotion this officer passed the police-
magistrates’ examination, and armed with this fact and
the commendations of his direct superiors he presented
himself before the Board for promotion. The members
of the Board were all regrets, but firm. They pointed
out that the office, and not the occupier, was graded, and
that though the officer had undoubted claims to con-
sideration, still it was impossible for them to increase his
salary in the position he was holding—but the clerkship
of petty sessions in a western town was vacant. True,
the salarv was onlv £250 a year, but it was on the way to
a police-magistrate’s billet. So he was persuaded to take
it. Barely two weeks had elapsed before his successor in
the Sydney office was appointed. The man selected was
in the same department at the. same salary— -viz,, £245 a
year— a nd on assuming the duties of the position he was
advanced to £275 per annum 1

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Latest N.S.W.-Vio. cricket match is the 66th
of the senes. Opening day was remarkable for the small
scores made—N.S.W. 170, and Vic. 5 for 67, on a perfect
wicket (even though the bowling and fielding were first-
class) wants some explaining. Doings of second day
were in striking contrast. As against 15 for 237 on Friday
only 9 wickets fell for 362 on Monday. Attendance both
days was meagre, and evidenced unmistakably that the
public won’t stand being surfeited—even with cricket.
At time of writing it looks as if Stuckey’s 130 (not out)
will pull Victoria through.
* * * *
Friday saw crack Vic. bowler Hugh Trumble
bring his list of wickets secured in Vic.—N.S.W. matches
up to 126—an achievement much ahead of any other
bowler’s in the same competitions.
* * * *
Sammy Jones, one-time Sydney’s most popular
cricketer, stood as umpire during the last inter-State
match.
* * * •
The no-balling of aboriginal bowler Marsh in
Vic.-N.S.W. match on opening day (Friday last week)
created something of a sensation. During the first three
overs he offended half-a-dozen times, and was pulled up
on about ten occasions in seven overs. Same umpire
(Crockett) no-balled the young Waverley bowler Howard
twice on the same day. The trouble over unfair bowling
in England has caused Sydney cricket scribes to suddenly
discover what many of the players themselves knew long
ago, viz., that there’s plenty of it in and around the
metropolis.
** . *
“ H.S.” : In the recent Vic. v. N.S.W. cricket-
match at Sydney, aboriginal bowler Marsh was no-
balled no fewer than 4 times in one over (3 times con-
secutively). Viewed even from.a distance, one at least of
these deliveries was a palpable “ throw nevertheless
the rowdy portion of the small attendance cheered on the
darkey—inviting him to “ chuck ’em,” and to “bowl
underarms,-’’ at.the same time freely hooting the umpire.
Crockett’s action, however, was fully justified. Noble
was also no-balled several times by Sammy Jones, the
N.S.W. umpire, as were also Trumble and others ; and on
the whole, it looks as if there is amongst bowlers of “ the
day ” either great carelessness or a growing peculiarity of
delivery that requires more of such prompt and decided
treatment as Crockett’s. The N.S.W. fielding, by the
way, was, on the Whole, no more than middling—always
excepting Noble and his little skipper, and perhaps a
couple more. Leg-hitting on both sides was wretched
during the first half of the match, and perhaps the most
sensational incident was the fine catch by Worrall which
dismissed Gregorv—at the time batting splendidly.
* * * *
A rule lately adopted by the M.C.C., Eng., is
that a bowler can be “ no-balled ” by either umpire. This
law has not been adopted in Australia yet. Its wisdom
can be understood from the incident in last inter-State
match. If, to dodge the criticism of Crockett, Sid.
Gregory, had changed Marsh to the other end, the Sydney
umpire would have been placed in an invidious position—
viz., he would have had to pass judgment on his brother-
official’s decisions. As a matter of fact, Marsh was not
transferred to the opposite crease on Friday as he would
have had a head wind to bowl against.
* * * *
G. B. Fry, on “bowling,” in a recent issue of
Manchester (Eng;) Athletic News : “ The Australians
are held up by some writers as a pattern to us home-
grown cricketers in point of legitimacy of bowling-action.
Now, one of the best of the bowlers in the last Australian
team, in the opinion of many county cricketers, had a
very doubtful delivery—and the man was not Jones.
Having mentioned Jones, I may say that I never saw
him bowl an illegitimate ball.”
* * * *
Pye, the Central Cumberland cricketer, aban-
dons the single-wicket game : he has got married.
The death of Queen Victoria robbed N.S.W.
Cricket Association of a ton of gate-money. Hastily
postponing the match from Anniversary Day the Associa-
tion unfortunately re-fixed the match for the day of uni-
versal mourning. Common decency compelled it to
abandon a second time Saturday’s gate.
* * * *
The reported heavy backing and comparatively
short price of Malster for the Newmarket sets one think-
ing. Highest weight yet humped by a winner in that
hard-to-pick event was Bungebah’s 9.3. Malster’s burden
is 9.5, so that if he scores ’twill be a record. Boz carried
the biggest load (10.1) into a place when he ran second to
Churchill. Newmarket Handicaps decided so far total
27. Nags with Bst. upwards appropriated 12, and only
twice have those having 7st. and less in the saddle landed
the money.
* * * *
The racing postponements over the Queen’s
death are likely to be very expensive to some of the clubs .
concerned, especially as regards losing Anniversary Day.
* » * •
Vic. acceptances on Monday were numerous.
Futurity Stakes has 21 left in, including the cracks Por-
tree, Fides, Finland, Debenture, Aurous, Malster, George
Frederick and Tarquin, who’ll make the race as interest-
ing as ever it was. Most notable withdrawals from New-
market Hep.—Bobadil, Lancaster, Fleet Admiral, The
Chief, Reliance, Cornquist, Ampier, Warrior 11., Duke of
Kent, Town Clock, Isa, Kenley, Carline, First Mate* Flor-
ence, Mylae, Horonda, Nikola, Strathjoy, Ruskin, Patina,
and High Jinks. Paul Pry and the M.L. champion, Ad-
vance, head the list with 9.11 each. The Watchdog is
nominal favorite. Top weight in Australian Cup—La
Carabine—dropped out and left the position to her stable
mate, Paul Pry. Others from Scobie’s quarters still in
are Malster, Clean Sweep, The Bride and Dreamland,
while War God, Merriwee, Seahorse, Khaki, Miltiades,
Horace, First Mate, Warrior 11., Lord Curreton, Juggler
and Hovis figure among the eliminations. Scratchings in
V.R.C. St. Leger were plentiful, the M.L. nags Cavalry
and Military being among them.
* * * •
The arrival of crack Westralian nag Australian
at Adelaide attracts attention to the widely-different
estimates of his capabilities held by N.S.W.' and Vic.
handioappers. In the Doncaster he has 8.4, and in the
Newmarket 9st. If dealt with fairly in the big 6-furlong
event there ought to be money in backing Australian for
the N.S.W. mile.
• * • *
“Q.”: Has been written in several places that
crack Q. mare Ma-Ma, which recently scooped the “King-
ston ” and “ Dickson ” Stakes at Rosohill (Sydney), is by
“ Woodstock, a son of Monmouth,” Ac. Let me correct
this. Ma-Ma, ch. m. by Woodstock- Minnehaha, Wood-
stock, ch. h. by Epigram—Welcome by Maribyrnong,
Minnehaha by Vespasian—Queen of the Forest, by Bara-
tarian. Queen of the Forest was drowned at Mt. Brisbane
in the floods (if my memory serves) ’93. The dam of
Minnehaha was bought at auction by Mr. H. M. Hughes,
of Culcraigie station, Burnett (Q-), at the same time as
Mr. W. Kent, of Dalgangal station, Burnett, purchased
Stockdove. Stockdove subsequently had a colt foal—
Prime Minister—and Queen of the Forest had a filly foal
—Minnehaha. Both (at 2yrs.) were trained by Mr. Kent,
but Minnehaha went wrong and never raced. Prime
Minister was eventually so.d to Messrs. Proudfoot and
Fletcher for 2000 gs. Mr. G. Bailey (Cannindah station)
purchased Minnehaha, and Ma-Ma is her first foal by
Woodstock. Woodstock is one of the sires at Cannindah,
and has been there for years. T’other Q. nag (Fortune
Teller), now in Sydney, is by the Dalgangal station sire
Othello by Epigram—Olive.
Looks as if Bill of Portland is going to prove a
big spec for his English buyer. The crack sire’s list for
this year filled quickly at lOOgs. per mare, and subs, are
coming in fast for next and following seasons at same
figure.
* • m •
Sydney footballer-pug. Conlon has gone through
the tare experience of being the winner in an unusually
brief battle and the loser in a much briefer one within a
few days. Tuesday, last week, he had a soft thing with
Starlight, at Golden Gate A.C. ; the dark bruiser crying
enough, owing to the usual broken wrist, ere the fourth
round was half through. Monday night, this week, at
Gaiety A. 0., “ Snowy ” Sturgeon did the cyclone act with
Oonlon, whom he hustled and harried from the jump.
In the first round, Sturgeon’s elbow lodged violently in
Oonlon’s right eye, jarring it very badly. Conlon was
coming out of his corner for the second tussle when
Sturgeon met him with a vimful right on the point, the
effect of which was to send Conlon full length on his back
for many seconds over the allotted 10, and caused Stur-
geon to stagger back in his tracks with a badly-damaged
bunch of knuckles. The house—the biggest yet seen at
Sydney Gaiety—was absolutely crammed from floor to
roof. The crowd only saw 163 sec. of actual fight for its
money, and yet there wasn’t an audible growl.
• * * *
“Jab”: Can’t fathom Sydney bruiser Jack
Oonlon. When Mick Dunn met him he got a bad attack
of the shakes, early ; ditto in the match with Joe Cullen,
who looked a “moral” for Jack right up to the time
Oonlon sat on the floor grinning and allowed himself to
be counted out. Yet Conlon’s displays at football
evidence grit above the ordinary, and then there was
that getting up after being spread-eagled by a fearful
punch on the back of the neck and ultimately licking his
opponent—Captain’s Flat miner Dan. O’Connell. Conlon
looked nervous from the start in his fight with Sturgeon
on Monday night and good judges tipped defeat for him
inside 3 rounds. He ought now to give the game up for
good.
• * • *
The second match between little ’uns Billy
M’Kell and boy Bert Weekes at Golden Gate A.C. (Syd-
ney) Saturday night drew a crammed house. M’Kell’s
poundage was given as 8.6, but on appearances he topped
that by at least lOlbs. Weekes scaled Sst. Operations
were fast and clever from the jump, but Weekes had a
heap of points to the good at the end of the third round.
In the fourth M’Kell made heavy impact on the “ point,”
spread-eagling the bantam for 7sec. Only the dodge of
falling (not allowed by the rules, but evidently unob-
served by the referee) enabled Weekes to see the round
out. Though the crowd howled for Weekes, and Weekes
alone, from end to end (his great grit and boyish face
have made him a pet of the public) superior strength and
weight told its tale and his power petered out fast, till
the eighth round, when a bump sent him prone, and the
referee, sizing matters up, gave M’Kell the palm. Those
responsible for matching Weekes against M’Kell a
second time should have their bumps felt. Previously
M’Kell was bound to a weight-limit, and then he won
convincingly. This time there was no such restriction.
Weekes will beat any man in his class (bantam) in Aus-
tralia, but giving away a stone or more is much too big a
task for a youngster of his streaky build.
•*» • *
Seventeen-vear-old “ Bob ” Turner and 37-year-
old Hughie Napier put up a tough tussle at the Golden
Gate A.C. (Sydney) last week. Stakes were £5O a-side and
a purse of £5O, all of which went to the winner. Match
was the outcome of talk, and the milk-trade of Sydney
split over the question of relative superiority—supplying
the backing in “fivers,” “tenners,” &c.', for each.
Turner had the hardest task yet set him. Often the pair
stood close in and belted away viciously and spitefully, for
there were heaps of bile at the bottom' of it all. Napier
was rushed from the perpendicular to the horizontal a
dozen times but came up smiling and full of ginger after
each fall. Yells, hoots, and cheers from equally-divided
partisans made an awful din. Veteran Napier lost his
head and battered away fruitlessly at the barricade of
elbows and fists Turner erected round his face while
’’ smothering up,” which practice he resorted to often.
Napier fell off from the eighth round and, in the eleventh,
after being laid out three or four times, his seconds caved
in.
* • • *
Apropos the Napier-Turner bruising-match at
Sydney. Writer remembers old-man Napier (who con-
fesses to 37, but must be over 40) fighting George Powell
(how in W.A.) at the whilom Sydney Gymnastic Olub
10 years back, and winning when ’twas thought almost a
“cert.” for Powell. Just previously Powell had been
beaten by Griffo, and his crowd lost a pile of money. A
bookmaker backer of Powell’s was said to have been
ruined over the match. The affair with Napier was
arranged as a sort of balm for the defeated man’s feelings,
but, after a hot struggle and some fierce punching,
t’other man got the verdict, though he was a bit lucky.
Subsequently Napier visited U.S., and did well therefor
a couple of years. Had Napier trained longer and not let
his feelings get the better of his judgment, the latest
match should have been a fairly soft thing for him, old
and all as he is. Any man with a good left will have
little trouble in accounting for Turner.

Cribb’s second match in America was fixed for
Jan. 18, when he and one Thurston were to meet for a
500dol. purse. So confident were both that they decided
there should be no breaking-up of the money—winner
must have all. Thurston’s methods resemble Orilb’s ;
hence something of the hottest was anticipated. Indica-
tions were that the Yankee would start favorite, which
ought to admirably suit the Oribb party.

’Frisco Examiner (loq.J: “When they sent
Otto Cribb over from Australia they said he was a fighter.
He is. Oribb is a man who is likely to whip almost any
welter-weight he meets, and he is likely to get beaten
himself by almost any of them.” Which is exactly how
the best Australian judges size Oribb up.

The gall of some pugs! Billy M’Carthy, who
couldn’t hold his own with ninth-rate Jack Tuckwell of
Sydney recently, is in M.L., and notifies per medium of a
showy circular (with his own photo, as the central set-off)

country previous to his departure for America, where he

world ! Great Scot!
* • * *
The high-falutin challenges thrown out by that
lathy darkey Felix are getting him into a hot corner.
His latest was aimed at Starlight and Joe Cullen, whom
the large-headed black said he could whip in the one ring.
Starlight retorts that though he’s verging on 50 and has
been 16 years at the game he’ll gladly take the big coon
on single-handed anytime and feel that he has a soft snap.
Cullen says : “ Him ! He fight Starlight and me in the
same ring ! Never mind about Starlight. I’ll fight Felix
myself, and I don’t want a penny if he stops me inside 10
rounds. He might outpoint me but he couldn’t hurt a
baby. If Felix’ll fight me P.R. rules I have a backer
who’ll bet £lOO I win.”
* * * *
U.S. mail brings news that Fitzsimmons isn’t
taking any more of the P.R.—he’s out of it for good. “ I
have a wife whose feelings must be considered and chil-
dren whose future is in my hands,” said the freckled
freak. “As a middle-weight I have beaten the world,
and as a middle-weight I have held my own with the
heavy-weights. Only when there’s no bread in the house
will I tackle the game again.” It’s just as well that Fitz.
has decided this way, for his lately-developed habit of
dashing in and taking chances would have suited giant
Jeffries down to the ground.
Vancouver mail tells that Paddy Ryan (the man
from whom J. L. Sullivan got the U.S. championship 19
years ago) died Dec. 14 of Bright’s disease. Twenty-five
years ago Ryan was top of the heap in Yankeedom. His
match with Sullivan was for 5000dols. a-side and under
London P.R. rules. Sullivan won in 9 rounds. Ryan
took the title from Goss, after an 87-round struggle.
Sullivan’s win quite wrecked Ryan—who was an Irishman
born in Tipperary almost 48 years ago.
m * * •
“9.5.”: That “Sporting Notion” (Feb. 2) re
the road-rights of cyclists is all wrong, because arguments
are based on the assumption that Melb. trams run in the
middle of the road. A cyclist can pass a tram going the
same way as himself on his left hand, and still be on his
right side. The crown of the road is between the two
sets of tram-lines. No doubt it was on this ground that
the cyclist won in the case cited.
• * • •
That hideous brutality, the big Yankee six days’
international cycle grind, ended in N’York, 15th Dec.,
before 12,000 people. The finish was sensational. Ameri-
can team (Elkes and 11‘Farland) won by 12ft. only from
Canucks Pierce and M’Eachern. Winners traversed 2628
miles 7 laps. Canadians got over the same distance.
Frenchmen were only one lap away while the Germans
covered 2596 miles. Seven teams withdrew ere the
struggle closed, and many riders were in hospital, at
latest, suffering from heavy falls and other accidents.
Nearly all those who survived the thing presented a piti-
able appearance from exhaustion.
• • • »
Pro. cyclists are busily engaged in smothering
their means of livelihood, and look like proving as
successful as were the peds, scullers, and ’rastlers years
ago. Reported that some prize money won at a recent
big meeting was split up into a “ divvy ” of 11s. 7d. each,
and that the money for one important handicap was
whacked among 12 of the starters.
* * * *
At Prospect (N.S.W.) fish-“hatchery,” there
are at present 750 fine rainbow-trout, which will be two
years old in Sept. next. A trout of this variety, recently
found dead in Prospect reservoir, weighed 3ilb. ; and an
81b. brown-trout was, the other day, taken from the Mac-
quarie River. Also, in the Bibbenluke River, Monaro
district, were recently captured (with the rod) six rainbow-
trout, averaging nearly 31b.
m 'm * *
First of present year Australian swimming
supremacies (the quarter-mile) was decided at Hegarty’s
Baths, Melbourne, Monday. Seven —Pearson and Creaton
(Vic.), Cavill, Read and Craig (N.S.W.), Champion (M.L.),
and Hogan (Q.) —lined up. Cavill won from the start
(and the three N.S.W. men finished as named) in 5.53 2-5
—a fresh record for the continent, topping the previous
by 4-ssec., and only l-ssec. slower than the English
fastest, done by J. H. Tyers in ’94. Cavill will probably
win every event he starts in during the week, as his
present form is exceptionally good. At Goulburn
(N.S.W.), t’other day, he put up 1.3 2-5 for 100yds.

Don Walker, famous throughout Australia as
the man who beat Zimmy, Bill Martin, and other cycling
arena gladiators, is now mounted on a Red Bird bicycle.*
In case George Reid should desire to make
him a birthday present, The Bulletin hastens to
inform him that Sunday (Feb. 10) is the natal day
of B. Rather Wise.
Dry-dog Reid, now that he has secured
a N.S.W. Freetrade Council composed of his
“own ticks,” is inviting the members of
N.S.W. Freetrade Association to join his
Committee. He first snuffed them out at
his hole-and-corner meeting, and then tried
to propitiate them by appointing a few to differ-
ent committees on which they would have no
power to interfere with, his “ticks.” As this
move does not seem to have bull-dozed
the more independent freetraders, he is play-
ing another card. He is forming a large
committee—with no executive power—to organise
and find funds, while the Council composed of the
ticks pulls the string against all and sundry who
don’t fall into line. Freetraders with any Federal
inclinations will be sent against the strong men
whom the “ticks” daren’t tackle. If they go
under, as most of them will, they will be victims
to party loyalty, while Dry-dog and his ticks will
“loyally” enjoy the fun.
“ Melbourneite ” : The death of Queen
Victoria was a sad event, but that was no reason
for the Vic. Postal Dept, to make it irritating.
On receipt of the news at 11 a.m., the G.P.O.
issued instructions for all post-offices to close, with
the result that hundreds of people who had
counted on receiving money, by p. 0.0. or postal-
note, on the following day were compelled to wait
another 24 hours for the remittance. Also, tele-
graphic communication was unexpectedly cut off,
and the savings-banks went practically bung for
the time being, and public convenience suffered
awfully because the Monarch had died on a
Tuesday evening instead of a Saturday
afternoon. Ten days later the funeral was
made an occasion for a close holiday at the
postal, telegraph, and money-order offices. No
letters were delivered from Friday afternoon till
Monday morning. Saturday’s express train to
Sydney severed its valuable connection with the
mail service, and so a great many persons had to
feel the loss of the Queen as a personal annoyance
m order that a score, at the most, of the G.P.O.
employes might not be required to put in a little
work. To voluntarily regret the death and conse-
quent funeral of the Queen was one thing, but to
have needless lamentations forced upon one by the
Government was quite another thing, and people
objected to it.
The Bulletin draws the attention of the elec-
tors of Parramatta and adjacent parts to the candidature
of Mr. W. Sandford to represent that seat in the Federal
House of Representatives. Mr. Sandford, whose senti-
ments are more fully expressed in another part of this
issue, is the man who runs the Eskbank Iron Works, and
has kept that industry afloat in spite of enormous difficul-
ties. He has learned by bitter experience how much
Freetrade assists local industry—down the hill. His
platform is soundly democratic,'and is that advocated bv
The Bulletin— the same by which Queen Elizabeth laid
the foundations of England’s commercial and maritime
supremacy. In those days England was a country with a
small, scattered population which had hitherto lived by
exporting raw material, and the good and wise Queen
Bess commenced the new era by inaugurating a system
of rigid Protection. Mr. Sandford advocates uncom-
promising Protection to aid our own manufacturers
and keep the making of our own goods in our own
country. He also supports Arbitration and Wages Boards ;
legislation against all colored or undesirable immigrants ;
Federal control of the Railways and the building of
strategic and commercial lines to connect Port Dar-
win with Brisbane on the one side and Perth on the
other. These railways, under Federal control, can be
used to encourage and foster the baby industries of the
Continent, as is done in Germany with such deadly effect
on British trade. Mr. Sandford is a believer in Adult
Suffrage, and wishes to make Australia a prosperous
White Man’s land, which can clothe, keep, and amuse
itself absolutely during either peace or war.*
Dry-Dog and co. are sending Ashton
against Chanter for Kaverina! Chanter was a
prominent Federal worker, and nearly every
elector in the division is a Federalist. Does
somebody want to see Ashton come a cropper?
Descriptive flowers from S.M. Herald’s
cabled account of the royal obsequies :
From the wharf at Cowes the petty officers of the Royal
yacht Alberta bore the coffin aboard the Royal yacht
through the escort of Grenadiers amid the booming of
muffled guns.
The harbor was entered by the funeral procession a few
minutes before five o’clock. The torpedo boat-destroyers
on either side pf the Alberta moved in jerky dubitant
march, and looked a weird-like escort in the sundown.
Muffled guns! They must have meant muffled
buns.
There is an amusing little political comedy
being played in Melbourne just now. The Age is
barracking almost daily for calling Parliament
together to pass an address of condolence on
account of the death of Victoria. The Akgcs
gets red in the face with indignation at this pro-
posal, and says it will be time enough to condole
when Parliament meets in the ordinary course in
June. But that, of course, is not the real reason.
If Parliament were called together now, the
Radicals in the Leg. Assembly would insist on re-
sending to the Council the democratic measures
which that House of Fossils has impudently thrown
out again and again ; and if the Council rejected
them once more, public indignation would be so
great that the members of that body who are
candidates for the Federal Senate would not stand
the ghost of a show. Ordinarily the ultra-loyal
Argus would bellow lustily for a special meeting
to pass an address—but Policy before Loyalty.
Another 2000 troops for the Transvaal are
being collected in N.S. W. No consent of
Parliament has been asked or given, and
one thousand of the troops are apparently
sent more or less “on spec.” These men
are drafted from peaceful industries, where
they are helping to make Australia, and sent
away to be wasted or maimed or slaughtered in
war, or enterically rotted, at the mere nod
of Messrs. Lyne, See, and French. The
better English of two hundred years ago
rose in revolt against the monarchs who
attempted to enlist troops without the con-
sent of Parliament. It is one of the cardinal
maxims of English constitutional government that
the enlistment of troops is in the province of the
people only. Yet here in Australia, through the
weakness of popular representatives, and the
apathy of the people, we are yielding to influence-
hunters and title-hunters a power which the
English have defied tyrants to usurp. And the
incapable Lyne, having muddled away hundreds
of thousands of pounds in the so-called Common-
wealth celebration, after having received indefinite
Parliamentary authority to spend some £20,000
—having muddled it away, thrown it away, from
sheer incapacity to control expenditure; having
incurred enormous liabilities for a Sydney wharf-
age-resumption scheme which he has not the
courage or the savee to carry out; having,
momentarily at least, bankrupted the Treas-
ury by such a mammoth waste of money
as has never been paralleled in Australia
before, has just floated £500,000 of Treasury
bills to fill his empty exchequer borrow-
ing the money where a splendid revenue
in the hands of a financier with an atom of sense,
would have made borrowing unnecessary ; bor-
rowing it to pay his daily liabilities in defiance
of the principles of sound government. And
this ten-times-convicted incapable, who hangs
on to his State Premiership, and its privi-
leges and prerogatives, while undertaking a
Federal Ministerial post, which a dozen men such
as he could not adequately fill, has the impudence
to go and ask the electors of Hume to return him
to the Federal Parliament. Good Lord ! what is
this country coming to ?

Microbes and Disease.
The normal condition of all life, animal or
vegetable, is health. We were all intended by
Nature to be healthy, and, if we can, by care,
keep all the mechanism of our bodies in complete
working order, it is doubtful whether any disease
can establish itself. The most virulent microbe
would be unable to effect a lodgment in a perfectly
sound and healthy body, or, even if it did, the
white corpuscles of the blood, whose province it is
to deal with such an intruder, would immediately
assemble in such force, and make so vigorous an
attack, that the microbe would be exterminated
and absorbed before it could do any serious
mischief. What gives the microbe its chance is
an enfeebled condition of the vital forces. It
attacks a citadel, the defences of which are in
some parts weak ; establishes a foothold, and then
proceeds to multiply by dividing itself into two or
more parts. These parts again divide in a similar
manner until what was originally one microbe
becomes tens of millions. Our first great care
should be to keep the body in thorough repair.
The organs most susceptible to weakness are the
kidneys and liver. We catch a slight cold. The
liver and kidneys become congested, and cannot
properly perform their functions, and there is an
accumulation in the system of waste products,
which would ordinarily be carried off through
their natural channels, if the kidneys and liver
were doing their duty. The result is Rheumatism,
Indigestion, Dyspepsia, Impure Blood, Bilious-
ness, Jaundice, Ansemia, Gravel, Gout, Neuralgia,
perhaps Bright’s Disease. All these disorders are
due to defective action of the kidneys and liver.
Warner’s Safe Cure possesses a wonderfully specific
action upon those vital organs, and speedily
restores them to health, with the result that any
complaint due to their inactivity can no longer
prevail.
Mr. Reginald Parnell, of Barcaldine, Queens-
land, in a letter dated November, 5, 1900, says:
“I suffered for a short time, but severely, from
indigestion, which caused great pain in my chest
and between the shoulders. After passing several
sleepless nights, I determined to try Warner’s
Safe Cure, and am pleased to say that the pains
have entirely left me.”*

Westralians, on Tuesday of last week, showed
another slump in everything. On Tuesday this week
there was a boom, but it is noticeable that, while every
slump takes them lower than they were before, every
succeeding boom leaves them somewhat behind
the previous one. Associateds, £2 145.—155. higher
than last Tuesday, and 15s. means an increased valu-
ation of upwards of £400,000. Northern Associateds £2 15s.
—lss higher than they were a week ago, representing
an increased valuation of about a quarter-million. Main
Reefs £2, a rise of 6s. ; Perseverances, £7 125., a rise of
£2, which means £350,000; last week they dropped
£2. Boulders, £1 4s. 6d., a rise of 2s. fid. bo
the mine is worth on the market £218,760. more
this week than last. Fingall Oonsolidateds, £6 7s. 6d.
—a rise of about 10s. for the week. Ivanhoes,
£8 12s. 6d. ; last Tuesday they were £6 55., and £2 7s. od.
means an increased market valuation of nearly halt-a-
million. What has happened in the mine to make this
change, or in any of the others for the matter of that
Absolutely nothing, so far as people know. Lake Views,
£7 ss.—onlv £2 higher than a week ago, and £2 is exactly
half-a-million. South Kalgurlis, £2 15s.—about 10s.
higher than last week.

British Exploration Co. of .Australasia: The
last successful float in London is all on account of
A. E. Morgans, who can do much as he likes with the
London market —which cannot be said of any other man.
Capital, half-a-million in the same number of shares;
object, to take over mines, gold, copper and tin, in
different parts of Westralia; purchase pnce fixed by
Morgans, who is the vendor, at £310,000, payable £60,000
in cash, and £250,000 fully-paid shares. “ The vendor
will pay all expenses in connection with the formation of
the co. including registration and brokerage,” which is
very good of him. Working capital provided by this
issue, £90,000, and 100,000 shares reserved to provide
further working capital when required. The £90,000
won’t go far to work so many mines in so expensive
a mining country. They are mostly isolated, and though
Morgans has been successful in two or three places he will
be different from any other mining man found in Westra-
lia if he is successful everywhere. The list includes the
Nullagine conglomerates in Marble Bar district, said to
resemble the banket formation of the Rand. They are
represented as being worth over 4 an oz. to the ton.
Stated that hundreds of tons have been worked for this
average, although a considerable quantity of gold got
away in the tailings, and it is declared that 6dwt. per ton
will more than pay all expenses. It may be news to Mr.
Morgans to know that these deposits were examined by a
very big mining man indeed seven years ago, and if they
had showed anything like lOdwt. they would have been
taken up and worked on a much bigger scale than is now
proposed.

Mt. Lyell Blocks : Stated at last meeting, held
in London, that 6475 tons had been .delivered to Mt.
Lyell smelters, which realised over £46,000 gross—all the
result of less than five months* work. Another contract
arranged for smelting, and regular parcels going forward.
Proposed to lay out £4OOO in shaft-sinking and other im-
provements. Upwards of in hand.
Mt. Lyell West : Chairman admitted in London
recently that though they had one body of ore of con-
siderable value in itself, it was absolutely unworkable by
itself and they were looking for a deposit of pyntic ore.
Even if they failed in finding it the position was not
exactly hopeless because in the southern portions of the
field there were mines in a similar difficulty, i.e., they
had pyritic ore and nothing to flux it with, while that of
the Lyell West was silicious without pyntic ore. total
amount spent to date, £5lBB. Little encouragement
given by the last expert who reported. Said in effect
that copper had been found but without regularity or
quantity. Believed no large bodies of rich ore
would be found, but there were prospects of opening up
a fairly large body of low grade, and there was also a
chance of picking up the South Tharsis silicious ore body
towards the northern boundary. Briefly it might be thus
summed up : If the London shareholders consider only
the interest of Mt. Lyell field they will continue to put
up. If they consider only their own they had better shut
up.
r * * * *
Great Cobar Copper: The beginning of the end.
Resolution passed for the winding-up of the old co. ; con-
firmation only wanted. The tributors, represented by W.
and T. Longworth and Dr. Read, have exercised their
option— i.e., they have got the mine for a modest half-
million. Several interesting features in connection with
this sale have been kept as dark as the lungs of midnight.
About two-thirds of the purchase money is retained by
the tributors by reason of their two-thirds interest in the
co. ; in other words, they paid £500,000 with one hand
and took two-thirds of it back with the other. They
started buying the shares at half-a-crown, and although
they did not leave off at £4 their average pnce was pro-
bably under £2. They were seriously checkmated nearly
three years ago by the co. letting another tribute over
their heads on the basis of a heavy nett royalty—a most
unusual proceeding, and to all appearances unwarranted
by the circumstances. Under the terms of that tribute
the old tributors imagined it could be cancelled for some
£lO,OOO. Rumor says that it cost them £40,000 ; that the
price went up with every day’3 delay, and what it would
have been to-day had they not closed for the higher
amount few people know. The worries of this concern
are all over now. The tributors have made an immense
f ortune, the result of their own unaided exertions. They
have paid over a quarter-of-a-million in royalties and they
take over a mine at half-a-million which had practically
no value when they took it up. The remaining share-
holders, after receiving many divs., will now get a final one
of over £4 a share, and the nominal tributors have had
the pleasure of collecting £40,000 as the result of a
** move ** made and carried out without risk and “as
easy as kissing your hand.”
An Adelaide tradesman, as a sign of
mourning for his Queen, emptied one of his shop
windows, carpeted and draped it in black, and
put in it a huge marble tombstone, festooned with
funeral wreaths. Also, a local theatre hung its
proscenium with crape and then went right on
with the nigger-minstrel show.
The “ exceptionally well-conducted ” Im-
perial soldiers who visited Australia left Mel-
bourne with over 400 of their number under
arrest. The bulk of the offences were of course so
trivial as to appear insignificant to civilians, but
nearly 20 of the prisoners were transferred from
watchhouses where they had been lodged mainly
for unprovoked assaults and insults. Col. Wynd-
ham told a Melb. interviewer that his men com-
plained that everybody in Melbourne wanted to
fight them. The truth was that the people of
Melbourne did not feel inclined to submit to the
frequent remarks of the soldiers regarding their
legitimacy. The police opinion is that the thou-
sand Britishers are the worst-conducted set of
men ever seen in the city and that, if they are
good-conduct men, a visit from ordinary British
soldiers would have meant annihilation to either
the city or the visitors.
At Povery Point.
A correspondent asks if the closing of the theatres on
account of the Queen’s death meant that that unfortunate
class, the minor actors and tail-end people of the profes-
sion, had to lose a night’s wage, which they can ill spare.
Another view is that a good many managers can ill
afford to pay a night’s expenses while getting no receipts ;
plenty of managers, despite all appearances, live most of
their time on the edge of a financial precipice. Did the
landlords, by any chance, forego the night’s rent ? And
if they did, did anyone let them off a day’s interest on
the mortgage ?
Smiler Hales has embarked as war-lecturer.
Melbourne Aoe is crying out for a vigorous policy in the
management, of the local Exhibition Building, as though
that “ white elephant” institution were favorably circum-
stanced for all sorts and conditions of show business. In
the first place, the Exhibition Building is out of the way
of the large majority of the public that spends much
money in the purchase of entertainment. It is cold and
cheerless in the winter. It is only permitted an occa-
sional liquor license, under restrictions imposed through
. the protests of neighboring pub.-keepers. The mere
washing of the floor of the building costs £2O a time, or
more, and the incidental expenses of gatemen, door-
keepers, attendants and police are very great.
Talking of “ Joseph of Canaan ” and the refusal of a
license to play it in Great Britain on the ground that an
admittedly Biblical story is not eligible for production,
the London correspondent of a Sydney paper remarks the
obvious fact that “ the spiciness of a Biblical play lies in
the names.” Give other names to Joseph and Mrs. Poti-
phar, and at once the lady’s attempted seduction of the
good young man becomes a quite commonplace theatri-
cal incident. The naughtiness must have a cloak
of religion, otherwise it can’t draw such mobs of
prurient, unsophisticated humbugs as gloated over “ The
Sign of the Cross.” They must have a “beautiful
moral lesson ” and they want it “ blue.” The repulsive
“ story ” of Lot’s daughters would probably make an
enormously successful play if it were cleverly written
and presented in Biblical form. If any other named
character than old man Lot were loaded up with liquor
by his spinster daughters for immoral purposes the Ker-
ristian world would howl for a prosecution of the mana-
ger. But a play that introduced the wreck of Sodom and
Gomorrah in the second act, and subsequently showed
the Misses Lot retiring to an inner room on the
mission recorded in Holy Writ would fill a long-felt want.
Miss Jennie Opie, as the big, manly, striding middy in
Bland Holt’s drama, sets one thinking of what the late
J. B. Buckstone said of Mrs. Chippendale in “ The Over-
land Mail ” long ago. Old Buckstone was a privileged
person in the matter of “broad” jokes, and Mrs. Chip,
was about as buxom as they make ’em. “My wife,” said
the unctuous comedian, “is an angel. In fact, she’s
better than an angel, for she has something to sit down
upon.”
Demise : Vanderfelt the English actor. 1-Ie had the
misfortune to become attached to Miss Fortescue in a
dual capacity, first as leading man, secondly as ardent
lover. She was a business woman, carrying the Garmoyle
aroma round, and had no idea of allowing the dog to
lie where the deer ought to be. So poor Vanderfelt might
enact Romeo, on the boards, to his heart’s content, but
off them there was a wall of ice. His example was Wilson
Barrett. Twenty years ago, or a little more, the whole
profession agreed that Barrett never would capture the
great Miss Heath, reader by appointment to H.M. Queen
Victoria—but he did ; and she was not so happy as she
expected.
“ Sfogato ” in reply : “ E.” (B. 19/1/1) on the somewhat
unsubstantial basis of “ two programmes,” ridicules my
assertion (B. 12/1/1) that “ of French, Italian, or German
pupils, Madame Marchesi has none.” Not precisely my
words —but, never mind. lam aware that Marchesi has
had a very few French pupils; for instance, her own
daughter, Blanche, who—having sung in Paris at one of
Lamoureux’s concerts—had the following courteous re-
marks written about her by Marcel, in the Journal pour
Tous : “ Good God 1 How has Mme. Marchesi had the
audacity to approach such famous musical numbers with
vocal qualifications so absolutely nil I —with an organ of
such disagreeable quality—painful in the upper register,
completely wanting in sonority in the middle, and
with lower notes so mean? Her voice bleats,
goat-like, throughout its whole extent; enunciation
she has none ; and her mouth opens cavernously in the
high notes, only to emit a thin thread of voice, without,
consistency, and without quality. In what school has.
Mdme. Marchesi learnt to produce sound in such an exas-
perating fashion ? Whence such cruel heaviness of style ?
Rough ! but I see it is more or less corroborated by several!
other leading Parisian critics. “ E.” also, remarks that
no teacher can turn out great singers from poor material.
True ; but a bad teacher often ruins good material.
“ E.” further states that, “of course, Italian voices don’t
need to leave their own country for,culture.” But many
Italians do. Ciampi-Cellaj (who was here with Oarlotta.
Patti) possibly the best concert baritone who has visited
us, was, although an Italian, a purely French vocalist,
and the school which has turned out artists like Faure,
Maurel, and Merly must naturally have a high reputation.
I don’t wish to deny to Marchesi whatever credit is due
to her, but I protest against the mischievous notion—
due to irresponsible, and, possibly, venal scribes—
that she is the great I Am of vocal tuition.
At the Paris Grand Opera and Opera Oomique are to be
heard a host of French-taught artists, compared with
whom the few really good singers whom Marchesi has.
produced are not to be mentioned. I’ve heard the former,
but “ E.” has “ seen two programmes ! ” As for the often-
cited Melba, she was an accomplished vocalist before she
went to Europe—thanks to the late Signor Ceechi. “As
for German voices,” proceeds “E.,” “no fashionable-
teacher outside Germany wants to be afflicted by them"
Yet Marchesi is herself a German, who married the Mar-
chese di Oastrone, and her present name is an adaptation
of his title. German singers who have studied in Ger-
many generally, it is true, lack vocal finish, but Germany
has for all that produced some of the finest voices the
world has heard. What of Tietjens, Stolz, and Waldmann ?'
—of the tenor Wachtelj and the basso Formes—all
celebrities in Italian opera. Finally, “ E.” “ knows that,
some of Marchesi’s star pupils within the past two years,
have been French and Russian.” Well, the Russians who
have attained any vocal eminence, outside their own
country could be counted on the fingers of one hand;
and, though I will not return “ E’s ” very gratuitous in-
sinuation of falsehood, I will say that it would be
interesting to learn the names of the “ star pupils” in
question.
Nance O’Neil, whom Melbourne people (women chiefly)
raved about a little time ago, but who did not altogether
thrill The Bulletin, receives very unkind treatment
from Stafford Ransome, who was “ doing ” Australia for
the London Sphere and Daily Express at the time. He
writes in the former paper that the bulky lady has “a.
rudimentary knowledge of acting,” and that when she
gets to London—if she ever does—“ both she and her
audiences will be disappointed by the results.”
Miss Amy Crawford, the Bendigo girl who was intro-
duced to Melbourne as a recitress at the time of the
Castles Boom, is now second lady in the Nance O’Neil Co.
Maoriland papers gave* the damsel warm praise for her
performance of Mrs. Vane in “ Masks and Faces,” wherein
also J. B. Atholwood scored a hit with his Triplet. The
substitution of Atholwood for Barton Hill, who played
the part (in Australia) for much less than its full worth,
is more understandable than the rapid promotion of Amy
Crawford. The dark-eyed girl from Bendigo has a tall,
fine appearance, charming elocution, and lots of talent,
and so, all things considered, the wonder is that—but
perhaps M‘Kee Rankin has secured her on a long lease.
The Bulletin hopes not. This country can’t afford to
lose any promising young actresses for the next few
years.

It Was Not His Fault.
The man who sits down to his supper and
refuses to eat it is not likely to rise in the esteem
of his wife or his cook. Excellent cooks have
thrown up their situations, and gone off in a huff
simply because the master of the nouse has casually
remarked that there was a trifle too much salt in
the soup.
Nevertheless, Mr. John Bennett, according to
his own story, failed to get any satisfaction out of
his meals for several years. Yet nobody
complained of him* because it was not his fault,
lie would not have dreaded the coming of a meal
time, as he actually did dread it, had he possessed
the power to choose his own feelings.
But alas ! a dtaf man may love music, or a blind
one long vainly for the sight of remembered
colours.
“ F rom 1884 to 1889, ” says Mr. Bennett, “ I was
a helpless victim of that tormenting and
incorrigible complaint—indigestion. How it came
on me at the outset I cannot say. It is like
waking up in the night and finding a thief in
your house. How he got in you may never
exactly discover—not even by the aid of the police.
“ What I do know is, that it annihilated my
appetite and spoiled my comfort. The little I
did worry down often came up again—undigested,
and consequently of no advantage to me.
“In fact, I dreaded the coming of meal time,
and wished it were possible to get along without
eating. _ But this is the horror of chronic
dyspepsia—that one must eat in order to live, and
that existence under such circumstances is scarcely
worth having.
‘‘During all those years—about fifteen of them
—I never knew what it was to be well. Of all
the medicines I resorted to, and they comprised
almost everything I heard of that had the
slightest hope in it, none did me any good; that
is, none went to the bottom of my trouble. Any
weary and hapless dyspeptic will understand what
I mean.
borne time in 1899 (just ten years ago now) I
bought a bottle of Mother Seigel’s Syrup of Mr.
Sept. Powell, the chemist here in Paddington.
He has been long in business in this place, and
can be trusted to recommend only what is good in
his line.
“I need only add that the result of my using
this medicine was far beyond my hopes or dreams
Before I had finished the first bottle I was better’
and after taking the Syrup a few weeks longer
I was cured. Yes, and really cured; for never
since then has a sign of my 'old trouble shown
itself.
“ What I think of Mother Seigel’s Syrup may
be inferred.”—John Bennett, 48, Begg Street
Paddington, Sydney, N.S.W., August 30th, 1899 *

AND
THE AGED.
At the Hunts (England) assizes, recently,
four young men, convicted of criminal cumulative
assau t upon a 14-year-old girl, received but 12
months “ hard ” each. In N.S.W. they would
probably have been hanged. Surely the penal
sw?ng lUm ° f alleged civili sation has too long a-
One Goldsmith, an Adelaide ArVesleyan
parson, got himself and his tricycle in front of a
traincar the other day, and became mixed up with
the horses. Then he wrote a long letter to the
papers, some extracts from which are as follows :
The angel of the Lord encampeth round about them
that fear Him, and delivereth them.—Psalm xxxv. 6.
I will give a short outline of the occurrence and how it
was I escaped from impending danger, and anyone will
see that but for God’s special interposition I must have
lost my life. . . The Payneham tramcar came behind
me and ran me down. In-a moment I and the tricycle
were resting upon the swingletree behind the horses’
heels. How the horses went past me with their reins
crossed without being frightened ; how the tricycle
passed between them without causing them to kick up •
and how they stood still until removed from the’
tramcar, could only be accounted for by the restraining
power of Him who gathers the wind in'his fist. As God
would have it, I did uot receive a nervous shock, as le-
ported, nor did I lose my presence of miud, but when
the tramcar stopped I simply expressed a wish that the
might be removed at once before they commenced
kicking against the tricycle, and the Great Master kept
them perfectly still. It was by a kind overruling* Provi-
dence that I happened to be exactly in the centre of the
tram.me when overtaken. ... I was preserved alto-
gether from excitement or fear, and was never more com-
posed m my life than when sitting behind the heels of
the horses. ... By the overruling providence of God I
escaped with only a few bruises, and in company wil h two
gentlemen, who very kindly walked with me to the Dorcas
ward, but whose names unfortunately I do not know, I
attended to my hospital duties as usual. As one may
suppose my tricycle was broken to pieces. I canrot find
words; to express my deep sense of obligation and my
thankfulnees to Him of whom it is truly affirmed “ As,
the mountains are round about Jerusalem, so the Lord is
round about His people,” to whom alone he ascribed all
thanksgiving and praise; During the last few days W
right leg, which was wounded in five different places, has
greatly swelled and become so painful that I fear 1 may
have to lay up (sic) for a' time ; but l am conscious that
I am in good hands—One who can accomplish His pur-
pose in any way He pleases'. To His kind care I have
every confidence in committing myself.
What a humorous idea of the Deity the average
parson possesses ! He considers his God as an in-
dividual who allows tramcars to run over him and
then specially interferes to prevent him being
bruised in more than eleven places; and who
drops him out of boats, and then takes special
measures to rescue him when he is scared to death,
and has acquired life-long rheumatism. Provi-
dence, in his view, is an unfinished sort of insti-
tution which seldom interferes quite in time,
but gets there at the last moment when the
believer is badly knocked about and half-dead.
It suggests a sum in proportion : If parson Gold-
smith, because he believed, was only* run over by
two horses and bruised in five places, how many
fewer horses would have run over him, and how
many less places would he have been bruised in, if
he had believed more ? And if, by reason of his
faith, he was only damaged on one leg, would he
have been damaged on four legs if he had had
cnly a quarter of the faith ?
Fitzharris, alias “Skin the Goat,” who
drove the Phoenix Park murderers to the scene of
the crime, and afterwards aided them to escape,
has just calmly applied fora renewal of his license
as a car-driver in Dublin ! T.irn Healy asked in
tlie Commons whether there was any reason why
the authorities should not “assist released prison-
ers.” Humor?
A London print named Answers recently
asked certain celebrities what they did when
tired, and published two columns of their replies
under the headings, “That Tired Feeling : What
Certain Celebrities Play at When Jaded.” Agent-
Ceneral Reeves, of Maoriland, replied :—“When
I am tired I make a point, as far as possible, to
avoid answering any questions. lam very tired
now. ”
He prayer-efficacy. The Tientsin (North
China) missionaries recently had an embarrassing
set-back. Rain was much wanted, and a Sunday
was fixed to solemnly supplicate therefor. No
results. Then the pagan Chows had a great pro-
cession, with gongs and crackling fireworks, and
ere the show was half over down came rain in
torrents.

FEDERAL PARLIAMENT.
HOUSE OF REPRESENTATIVES.
TO THE ELECTORS OF THE PARRAMATTA ELECTORATE.
Gentlemen,— _ , , „ (
lam a Candidate for your suffrages at the forthcoming election for the I ederal House o_ p
sentatives. lam a manufacturer, and I believe the policy enunciated by the Right Hon. Mm
Barton to be the policy most fitted for the young Commonwealth, and Mr. Barton will nav y
strongest support. , . . • „
I am in favor of Australian productions of every sort, and if you elect me as your Represen
shall give my special attention to the development of the varied industries that have started, or
about to start, in Australia. , , •
lam in favor of the Federal Government taking over the Railways of all Australia, and wor g
the same to assist our own productions by minimum freights, carrying such products to market at t
very lowest possible cost. , n
In and around the Parramatta Electorate have been started Coal, Coke, Iron and Steel, Hopp
Machinery of various kinds, Soap, Oil, Leather, Woollens and Tweeds, Fruit, Jam, Pottei% Lime
and Cement, and many other Industries which require developing. I consider that if Mr. -barton s
policy is carried out we can have as varied and successful industries in the Electorate as in any par
of the world, giving constant employment at good wages to tens of thousands of men. .
I am against any direct taxation by the Federal Government, but shall insist that sumcient
revenue be raised every year to pay all debts incurred. *
I approve of railways through Australia, connecting the capitals from Perth to Brisbane. _
I approve of a Federal Conciliation and Arbitration Act. I believe in Wages Boards, consisting
of equal numbers from masters and men, with a Chartered Accountant and a President for each par-
ticular trade to deal with the question in dispute before appealing to the Act.
I believe in adult suffrage. _ .
I am decidedly in favor of the exclusion of undesirable immigrants, irrespective of colour, my
object being to have a healthy and prosperous white Australian race. , .
I consider the Federal Parliament should be carried on as a successful business is earned on ;
divisions of departments and suitable men appointed for such departments.
As a business man of solid practical experience I cannot forget that we have now a population of
some four million souls, and I will leave no stone unturned to secure for Australia the labor and wages
necessary in the manufacture of our own requirements, and I shall not rest satisfied until Australians
are able to produce all their wants in time of Peace or War.
I place myself in your hands, with best wishes for the new Commonwealth, and trusting to
secure your support in my earnest desire to secure Australia for the Australians, I am, yours
faithfully, W. SANDFORD.
Eskbank Iron Works, Lithgow, 29/1/01.

A POLICY FOR THE {Price 6d, Posted Bd.
COMMONWEALTH
Federal Flections.
TO THE
FEDERAL ELECTORS
OF
NEW SOUTH WALES.
Gentlemen, —Encouraged by the very influential requi-
sition presented to me on the 22nd instant, and to which
there are now 6752 signatures attached, I respectfully
announce myself a candidate for one of the seats at your
disposal in the Senate. As it is physically impossible to
canvass the whole colony, I do not intend to attempt it.
To not a few of the electors, however, I imagine my views
on questions affecting the Commonwealth cannot be
altogether unknown, as they are practically the same I
advocated when a candidate for the Federal Convention,
and during the subsequent Referendum campaigns : in
all, I addressed 36 public meetings in the Metropolis and
suburbs, and in different localities from Albury to Ten-
terfield. In 1897 you were pleased to elect me, out of 49
candidates, as one of your 10 representatives to the Con-
vention which held sessions in Adelaide, Sydney and
Melbourne, in the order named. The debates and divi-
sions in the Convention testify, I think, to my having
attended assiduously to my duty, and, should you repeat
your confidence by electing me, it will be my earnest
endeavor to discharge my obligations to your satisfaction.
You will not, I am sure, expect me to take a narrow
view of matters political, but rather to remember that
what is required is to assist in the building of a structure
that is intended to last for all time.
Section 51 of the Commonwealth Act contains a list of
39 subjects concerning which the Federal Parliament has
power to legislate, amongst these being Taxation, Borrow-
ing Money, Post and Telegraph Services, Defence, Quar-
antine, Currency, Banking, Insurance, Bankruptcy,
Marriage, the Acquisition of Railways, Railway Construc-
tion, Old Age Pensions, Conciliation and Arbitration for
Prevention of Industrial Disputes, External Affairs.
During the first session there will be an immense
amount of preliminary work to be done, not a little, I
hope, of a non-disputatious character.
As a Freetrader, I am, of course, loyal to the principle
that more should not be taken out of the pockets of the
people than isnecessary.for legitimate Government require-
ments. It will be the duty of members of both Houses to
iace without delay the financial position, which admittedly
is not without exceptional difficulties. With regard to
these my business experience, I trust, may be found of use.
For some time to come it is obvious the Federal Govern-
ment will have to rely for its revenue on Customs and
Excise duties and on income from the Public Services, it
being necessary for many of the State Governments to
have at their disposal the power of direct taxation to
supplement their share of the surplus Federal revenue, so
as to meet current engagement without having recourse,
as in days gone by, to the very reprehensible system of
parting'irrevocably with the public estate and living on
the proceeds. As the Federal Treasurer must have
revenue to carry on the Government, he may reasonably
be expected to prefer for that purpose what is generally
called a revenue tariff, and which, I may mention, is not
incompatible with Freetrade principles. It will take time
for the leaven of what not a few of us consider economic
truth to fully assert itself, but Freetraders, having con-
fidence in their principles, look upon Intercolonial Free-
trade as an instalment of the principle they advocate, and
believe that when the advantages of an extended market
thereby secured have been enjoyed by local manufac-
turers and others they will begin to ask themselves,
“ Why only Intercolonial Freetrade?”
Great economies, as well as greater efficiency, should
follow the amalgamation of. the Public Services, such as
the Customs Department, Post and Telegraph Depart-
ment, Defence, Quarantine, &c. In this connection I ex-
ceedingly regret that a feasible, equitable, and accept-
able plan has not yet been formed for federating the Rail-
ways. The Commonwealth Act contemplates this being
done in time, subject, of course, to the approval of the
respective States. The late Mr. Eddy assured me
that if all the Railways in Australia were under
one control the saving in working expenses would
be very considerable—probably not far short of £300,000 a
year—a uniform gauge gradually superseding the present
systems, and, under such management, the whole of the
rolling stock required could be manufactured in our work-
shops, and thereby, amongst other advantages, ensure
permanent employment to those at present in the service,
and open out to many of them a sphere of greater advance-
ment in their respective departments. Before many years
elapse I consider it will be essential for purposes of
national defence that there shall be a railway connecting
Western Australia with South Australia, that the Port
Darwin line shall be completed, and that the Queensland
Railway system shall be extended westwards to junction
with the transcontinental line having its northern termi-
nus at Port Darwin. There should be nothing wanting on
our part to make it impossible for a Foreign Power to
filch any portion of our territory. All these ideas will, of
course, take time to accomplish, but as population in-
creases—and it ought to increase by immigration from
Europe more rapidly than for many years past—and as
the Federal Government will be able to borrow on more
favorable terms than many imagine, the annual charge
on the increased indebtedness to an increasing population
will, in my opinion, not be such as need frighten the most
timid. We must show that we have confidence in our-
selves, in our resources, and in our future, if we are to
get others to believe in us and to co-operate with us in
the manner and to the extent we desire.
Shortly after the Session commences, the Federal Par-
liament should give its attention to the important ques-
tion of Currency and legal tender. It will not alone be a
great public convenience to have a Government Legal
Tender Note, convertible into gold on demand, available
for use throughout the Commonwealth, but, if competent
non-political Commissioners are appointed to take charge
of the Note Issue, a substantial and yearly increasing
revenue may reasonably be expected therefrom.
If I have the honor to be returned as one of your Mem-
bers, I propose to try and induce the Government to
adopt a scheme which, according to my judgment, will
materially lessen the danger of Financial crises occurring.
Such a terrible panic as that of 1893 is, one would hope,
not likely to recur in a lifetime if practical effect is given
to the scheme alluded to, or to one on similar lines ; but,
to make it effective, Federal legislation is necessary.
It will be my endeavour, if elected, to support an early
choice of the site for the Federal Capital, and thereafter
to urge the immediate erection of Houses of Parliament,
Government House, and the other Public Buildings.
As the establishment of the Commonwealth will neces-
sarily enlarge the field of Government action, it will
doubtless come to pass that a closer political connection
will exist between the Commonwealth and the British
possessions in the Southern Pacific. At the present time
our fellow-subjects in the New Hebrides, which are under
dual control, find themselves at a serious disadvantage
owing to the different views taken by. the British and
French Governments respecting trade regulations.
I indulge the hope that in time New Zealand, Fiji, and
New Guinea will all become component parts of the
Commonwealth, and, in consequence, that certain ex-
isting localisms will disappear by the inhabitants entering
into the broader life which we are how beginning.
As it is not my intention to canvass the whole colony
I propose, at public meetings, to reply to pertinent ques-
tions kindly sent to me in writing by representative
bodies or electors throughout the colony. .
It will, in the public interest, be a pity if the expense
of Federal electoral contests becomes so heavy that
eligible candidates of moderate means are debarred from
offering themselves. We require the best available men
to represent us, and, needless to say, these are not
always the men with the longest purses. lam sure it is
not your wish that the Senate should degenerate into
what is called a Party House, but rather that its members
should take a broad, independent view of matters
Federal and National, at the same time, when occasion
demands, being prepared to conserve the special interests
of their respective States.
Your faithful Servant,
J. T. WALKER.
Roaemont, Woollahrn,
January 31,1901.

result has always been that rain follows cannon-
ading in wet countries where rain follows almost
everything ; and in dry countries it doesn’t. It is not
only used to disperse hail-storms, but (in some coun-
tries) to prevent eclipses of the sun. (4) Why do you
put Styria in inverted commas? Is it a joke?
