FOUR YEARS ILL HEALTH
Sleepless, Weary and Depressed.
RELIEF DESPAIRED OF.
But a “Friend in Need.’’
The Case of
MRS. SUSAN BASTOCK*
[For the Stai
’ ill's Susan Bastock, who lives at No. 72
! Jay-street, Glebe, Sydney, welcomed the
hewspaper man with much politeness, and
nil the latter announcing that his visit Was
|>aid- with the object of eliciting the facts
Relating to that lady’s critical illness and her
Extraordinary recovery therefrom through
tb; administration of Clements Tonic, he
Was soon seated in the front parlor, where
Mrs. Bastock readily assented to supply him
with every detail connected with her case,
“To look at me now you would hardly
think that I am the mother of a family of
thirteen children, and considering the great
deal of suffering which I have gone through
I ought to be—and indeed I am—very
thankful for my present good state of
health. The great physical strain of rearing
such a large family must have weakened my
system terribly, for it ultimately resulted in
my having a premature confinement, which
culminated in inflammation of the bowels.
It took a long time before I could got about
again, and for four years afterwards I never
possessed good health; and at the expiration
Of that time I was visited With a sickness of
a more serious kind than my previous one.
It is considered an extremely interesting and
critical period of a woman’s existence, and
one which is fraught with important con-
sequences ; for, according as it is passed
with safety or otherwise will her future
health be. o It was at the period when my
change of life took place, and never shall I be
able to describe the pain which I had to en-
dure. Women usually regard the approach of
the change of life with a good deal of anxiety
and fear, for at that time certain diseases
Which may have lain dormant, as it were,
in the system, are likely to make their
appearance, and I was in dread of any extra
derangement, because my constitution was
not strong enough to Withstand it. lam
glad to say that i was not attacked by any
disease during my illness, but I had quite
enough to bear as it was, without any other
disorders taking place.”
“ Were your sufferings very acute, Mrs.
Bastock ? ”
“They were extremely so. The pains
were so bad that I could not boar anyone
to touch me. When anyone approached
very close to me I would give a spasmodic
flinch as though anticipating their touch.
Sleepless nights I passed without number,
and I was always Weary and depressed. I
think there are very few women who have
gOne through the same ordeal and have-ex-
perienced such dreadful headaches as mine
were. My eyesight became affected, and I
lould not see to read ; in fact, I did not have
much inclination for reading. I was too
much worried with my deplorable condition
to take any interest in books or papers,
■frequently, hot flushing of the face would
iake place, and then I would be conscious of
r feeling that can only be described as ner-
vous irritation. It was quite a common
Jiing for mo to faint, and I would lie and
think of all the evils that may occur if I did
not soon get better. My digestive organs
" eve quite upset, for although I lay for
fully a fortnight without touching anything
in the way of food excepting a little toast
and water, and even that light nourishment
.vms too much for my stomach to digest. I
vas never free from pain of some sort, and
1 a state of desperation I would sometimes ,
ake a little brandy or whisky in the hope of
icquiring relief. But- stimulants only aggra-
vated my suffering, and I always felt worse
iter them. <9 l had got awfully thin, and my
ace looked as though there was not a drop
of blood left in me. ’The intense mental
depression experienced during my sickness
was sometimes more than I like to think of
even now* ”
“What did your doctor think of your
case ? ”
“I had three doctors, and I am sure they
all despaired of my ever pulling through it.
But they were not the only ones who thought
so. One day a lady friend, who had just
returned from the funeral of her sister, said
to me : 1 Ah* old girl, you will be the next I
shall have to follow.’ It is not very en-
couraging to be spoken to like that when a
person feels half-dead. My medical advisers
were very attentive, I must saj r ; but their
medicines all failed to have a beneficial effect
upon me. Goodness knows how much I had
• taken, but I still remained in the same sorry
plight. So low did I sink that I believed I
was drawing near the grave. My husband’s
belief in the restorative and curative qualities
of Clements Tonic had so often been drummed
in my ears that at last I consented to see
what it was like. I had lost faith in all
medicines, and when my husband brought
me a bottle of Clements Tonic I succeeded
in livening myself up with hope again, for I
slowly, but surely, began to mend under its
remarkable influence. The bodily pains
were quickly under control, my dreadful
headaches were soon completely cured, and
the newly-found remedy gave me a very
healthy appetite. I continued taking Cle-
ments Tonic regularly, as I was convinced
it was doing me a lot of good. My blood
was once again running through my veins
with increased vitality, and I could always
depend upon having a good night’s sleep.
With the departure of my severe headaches
I could sit down and enjoy a read, my
eyesight being completely repaired. The
feelings of depression departed with the
advent of new life, and as the time passed
on it was soon my pleasant privilege to
resume my customary duties. The doctors
Were greatly astonished at my rapid recovery,
and so were all my friends. They could see
by the way I had brightened up, and the
way I was putting on flesh, that Clements
Tonic was making a new woman of me. I
am nearly as active now as when I was a
young woman, and I am thoroughly healthy
in every respect. My children are always
given a dose of Clements Tonic whenever
they have anything the matter with them,
and I am under such an obligation to that
remedy for my new lease of life that I never
neglect to recommend it to my friends.”
“ We will give publicity to your state-
mpnts with your permission.”
“ I hope you will do so, as others may be
cured in the same way. ”

When the warm weather arrives the impurities that have accumulated in the blood and tissue during the
cooler months are likely to cause more or less languor, headache, skin eruptions, low spirits, insomnia, etc.
The most simple and effective means of freeing the system of these impurities is afforded by a course of
Kutnow’s Improved Effervescent Carlsbad Powder, which as a purifier of the blood and tissue is absolutely
perfect. - ,
It dissolves the noxious acid and alkaline formations that poison the system, and removes them from the
body by the excretory process, and then, by cleansing and strengthening the stomach, liver, and kidneys,
prevents the further accumulation of these impurities.

Science in the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“What d’ye want?” snapped the selector’s wife,
sharply opening the door after a precautionary
glance through the paper-patched window. The
snarling sheep-dog, more or less satisfied that
everything was right, sneaked back to his chosen
spot, and the dusty, dilapidated man at the door,
giving a quick glance at the dirty, straggle-haired
woman, intuitively discerned a hopeless combina-
tion of ignorance and loquacity. His first impulse
was to turn away in despair, but, bethinking him-
self of the results of perseverance, his pulse
quickened with renewed courage.
“I, madam, am the world-renowned phrenolo-
gist, Professor Cranium, late of London, Constan-
tinople, and Surry Hills,” he said, loftily, by way
of introduction.
“ Th’ what ? ” gasped the woman, withdrawing
a little, and nervously catching up the corner of
her greasy apron.
“I examine the developments of your children’s
heads, madam,” he explained, calmly, “and tell
you what each one is adapted for.”
“Oh, I see,” said she, advancing confidently,
and resuming her leaning posture against the
door; “you’re the photograph-taker what’s goin’
to take Miss Muggins’s school.”
“Oh, dear, no !” snapped the professor, rather
peevishly. “I see you do not thoroughly under-
stand me.” And, opening an ancient handbag,
he carefully unfolded a battered roll of paper on
which was scrawled what he termed a “phreno-
logical diagram.” “You see,” he proceeded to
explain, pointing a long, grimy finger at the
paper, “this diagram shows ”
“What’s it for, mister?” interposed a bare-
footed girl of ten, who had slowly edged her way
past her open-mouthed parent on the verandah.
The next instant she was tearfully endeavoring to
wipe away the marks of her mother’s flour-
covered hand from her unwashed face, and with a
“ Take that, y’ impident hussy ! ” she was bundled
unceremoniously into the kitchen. Inside, a num-
ber of unruly sisters and brothers were engaged in
pitched-battle over a coign of vantage in the shape
of a crack in the wall, which afforded a good view
of the long-haired monstrosity at the front-door—
who glared fiercely at the slumbering sheep-dog
during the interruption.
“You see,” he went on, patiently, when the
harassed mother had returned, after restoring
order, “this diagram shows the different organs
of thought that are centred in the brain ; and,
according to the manner in which these various
organs are developed, I form conclusions as to
what particular calling a child would be naturally
fitted for.”
“ Oh, y’ needn’t come here lookin’ for lunatics,”
said the Woman, sharply—“if that’s what y’er
after. My children ’ave all got their proper
senses, thank Gawd.”
The disgusted scientist turned his aching head,
and gazed hopelessly across the dreary sun-scorched
plain for one full, depressing minute. Then he
drew a long, deep breath, and returned to the
charge with such vigor determination that,
after about 10 minutes’ hard explanatory talk, the
benighted woman was enlightened to such an ex-
tent that she declared phrenology to be far-and-
away the grandest thing she had ever heard of.
. “My husband’s away at Lame Horse, she said,
thoughtfully ; but I don’t think he believes much
in these things; he’s so funny, you know. How-
somever, I should like to ’ave ’em done just t see
if they’re goin’t’ turn out anythin good, x can
never tell, can y’ ? ” ,
“That’s true, madam. You never can tell
until you have had their heads examined by a
capable phrenologist —which I can fairly claim
t .”
«I should juat like you to ’ave a look at my
’Enery,” broke in the woman, with sudden en-
thusiasm. “E’s my eldest son, and ’e just is
clever. Do you see that beautiful drorin on the
wall, above the mantel-shelf. Well, my ’Enery
did that when he was only nine, an’ all out of is
own’ead, too ! ” . . , ~
“Your son must be a genius,” remarked the
diplomatic-old battler, trying to look interested
in the “ drorin”’ —an atrocious attempt at orna-
mental capitals, reading “ What is Home without
a Mother.”
“A genus!” exclaimed the proud parent.
“ You’ve jus’ said it, mister, an’ you’re not the
only one as thinks so. Miss Muggins ses he’s a
genus, and ses we oughter make a hartist of ’im,
but I don’t think them hartists are respectable,
do you ? They never seem to prosper, an’ on’y
yestiddy I was lookin’ in a paper an’ sawr a
pickcher of one. He seemed dreadful poor,, an
looked as if he had somethin’ awful on ’is mind.
What d’ye charge, sir?” she asked anxiously,
after a pause.
“Five shillings,” cautiously replied the im-
pecunious one, nervously clutching the handle of
his handbag.
“ Five shillins ! ” the woman cried in surprise.
“ That is cheap. I thought it would be about a
couple o’ quid.”
“Five shillings is my fee,” the Professor re-
peated, feeling that the battle was won— ‘ ‘ for
which I give a full, detailed ”
“Moth-er-r, th’ bread’s burnin’ !” shrieked five
or six voices in chorus, and the woman, with a
horrified scream, rushed frantically towards the
kitchen. “My Gord,” she cried—“ my bea-u-tiful
batch of bread ! ”
Reaching the fireplace, she made a desperate
dash at the camp-oven, and sweeping ashes and
lid to the ground together at one fell swoop
emptied the half-baked loaves excitedly on to the
earthen floor. Turning the loaves over in a dazed
manner she sniffed the air critically and then gave
a puzzled look towards the fireplace. The next
moment a son of six sunburnt summers
made a troubled exit through the kitchen-
window, closely followed by a broom, hurled
after him by his angry mother. “Take that, y’
brazen-faced, mischeevous young brat ! . If I
catch y’ throwin’ dough into the fire again I’ll
break every bone in yer body—that I will ! ”
Then she gathered the loaves into the oven again,
hung it a few links higher above the fire, and,
after asking M’riar, the ten-year-old daughter,
whatever in the world she thought she was rear-
ing her for, and commanding her under penalty
of having her “neck screwed ” to put some more
ashes on the lid of the oven and keep the young-
sters quiet, she sailed for the front door.
“I s’pose you’re nearly tired waitin’, mister?”
she said apologetically, stroking back her nomadic
hair with both hands, and cooling-down after the
excitement.
“ Oh, no,” wearily replied the persevering dead-
beat, who had reluctantly yet hopefully struggled
through the uproarious interval. “But if you
would like to have your son Henry’s head ex .”
“Yes,” she interrupted, “I would certainly
like to ’ave ’Enery done'; but ,I won’t bother
about any of these good-for-nothings. And even
if they were worth it, times is so bad now with
drought an’ one thing an’ another that really,
mister, we ’ave to look at every shillin’ afore we
spend it.” -
“ But you will have Henry done,” the Professor
persisted anxiously.
“Yes,” the woman said, reflectively, “I would
like 4;o ’ave ’Enery done; an’ if lie was at home I
wouldn’t mind lettin’ y’ do ’im now, but he went,
to Gonbung Station yestiddy to stop a few weeks
with ’is cousins. However ” (here the expectant
Professor said something inarticulate) “if you’re
connin’ round this way any time next year, I
wouldn’t mind givin’ y’ a few shill —”
Here she was cut-short by a hideous uproar
that just then arose from the kitchen, and the
next minute the family milch-goat, unwillingly
ridden through the house by the delinquent son,
dashed furiously past, threw its rider violently
against the verandah-post, and pursuing its mad-
dened flight, knocked the professor on to the
broad of his learned back.
“Pm so sorry, mister,” said the house-mother, .
breathlessly; “ I hope you’re not hurt, but if y’..
come round this way again don’t forget t’ call
an’ —— ” .
But the ill-starred Professor, ignommiously
picking himself up, and gathering-in his wounded
feelings, had walked far, far away into the
deepening gloom, expectorating black soil and
blasphemy!
BARKY SPRAYTON.
In a certain recent case in which members
of N.S.W. police force lent themselves as spies in
a civil proceeding, an inquiry is now pending.
Inspector-General Fosbery has determined, to put
down an objectionable practice, which, in this
instance at least, was indulged in without his
cognisance.
Gadshill writes :
Bulletin mention of the feeds that usurer Sam
Lewis provided for his victims has set me thinking
of one of Dickens’ strongest pen pictures, viz., the “ dis-
counting dinner ” given by old Ralph Nickleby to Sir
Mulberry Hawk and his pupil Lord Frederick Verisopht,
whereat the usurer’s niece, Kate, figured as an innocent
decoy for the lordling. Messrs. Pike and Pluck, satellites
of Sir Mulberry, came to assist in managing his pupil, and
Colonel Ohowser (of the marines) was the makeweight
guest. It being the particular duty of Pike and Pluck to
laugh uproariously at Hawk’s jokes, their merriment
encouraged Chowser also' to try his wit on the company.
Chowser’s presumption met its deserts. Pike and Pluck
scowled ominously, Hawk cursed the interloper s impu-
dence, and Lord Verisopht, dimly conscious of the outrage,
turned his eyeglass upon poor Chowser and said,
“The Dayvil!” Dinner over, Kate Nickleby fled to
a sitting-room upstairs, somewhat scared by the
manners, voices, and easy familiarities of uncles
strange friends. An hour or two later Hawk slipped
away from the table, found Kate alone, and had begun to
force his drunken endearments upon her, when Ralph
Nickleby came to the rescue. Or, rather, he came per-
haps to see how the girl was shaping in her new part.
But as she stood before him with crimson cheeks, tears in
her flashing eyes, her hair slightly disordered, there came
to Ralph’s mind the memory of some schoolboy scene in
which his dead brother had looked at him as Kate now
looked. And for the sake of "his brother’s child the old
schemer so far risked the loss of business as to turn upon
his valuable ally, Hawk, and bid the profligate begone.
One hears young men of the trans-continental Australian
type expressing scornful amusement at the thought of
anyone having an admiration for Dickens’ Work in these
enlightened days of the smart short story. For my part,
I hope I may die a true believer in the genius of the man
who wrote “Nicholas Nickleby.”
The Drover (Pedigree unknown).
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
How is.he bred? Well, I don’t.know. I sup-
pose it appeared in the stud-book when the horse
was foaled ; but he’s had so many pedigrees and
different names before I owned him that I expect
his real identity has got badly mixed-up. But
you may be sure he’s as clean as they, breed ’em.
Can sprint like a two-yeai -old, and stay for ever.
When I bought him I didn’t ask for a pedigree.
I saw the neddy win; three races in one day
at the All-In Race Meeting, and he appeared
on the card as The Drover (pedigree unknown)
and a maiden: But the way he spread-eagled
his fields made some of us think there might
have been some slight omission re the perform-
ances that accompanied the nomination, as the
horse was described as having run unplaced in
numerous races.
Well, either the calibre of the horses The
Drover had formerly met was exceptionally high,
or the fields at the All-In Meeting consisted of a
lot of brumbies. Anyhow at the settling that
night I bought the, horse, and gave a good stiff
price for him.
When I had paid over the cash and got my
receipt, the fellow I bought him from said,
“Look here, you own as honest an old neddy
as ever stood on iron.” Then, after a lengthy
pause, he added, “But where do you reckon to
race him ? ” I mentioned a couple of places, but
The Drover’s late owner shook his head, and
quietly remarked, “Don’t take him there. We
got away with their double at both places last
year.” “Why, I thought the horse was a maiden
previous to winning here?” I said. “Well,” he
replied, “as far as I know, The Drover was a
maiden, as I don’t remember a horse of that
name winning a race at any of the meetings where
we have been.” I then mentioned a few other
places where I might race the neddy. “No, I
wouldn’t show him at any of them,” he said.
“ You mi(/lit have a bit of luck, and get through
all right, but I wouldn’t advise you to take it on.
And, look here, young fellow,” he continued, “ 1
might as well tell you a few of the places where,
if I owned The Drover, I wouldn’t try to race
him.” He then named nearly all the chief race-
meetings of Queensland, N.S.W., and Victoria,
but added, as a bit of consolation, “They tell me
that when this war in South Africa is over, and
the country gets settled down again, there will be
a few decent sort of races to be picked up with a
horse of the class you’ve just bought. And as I
give you my word that S’Africa is one of the few
places that me and the old battler haven't worked,
it might pay you to remember it.
ON THE RAILS.
“Joe of Birmingham,” writes The Bul-
letin’s London correspondent, “is down to
‘ chair’ the ‘ Australian Commonwealth Banquet ’
on Jan. 21. Now that Fed-Austral has a lot of
large salaried billets in prospect a muster of ‘ big
cattle ’ may be expected. Anyhow it’s worth two
guineas at any time to see ‘ slim ’ Joe trying to
appear delighted with everything—and Chamber-
lain don’t like us Australs, for we know what we
want, and lack the diplomatic varnish to lessen
the brutality of our directness in asking for it.
Besides, as "self-made people, we remind the
Brummagem J.C. too much whence he sprung,
and that alone is a crime to any such self-seeker.”

The Kidneys are the most important organs of
the body beeause they filter the blood .
The statement given below was made by a resi-
« dent of Leichhardt, this city. If the same state-
ment had been made by some person in another
town or country it might, of course, be true, but
we would never know, because we couldn’t prove
it. But we can prove this statement, for the lady
who gives it lives right here in Sydney.
Mrs. Pat. Duffy, 43 Marion-street, Leichhardt,
this city, says:—“Twelve months ago I took
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills for pain in the
back, which I knew to arise from the kidneys.
The doctor said that I could expect it owing to
the change of life. I had been taking various
remedies (both internal and external) without
getting any benefit. Only a few doses of Doan’s
Backache Kidney Pills released me from all my
pain, however.
“ My husband had been suffering from kidney
troubles, and, seeing how splendidly they acted in
my case, he took the same remedy. He works
for the council, at asphalting, and is out in all
kinds of weather. The pains in his back were
extremely bad. He had been unable to get more
than a passing relief out of anything he had pre-
viously tried, but Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills
cured him. Since being cured, he was out work-
ing in the rain, and he caught a cold in his kid-
neys, and so lie took more of the pills. They are
first-rate kidney pills, and he says that nothing
ever did him as much good as Doan’s Pills. I
procured these pills at E. Newman’s pharmacy, in
King-street.”
This is only one instance of what Doan’s Back-
ache Kidney Pills are doing in Sydney. There
are hundreds of similar cases equally as strong
and equally as convincing, and they are all Sydney
people. For instance: Mr. W. Hibberd, St.
Paul-street, Randwick, Sydney ; Mr. W. Lane,
102 George-street, Erskineville, Sydney; Mr. J.
Cameron, 297 Balmain-road, Leichhardt, Sydney;
Mr. Jas. Rees, 6 East-street, Surry-hills, Sydney ;
Mrs. Spear, 32 Goodsell-street, St. Peters, Syd-
ney ; and scores of others.
As we hear of no other medicines curing Sydney
people like this, why are Doan’s Backache Kidney
Pills so successful here ? We will tell you why.
Doctors used to think that only urinary troubles
were to be traced to the kidneys.
Modern science has proved, however, that really
all constitutional diseases have their beginnings in
disorders of these important organs.
And this is common sense, for the kidneys filter
and purify the blood. When they do not the
whole body suffers.
When the kidneys are ill, impurities permeate
the blood, muscles, bones and nerves, like foul
vapours from a marsh. _ They saturate every pore.
There is a dull pain in the back, rheumatism,
dizziness, headaches, nervousness, heart trouble,
pains in the limbs, bloating, sallow complexion,
circles under the eyes, bladder irritation, suppres-
sion of the urine, worn out, tired feeling, lack of
ambition.
Our readers will be interested to see the picture
of James Doan, the discoverer of this famous
medicine, which we publish at the head of this
article. He is the noted chemist of Kingsville,
Ontario, and his discovery, spoken of above, has
made his name known all over the world.
Remember it was Doan’s Backache Kidney
Pills which Mrs. Duffy used. See that you get
the same. You may always know the genuine,
because the wrapper bears the full name, Doan’s
Backache Kidney Pills, in bold type, and has the
signature of James Doan. You need never be
imposed upon if you will bear these two things in
mind.

My Jane—who’s got religion—sometimes rants
As ’ow it pleased the Lord to make ’im plain,
To save im from the sin o’ being vain—
Maybe the Lord forgot Jane makes ’is pants.
But, all the same, I views ’im with content,
A Itho’ with Jane I sometimes disagree,
W en she gets narked, and sez ’e favors me
Becos ’is legs is just a wee bit bent.
’E is our only kid wot got a place,
The rest all chucked the race up at the start.
’E umps ’is weight along to cheer me ’eart,
An’ I just love ’is ugly Chivy Chase.

CRIPPS CLARK.
The Unit.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When Wilson arrived at Quandong, to take
charge of the school that a maternally-minded
Ministry had seen fit (at the instigation of
a man with a vote) to impose upon the children of
the district, he was received with open arms by
the few families who posed as “society.” And
indeed, Wilson was a decided acquisition in more
ways than one. In the first place, he added to
the small circle an element of learning that had
• hitherto been missing, and he supplemented it,
when opportunity arose, by the display of a very
pretty sentiment. At times, too, he pottered
about the border-line of the melodramatic—an
emotional frontier intersected by many pitfalls.
M ith Mrs. Tom Cameron, above all others, the
ardent school-teacher loved to hold (as he put it to
himself) sweet communion, 55 and very early in
the course of their intimacy the little woman
received him with graciousness. She was a
brunette, of the type known by some people as
spirituelle, was possessed of dreamy, languorous
eyes, and—a perfect ankle. Little wonder, indeed,
that so sweet a morsel of humanity should appeal
to the poetic side of Wilson’s nature.
Mrs. Cameron’s husband was the local miller •
a big man of the all-red typo His face was red
1 is hair and beard were red, his hands were red •
ns system throbbed with lusty life and vigor and
he turned the scale at sixteen stone. How did it
happen ? How was it possible that such extremes
as „ A OOl Cameron and his wife developed the
afrimty culminating in married life?
Wilson, the school-teacher, pondered long on the
matrimonial paradox without finding answers to
his mental queries. And, meanwhile, his heart
caught fire. Nevertheless he was wary. Long
years at Fort-street had superadded to his senti-
mental side a strong touch of the practical, and
his study of the absolutes, while showing that
‘ Nature ab ‘ lors a vacuum,” also taught him that
a full vessel cannot be filled. But’ surely it was
impossible that Mrs. Cameron’s heart could be
ti ded with love for the burly miller ; the idea was
absurd. Thus reasoned Wilson.
* * * *
Ihe bum and b-r-r-r-r of the mill machinery
droned over the district like an agricultural bene-
diction. Here were garnered and coined into
go.den sovereigns the results of the season’s labor
o the encircling paddocks, and over all presided
1 >m Cameron, the Mint-master. The man with
the red face and hands was Quandong’s practical
patriarch.
And “H’m,” said the patriarch, one bright
afternoon, as he stood at the mill entrance—“h’m
ah ; anyhow, it’ll keep the little woman from feel-
ing too lonely.”
The school-teacher had just entered Cameron’s
gate, a short distance up the road.
* * * *
Yes, you must find it dull sometimes, Mrs.
Cameron, said Wilson, as he stirred his tea.
“Ah ! you’ve no idea how monotonous.” ’ Mrs.
Cameron wa3 half-reclining on an easy couch
looking very bewitching in her chic muslin tea-
gown.

“ Are your wishes not to be consulted in the

“Don’t you see how impossible it is? Mr
Cameron’s interests are all centred here.”

“And you .”
“I am only—the woman ! ”
“And because you are onlv ‘the woman’ is
your happiness to be sacrificed ? ”
“I* is P ar t of the matrimonial code that man is
master, and women must, I suppose, accept it.”
A stupid platitude to shackle fools ! ” retorted
Wilson. It is always possible to compel cir-
cumstance. ”
, “ always, I fear,” sighed the little woman •
‘circumstance more frequently compels us.”
. ‘‘That is as you will,” suggested Wilson,
boiutions can generally be found.”
The picnic had been a success. Lunch was
over, and most of the party had strolled away.
Mrs. Cameron, as hostess, had stayed behind to
“tidy up a bit,” and now, her work done, was
resting. As she sat under the shade of a great
she-oak by the river-bank, idly fingering the
strings of a wonderful hat and looking
dreamily at the silent, flowing water, Wilson,
the school-teacher, thought her the incarnation
of all that was desirable in woman. He
had thrown himself on the grass a few paces off,
and his eyes were fixed inquiringly on her face.
A hundred yards up the stream, on a jutting log,
stood the miller, his red beard, like a bunch of
shredded carrots, sweeping his white jacket.
“You haven’t answered me,” Wilson was
saying in a hungry half-whisper—“ You must
have known.”
Mrs. Cameron pulled a red poppy from her hat
and scattered the leaves on the grass. “ Oh, yes;
I suppose I should have known—a woman’s intui-
lon is credited with wonderful powers of divina-

quietly. “ And you offer me ? ”

“Some time ago I had a very severe attack of influenza which left me
greatly weakened. I lost flesh rapidly and was in a very bad way.
“I took quantities of medicine, but constantly grew worse all the time.
Finally, I tried Ayer’s Sarsaparilla and began to improve from the start.
“I took about six bottles and was perfectly cured. I have used this remedy
in my family a great deal and I know it to be a thoroughly reliable health-
giving compound and family medicine.”
John Murrell, Railway Station Master, of Sunnybank, Queensland, sends us
this letter .together with his photograph, which we reproduce above.
This is a strong letter, one which must remove all doubt. It is additional
proof that Ayer’s Sarsaparilla is
“The World’s Greatest Family Wledicine.”
You ought to profit by it greatly, for if you are weak, have lost flesh, are with-
out appetite, and feel languid and depressed, here is a quick and certain cure.
Perhaps the trouble is with your blood and you are suffering from headache,
boils, eruptions of the skin, scrofula, and rheumatism; or if you are suffering
from weakness of any kind, here is a prompt and most perfect cure.
